The RED PAGE
NIGHT AND THE TIDE.
Through the mighty harbor gates the salt-sea tide
sweeps in,
Up the sweet blue reaches, laving beach and
pier and pile,
Gathering the City filth as foul as City sin.
Purging, cleansing all the deep-blue basin man
makes vile.
So the broad and brimming water on its mission
rolls.
Bringing sea things with it, ivorv-yellow froth
and foam,
Sea-weed on its surface floating, feeding fish in
shoals,
And a wave-worn ship that comes through many
tempests home.-
When again the tide goes out it bears among its
freight
One still passenger, whom dawn or wind shall
never wake;
Livid, facing to the moon—that shines in silver
state.
Like a lovely lotus lonely dreaming on a lake.
N.S.W.
P. E. QUINN.
The Rude Forefathers
Of the gospels which form the beginning
of the testament relating to the British
settlement of Australia, the most valuable in
many respects is that of David Collips, the
first Judge Advocate and Secretary of
N,S.W. Not so light as Tench’s, nor so
heavy as Hunter’s, it has the solid qualities
of Phillip’s record with a better literary
style, and of all the journals of the First
Fleet it is one which the historian could
least afford to lose. G. B. Barton, in
that splendid beginning of a History of
N.S. W. from, the Records, quotes more fre-
quently from Collins than anyone else.
Collins was a kindly, unassertive
man, a good factotum, who filled as
many offices in the early days of the
settlement as Pooh-Bali. Some things
of importance did not attract him, but
he saw nearly everything that was done
in the management of the infant com-
munity, the building of Sydney and the
foundation of its industries, and he jotted it
all down in his daily journal. The first
volume of his Account of the English Colony
in N.S.W., virtually a reprint of the diary,
was published in 1798. The second volume,
based on material supplied by Hunter, ap-
peared in 1802. They are now scarce, and
bring £5 or more in the market. Though
indispensable to the historian, they are too
detailed for the general reader, and Collins
was wise enough to provide for an abridg-
ment. This was made in excellent style by
his wife. The poor woman shuddered at
the sound of the lash which swished so often
in her husband’s pages, but she went
through with her job gallantly. The second
edition retains all that is of general interest
in the first, and is a much more readable
book.
Whitcombe and Tombs, Ltd., have done
well in reprinting this abridged Collins, and
at the price of 7s. 6d. it will now be possible
for many to read a story which is full of
interest to every Australian. No later his-
torian of the period can have the same
charm as the narrator who was on the spot
when the first white men settled down to
conquer the Australian bush. An isolated
community of little more than a thousand,
mostly convicts and mostly males, sur-
rounded by unknown dangers, and often face
to face with starvation, could not fail to
furnish incidents of an extraordinary charac-
ter. Drink was almost the only diversion.
Rum became more precious than the waters
of Pactolus, and was the principal “ meejum
of exchange.” Collins tells a story about
a man named Williams, who lost his wife
and child in the harbor, and buried the
bodies within a few feet of his own door.
The profligacy of this man manifested itself in
a strange manner. A short time after he had thus
buried his wife Ire was seen sitting at his door
with a bottle of rum in his hand, and actually
drinking one glass and pouring another on her
grave until it was emptied, prefacing every liba-
tion by declaring how well she had loved it during
her life. He appeared to be in a state of insanity,
as this anecdote certainly testifies.
The convict in primitive conditions reverted
to an ancient practice ; but Collins was im-
pressed only by the waste of good rum.
Phillip was a man of great energy and
prudence, and, as a gaoler, far more humane
than most of his time. His treatment of
the convicts was gentle compared with thfe
System of later days. These, for instance,
were mild punishments:
One of the criminals was condemned to suffer
death ; another to be burned in the hand and im-
prisoned 12 months ; two were banished to Nor-
folk Island ; and three to stand in the pillory, to
which their ears were to be nailed. These last,
affording something like amusement to the mob,
were sufficiently covered with dirt and rotten
eggs. ... It may be some relief to turn from
the contemplation of such iniquity, though it
should be only to the transactions of savages dif-
fering from these wretches but in complexion.
The blacks interested Collins a good deal,
and his account of their manners and
customs is still of great value. His book is
also the sole authority for the famous
voyage' of Bass and Flinders.
The reprint of The English Colony in
N.S. W. (copy of which we have received
from Angus and Robertson) has a useful
preface and some notes by James Collier. It
would have been more valuable if it had been
provided with an index.
A Soldier of Fortune
S. P. Hyatt, whose latest novel, Black
Sheep , was greatly helped by the British
Library censors, tells the story of his life in
The Diary of a Soldier of Fortune (Sydney
Book Club). According to the title-page he
has had experience as “engineer, sheep-
station hand, nigger-driver, hunter, trader,
transport-rider, labor agent, explorer, lec-
turer, pressman, American soldier, blockade-
runner- and tramp.” The occupations are
sufficiently varied, no doubt; but, in point
of number, are not to be compared with the
lists of some of our Abo. contributors. Hyatt
has worked some of these experiences into
his previous books, where they appear to
better advantage than in this over-long and
formal autobiography. Nevertheless, The
Diary is a human document of value. Hyatt
has seen the seamy side of many places and
has put the acid on all sorts of men. <He
has fought with his brains for a living and
fought with a gun for fun; he has been
cheated many times, been euchred by For-
tune, faced by Death, and survived over 80
attacks of malarial fever. It is not surpris-
ing that he comes out of it with a rather
jaundiced view of men and things.
Hyatt arrived in Sydney in a sailing ship
(he does not say when) and was duly im-
pressed by the Beautiful Harbor. “I have
seen many harbors since then, but I think
the only ones which excel Sydney are Dar-
es-Salaam, “the Place of Peace,” in East
Africa, and Nagasaki.” He was in Sydney for
a fortnight, and remembers chiefly the larri-
kins and the fan-tan shops ! A big sheep
station, 400 miles up-country, was a fine
place, splendidly kept up, etc. — “ yet some-
how I cannot say that station life made any
great impression on me; certainly it never
appealed to me. Even so far up-country as
that there was little suggestion of the back-
blocks, and no hint at all of the Australia of
the novelists. It was all quite prosaic and
quite proper.” Australia is used to criticism,
and this hunk from a very brief experience
is another instance of the way in which the
country suffers from the results of our
visitors’ defective education :
Australia to-day seems entirely devoid of the
element of Romance. Possibly the same can be
said of any prosperous country, for excitement
means insecurity and dislocation of trade. On
the other hand, it does seem rather remarkable
that practically all the successful Australian
stories of adventure should have dealt with vio-
lent crime of the bushranger type. Nowadays
those who would have been bushrangers a few
decades ago, who are still bushrangers in spirit,
find a safer and more remunerative field for their
energies in politics.
Hyatt, apparently, knew no more about
Australia when he arrived than what he had
gathered from Ned Kelly, the Iron-dad
Bushranger or similar fffirillers, and his notion
of romance is that of the butcher boy or the
servant girl.
Anyhow, dismayed by the absence of pro-
miscuous violence and blood-letting, also by
the tedium of never-ending “race-horse
talk” on the station, he quitted Australia
and tried S. Africa. His experiences there
were not much happier; but he was
afterwards joined by a brother, Amyas,
who helped him to stop the rot. Every-
thing resolved itself into unmitigated
animalism there. “ Every Afrikander who
was my guest in those trading days
did me a bad turn, or tried to do me
one,” and the greed of speculators was re-
sponsible for four-fifths of the numerous
deaths at the mines. Drink brought on
malaria, and malaria made men drink, and
men died like flies. He had no love for
Cecil Rhodes or the Boer ; even the alleged
“king of beasts” he thought a mean and
slinking pest; and after seven years’ hard
work, sickness and bad luck, making money
and losing it, he left Rhodesia—dead broke.
It was not all bad : he met a few good
men like Dr. Koch ; but “ the curse of the
African tradition ” had settled on the land
and sapped the strength of the people.
I loved the road—Heaven knows I love it still,
for its own sake and also for the sake of Amy as,
who was my partner on the road, who understood
it as L did, heard its call as clearly as I did—but
the road, our road, is gone. That abominable
railway has replaced it. or rather displaced it,
and the Romance of Rhodesia is dead. Men
make money there now by orthodox occupations,
die decently in hospitals of delirium tremens, or
phthisis, or typhoid, and have the burial service
read over them by a Clerk in Holy Orders, which
must be comforting to their relatives. They have
coffins where we used blankets ; they have cash
where we signed “good-fors”; they drink a
bottle of locally-brewed lager—ghastly stuff—
where we got through a case of whisky. They
have succeeded where we failed, and I am not
sure that their success is not due to the fact that
we did fail after having done the rough work for
them.
His experiences elsewhere were not more
fortunate. The best view of Mauritius, he
says, is obtained from a ship—when the ship
is going away. The Filipinos are greasy
little) savages, and the civil administration
of the Philippines abominable. He admired
the American soldiers with whom he fought,
but “ whenever the civil department touch
the service they leave a dirty mark.” The
canteen was abolished, with the result that
“ crime in the service was more than
doubled, whilst the death rate went up in
almost the same degree.”
The S. African contingent of fiction-
writers is swelling visibly, and a number of
them have struck oil in Britain, though no-
thing of first-rate quality has appeared since
Olive Schreiner’s Story of an A frican Farm.
Cynthia Stockley’s Poppy began in the same
way : but proceeded in the manner of Marie
Corelli. The Shidamite and The Rod of
Justice (Dymock's), by A. and C. Askew,
are dramatic tales of average merit. Ger-
trude Page has extracted a good deaf of
simple humor from Rhodesian life in Love and
the Wilderness and three or four other stories.
Her work, however, is poor in style. The
latest and longest story of the veldt and the
rooinek is The Dop Doctor by Richard
Dehan (Dymock’s). Like Poppy and The
Story of an African Farm, it begins with a
cruelly ill-used white girl ; but the principal
character is the doctor. Driven out of
London by a miscarriage of justice, he takes
to drink to drown his sorrows, and is re-
deemed in the good old way. The story
is one that holds the reader’s interest
at the beginning, and allows it to be dissi-
pated by a multiplicity of characters and
undue length. The coincidences are too
numerous ; but there is much crude power
in the yarn, which out-weighs its faults.
_—
L. J. Vance has made a light and amusing
story of The Fortune Hunter, based on a
comedy of the same name by Winchell
Smith. It is of the same class as Brewster’s
Millions, though the plot runs in the opposite
direction. The humor of the American city
man has a fair show, and the mixture of
business and sentiment is very judicious.
Frederic Manning
Manning, a Sydney native, took a high
place as a prose writer when he published
Scenes and Portraits last year. An earlier
volume of blank verse, The Vigil of Brun-
hild, showed cleverness in the control ,of a
difficult measure ; but was not remarkable.
A new volume of Poems (Murray) contains
better blank verse and a number*of lyrics
which, though they do not say anything new,
say it exceedingly well. Here is a passage
from a rhymed version of the chase of Hip-
polyta by Theseus which has all the ardency
of Swinburne :
Theseus followed his prey
As a lean hound follows the fleet
Quarry: the dusty way
Smoked with the speed of, his feet.
She was swift; but he burned in the chace;
He was flame, he was sandalled with fire,
Hungering after her face,
With a fury, a lust, a desire,
As a hound that whines for the blood
Of the hart flying winged with fear.
And she yearned, and she longed for the wood,
Seeming far from her still, though near,
And she strained and she panted and pressed,
With her head flung backward for breath,
And the quick sobs shaking her breast,
Agonised, now, as by death,
Fearing utterly and fighting with fate,
Stumbling. And swifter behind,
With a love made hot by his hate,
Strained he, pursuing. ...
A long poem on “Helgi of Lithend ” has
more than one resemblance to Tennyson.**
So, all day long, the uncertain combat flowed,
Between the grey bents and the broken ground
is very close to these lines from “The Passing
of Arthur ” :
So, all day long, the noise of battle roll’d
Among the mountains by the winter sea.
While recalling earlier writers in their tone
and cadence, the following lines front the
same poem give a better measure of his
accomplishment:
He never knew
The keen, sweet joy that smites through every
sense
Into the shuddering soul, and whelms the world
In an immortal glory, while God builds
Life beyond us, creating out of clay
The world’s imperishable dream, the hope,
The wonder, the desire, that gives us sight
Beyond our mortal doom. I have little wit;
I only know that in the looms of time
God’s will moves like a shuttle to and fro.
I have heard him in the waves and on the wind ;
I have seen his splendor shine among the swords,
Soften the eyes of women, light and smile
On a child’s lips; and know his presence there
Where all the waves stream eagerly to lick
The sunset’s bloody splendors.
“An April Dance-Song” is skilfully
fashioned. One .stanza will suffice :
April, with her fleet, sweet,
Silver rain, and sun-rays,
Cometh, and her feet beat
Lightly, on the lawn.
Softly, for her sake, break
Flowering the wet boughs;
By the brimming lake, wake
Lilies every dawn.
The short lyrics give ample proof of Man-
ning’s metrical dexterity, and some of them
are charmingly sweet and simple. “ Reve-
nants des Enfants ” will do as well as any
for illustration :
Hoftlyv on little feet that make no sound,
With laughter that one does not hear, they tread
Upon the primroses that star the ground.
Latticed by shade from branches overhead,
Swaying'in moonlight ; but their footsteps make
A twinkling like the raindrops on the lake.
The shy things that love silence and the night
Are fearless at their coming ; as they pass.
Neither the nightingale nor owl take flight,
So gentle is each footfall on the grass ;
They are a part of silence, and a part
‘Of sweetness sprung from tears hid in the heart.
There is enough in this book to show that
Manning has a poetical nature with a power
of expression which would carry him far if
he could forget his reading.
War—and Poetry
It is perfectly true that blood is the only
permanent ink, and that a mixture of our
own and the enemy’s is essential for the
writing of patriotic poetry. But it is not
true that our poets are justified in writing in
rose-water, pending the Japanese invasion.
There is always plenty of blood handy for
the true poet; he has only to jab his nib
deep enough into himself. It hurts, of
course; but that is a detail.
It is an heroic pose—this indignant protest
that we have the divine Spark in lis, but that
it refuses to flare up in a poisonous environ-
ment of great morning dailies, Royal Art
Society pictures, clerical controversies, and
average aldermen. Very heroic—-to each
little inch of humble admirers : very stupid
to the educated Australian who knows that
Australians have—at stray intervals —written
in their own blood, and wonders why they
do not dip their nibs more freely.
Lacking battlefields, surely there still re-
mains something to goon with. The primal
emotions of humanity, earth and sea and
sky are all ours, yet Conventional Criticism
persists that the roots of our imagination
starve fo£ lack of a little bone-and-blood
fertilizer. The cannibalistic instincts of
local genius have not yet been glutted. Do
we venture to suggest that E. A. Poe
flamed to immortality in an atmosphere as
raw and depressing as ours, we are crushed
with the statement that he was an obvious
by-product of the Boston Tea Party.
That his imagination had lapped blood is
nowhere more evident —we are told—than
in his loveliest of love-lyrics, “Annabel
Lee.” Conventional Criticism is in some
doubt as to whether the poet derived that
musical name from “Hannibal,” “Anno
Belli,” or just plain “Cannibal”; the
“ Lee,” however, was obviously a prophetic
tribute to the great Southern leader, born
some years later. As to the heroine herself,
we must understand that she was either a
vivandiere of dubious parentage (“ high-born
kinsmen ”), or—possibly—a famous mare of
the strain of Mars—by George Washington,
out of Bunker’s Hill, from War of Inde-
pendence. Perhaps Conventional Criticism
will now tell us whence Jtioderic Quinn
derived the inspiration of “ The Camp
Within the West ” —perhaps our most beau-
tiful stray flower of verse, in its perfect
inter-dependence of emotion and form. Did
he derive it from the “ Eureka Stockade,”
or—further back still—from the tribal wars
of the aborigines ?
DPWELL O'REILLY.
[“ Conventional Criticism ” is invited to reply,
and will be paid at space rates. Meanwhile, one
wonders why Dowell O’Reilly does not dip his
nib in himself more freely.]

601
the “TYPHOID FLY”
DANGEROUS DOMESTIC PEST.
This is our well-known domestic pest—the
common house fly. In addition to being a typhoid
carrier, the fly may carry nearly all the intestinal
diseases and is the prime agent in the spread of
summer dysentery, being ifi this way unquestion-
ably responsible for the death of many children
in summer. The fly also possesses importance as
a disseminator of the bacilli of tuberculosis the
danger of human infection from tubercular flv
specks being by the ingestion of the specks
on food.
It is a startling thought that a person who
drinks a cup of tea or a glass of milk into which a
fly has fallen may take into his system 1,250,000
bacilli of typhoid, dysentery or tuberculosis.
Summing up the position, the typhoid or house
fly is a general or common carrier of pathogenic
bacteria. It may carry typhoid fever, Asiatic
cholera, dysentery, cholera morbus and other
intestinal diseases. It may carry the bacilli
of tuberculosis and certain eye diseases. It
is everywhere present, and it is disposed

the qualities which render INSECTIBAHE so

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLTETIN.]
The Importance of Being Me.
I am a speck upon a world
That’s but a microbe to the sun ;
Which, in the halls of boundless space.
Doth like a flying cinder run ;
Yet am not humbled at my fate ;
It s not my fault at any rate.
It only makes me feel some pride
When I review the countless suns
lliat spread the heavens on every side;
I hey re mine—as much as anyone’s;
And in their magnitude I see
I he great import of being me.
Through all my life (and I as long
Have lived as most men of my age)
I’ve held the fancy isn’t wrong
(Tn fact, I think it rather sage)
That if my being didn’t be
These things would not exist—for me.
The world exists because I live
Upon it, and I know it’s here ;
The sun I recognition give
Because I see its shining sphere ;
About the stars I worry not,
I simply take them in a lot. ’
This world concerns me most of all,
lor me built up through centuries ;
And yet in no wise I feel small.
Because it has a myriad nle’s.
I know all things I know or see
Are only there because of me.
Here kings may sit on thrones of State,
And Wade may boss a Parliament;
Some men endeavor to be great;
My landlord tries to get his rent.
As far as I’m concerned they do
Just what my mind gives knowledge to.
For all the difference it makes.
The stars, two-up, the sun and Wade,
Kings, beggars, cities, meat and calces,’
Books and religion, sport and trade—
The whole show, with its details fine,
May only be a dream of mine.
I know some people take great care,
By writing books and every wav, *
To prove that everything is where.
And just exactly what, they say ;
I hey do, because I know they do :
But them I may imagine, too.
I am not troubled at my size.
If everything means anything.
Subject 'to what I know it lies,
And I of what I know am king.
I see, in everything I see,
The great import of being me.
N.S.W.
HAMER
Gifts of the Deep.
Fisherman, fisherman, blithe and gav.
Light of heart as a man could wish,
What is the gift of the deep to-day—
-1 Dregs of the sea or goodly fish ? ”
Hauling his net with brave, brown hands,
Never an answering word makes he,
Sunk to his knees in the yielding sands,
Washed and wet by the break o’ the’sea.
Ever and ever the dancing floats
Near the shore as he sings and pulls ;
White of sail are the fishing boats,
White of wing the gathering gulls.
Coil on coil in a wet brown heap,
The long line lies on the beach behind,
Hung with weeds from the under-deep.
Shoreward sent by the shouting wind.
Hoisted high upon spread, grey wings,
Sea-hawks wheel in the drowsy noon ;
Joy in his throat, a bush bird sings,
1 hrilling the trees in their mid-day swoon.
“ Fishermen, yours is the task of all,
Nets are cast in a thousand seas ;
Hand over hand, day-long we haul,
Hope at the heart, or ill at ease.
“ Into the deep we cast our nets
(Dreaming, it may be, of glowing gain);
Out of the deep we dredge regrets,
Joy that dwindles and lasting pain.
“ Yet, brown folk o’ the sea have said
Chance, the giver, may send a pearl.
Yon is a lad with lifted head ;
Yon, and nigh him, a golden girl.
“Sunset burned when they hauled their net
(0, but the gods were kind above !)
There, on the beach, it shone sea-wet
Pearl of all pearls—the pearl of love.
“Strong man, true to your task, well done ! ”
Slowly the gift of the sea comes in
Fish that plunge in the light of the sun,
Flutter of tail and flash of fin.
Pulsing, leaping and all a-thrill,
Silver and grey, and bronze and bright,
Gouting the noon with crimson gill.
How they gleam in the golden light!
“Fisherman, fisherman, blithe and gay,
How shall the gods regard my wish ?
What shall I draw from the Deep to day,
Dregs of the sea or goodly fish ? ”
N.S.W.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tiik Bullktin would oblige the paver
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with reyard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a yood tinny to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bullktin,” is the proper
superscription for “copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 4, 1910.
THE AUSTRALIAN NOTES BILL.
In some respects the FiSHKIi, Government’s Aus-
tralian Notes Bill is a grievous disappointment to
those who had pinned their faith to the new
regime. The scheme for an issue of Federal
paper money is one which might have been made
to serve several valuable purposes quite different
from the very humble purpose of raising a little
casual revenue to go on with. It might have
been a means to extend the power and influence
of the Federal authority almost indefinitely. It
might also have served to obliterate, in course of
time, the gigantic debts of the States. If the
proceeds of the paper money issue were
applied exclusively to the establishment of
a sinking fund the gradual obliteration
of the State debts would start immediately.
Also the extension of the Federal influence would
start immediately, for every year would bring the
Commonwealth nearer to the position of the
State Governments’ chief creditor. If war came
when the sinking fund had accumulated to, say,
£100,000,000, and the interest which the Com-
monwealth collected from the State Governments
had risen to, say, £3,500,000 a year, it would
always be possible to make a bonfire of the State
securities on hand, suspend sinking fund pro-
ceedings till the war was over, and tiius make an
extra £3,500,000 a year available for defence
without levying a penny of extra taxation. War
would be the only possible excuse for such an
action, but in war time almost anything is ex-
cusable. If the war made loans inevitable, the
wiping diit; by the mere application of a match,
of the £100,000,000 or any other amount of old
debt represented by the sinking fund would make
the raising of new loans so much the easier. Thus
it follows that if the available proceeds of the
note issue were applied to sinking-fund purposes
they would constitute a means for the extension
of the Federal power, a means for the removal of
the public-debt incubus, a possible war-chest, and a
fund at the back of the Federal paper money
which would make it the best-secured note issue
on earth—better secured by far than that of the
Bank of England. And if, instead of merely
providing for & note circulation, the Government
were to require the banks, as in Canada, to
hold 40 per cent, of their cash reserves in Federal
paper, and applied the proceeds of that much
more extensive scheme to sinking-fund purposes,
it would get more money and could do all these
things in a still more effective fashion.
* * * *
Unfortunately, none of these things seem likely
to be done. -1 he gold obtained in exchange for
1 ederal notes is to be ‘‘expended for any pur-
pose that the Parliament directs ” ; but though
the Government majority, which in this easels
Parliament for all the purposes of the Act, pro-
bably has its purpose already in view, it doesn’t
take the community into its confidence on the
matter. The money may go into some interest-
producing investment, or it may not. But if it,
or any part of it, goes into an interest-producing
investment, the revenue is to drift into the Trea-
sury and become part of the ordinary Federal
income. That reduces the business to a mere
revenue-gaining device, and all its other possible
elements of usefulness go overboard.
* * * *
When there is no Government-owned bank with
a wide system of branches for the issue of Federal
notes, it becomes necessary to circulate them
through the private banks. At this point the
first difficulty comes in. Canada solved the
problem very simply. It really doesn’t care
much whether its notes circulate or not, but it
enacts that out of every £5 of cash reserves held
by the banks, at least £2 must be Federal paper.
That amount represents the paper money which
doesn’t circulate at all and isn’t intended to, and
as it doesn’t get worn out by passing from hand
to hand, it costs little for maintenance. In ad-
dition, the banlcs, in making payments, must
give out a certain proportion of the money
in Government notes (of no specified denomi-
nation) if the payee so demands. The re-
sult is a certain circulation of Dominion paper
among the public, and a large steady holding
of Dominion paper by the banks—a holding which
can’t come back on the Treasury in a heap with a
sudden demand for cash. Queensland didn’t aim,
like Canada, at making the banks hold a large
proportion of notes in reserve, but merely pro-
posed, as the Fisher Government does, to create
a circulation among the people. Its method was
simple and effective. Every bank may obtain
from the Government paper money equal to one-
third of its capital. The money received from
any bank in exchange for paper is put on fixed
deposit with the same institution at 2 per cent,
interest. The note money represents the cheapest
deposit the banks have, and the one that is least
liable to be called for ; therefore they want Govern-
ment notes—as many of them as they are allowed
to have. If the Government, instead of using the
money derived from this system as revenue, had
applied it to sinking fund purposes the idea,
though far inferior to that of Canada, would have
been a fair success all round.
* * * *
The Fisher Government adopted neither of
these schemes, but resorted instead to a rather
crude method which is set forth in Clause XI of
the Bill as follows :
(IX) If the payee so demands, a bank, when making any
payment, shall make the payment in Australian Motes,
and in the denominations asked for by the payee.
Penalty Five Pounds.
Nothing in this section shall compel a bank to pay
more than Twenty-five Pounds in Australian Notes in any
one payment.
About the payment in Australian notes there can
be no objection, but the payment in any possible
kind of notes is a different matter. It is
set forth in Clause VI of the Bill that Aus-
tralian notes are to be issued in amounts of
10s., £l, £5, £lO and “any multiple of £10”
which presumably means twenties and fifties.
In these circumstances the amount in notes which
the Bill professes to require that a bank shall
keep on hand in order to be prepared to meet one
£25 cheque would be £ll5 worth, made up as
follows :
50 notes of 10s. each .. .. £25
25 ~ ~ £1 ~ .. .. 25
5 ~ ~ £5 ~ .. .. .25
2 ~ ~ £lO ~ .. .. 20
Incite ~ £2O .. ... 20
• £ll5
With nothing less than that could the bank feel
sure that it could meet a £25 cheque in the
manner that the Bill prescribes. And if the cus-
tomer was liable to make an unexpected demand
for gold, and w'as a customer whom it would be
impolitic to disoblige, that would raise the re-
serve against a £25 cheque to £l4O. Something
of this was suggested to Prime Minister Fisher,
and he replied that if a real difficulty could be
proved the Bill might be altered to read that
every cheque of £25 or under would be payable
on demand in either 10s., £1 or £5 Federal notes
only. An amendment of the sort would certainly
ease the strain a little ; yet even under these con-
ditions this would be the safe legal reserve against
a £25 cheque :
50 notes of 10s. each .. .. £25
25 ~ „ £1 ~ .. .. 25
5 „ ~ 15 ~ .. .. 25
£75
And if it was advisable, as a matter of policy, to
have £25 in gold, in case an influential customer
asked for that “ meejum of exchange,” the reserve
against the £25 cheque would be £loo—£7s to
comply with the law, and £25 more to prevent
possible loss of business.
Most cheques that are cashed across the bank
counter are comparatively small, so this difficulty
would be liable to strike the bulk of the business.
And in Australia branch banks are numerous, so
the difficulty might arise at many places. The
case of the Commercial Bank of Australia may be
taken as a possible sample. Its last balance-sheet
showed that it had £1,266,127 of cash in the
Commonwealth. It does business at one head
office and 246 branches and agencies—247
establishments in all. If the money was
equally divided there would be £5126 available
for each office, and that amount can’t stand
any excessive strain. The Commercial of Aus-
tralia has £3,006,562 of deposits that are liable to
be called for at any moment (this is quite apart
from about £2,500,000 of fixed deposits, which are
periodically maturing). If an institution is
allowed to pay, say, half in gold and half in
any kind of notes that happen to be handy,
then in ordinary times £1,266,127 of legal
tender (whether the same be gold or Government
notes) is a superabundance to hold against
£3,006,562 of immediate liabilities and £2,500,000
of periodically maturing liabilities. But if the bank
is required to be ready to meet sudden demands in
three or four or five or even six different
forms of currency, the situation becomes exceed-
ingly precarious. The bank, if it wants to do
business, must accept a customer’s £25 current
account deposit in silver or gold, or 10s. or £1 or
£5 notes, or it must be content to take part of it
in £lO notes or in a £2O note. Yet if the law says
that it must be prepared to pay out the whole £25
in any one of three or five different forms, then
the law demands that it shall keep on hand more
money than it receives.
* * * *
In the circumstances, it is probable that the
best thing the Government can do is to throw
its Australian Notes Bill overboard root and
branch, and start afresh with the Canadian
sj'stem as its model. By so doing it will get
much more money and get it at much less trouble.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Recent Visit to Canberra.
A party of Senators has been visiting the pro-
posed Canberra Federal capital site at the
public expense, and it received full permis-
sion to also visit the old Dalgety site at its
own expense, if it lelt so inclined. The trip
happened after a period of rain, and things
were looking a trifle better than usual. Senator
McDougall fell into the Murrumbidgee, a river
which is reserved by the N.S.W. State Govern-
ment for the supply of the Barren Jack reservoir;
and the fact that there was enough water to wet
Senator McDougall in this river, which the
inhabitants of the Federal territory are not to be
allowed to use, is advanced as evidence that
there is an abundant water supply in the pro-
posed Federal territory. The Cotter, in flood, was
described by the Herald as “ never more than
20ft. wide,” yet the Senators are alleged by the
Sydney Tory press to have marvelled greatly
over it. -L he Cotter seems to be an unlucky river
somehow. Statistician Knibbs, in his “ Year
Book No. 2,” gives nearly nine closely-printed
pages, or nearly 18 closely-printed columns, to
the enumeration of the rivers of the Common-
wealth, and even includes a Queensland creek
two miles long; yet he doesn’t so much as men-
tion the Cotter, the Faddy or the Naas—the
streams on which the Federal city and
territory are to depend. Perhaps he saw them in
a dry time. Certainly Knibbs ought to be spoken
to severely about his failure to realise the exist-
ence of these rivers. Attorney-General Hughes
made the visit an opportunity to explain
that the arrangement by which the Federal
port stops at high-water mark, and, ex-
cept for about half an hour per day,
doesn’t even touch the water, really means
nothing. There will be no difficulty about letting
the Federal Government have the use of the
shore and the water; and the refusal of tne
N.S.W. State Ministry to define the territory as
extending down to low-water mark, and includ-
ing the use of the water beyond, only proves the
frank and untrammelled desire of the N.S.W.
State Ministry that the Commonwealth shall
have and use the whole shore and the adjacent
water. So far as can be gathered from the Sydney
morning press, the idea that there is anything
absurd in having the Federal capital territory in
two fragments about 100 miles apart, and one of
the fragments well inside the legal 100-miles limit
from Sydney, has been wholly dissipated by the
Senatorial visit and the fact that Mr. McDougall
fell overboard in a State river. The lamentable
scarcity of the local rainfall, as shown at the
adjacent town of Queanbeyan, which has the
poorest record, bar one, in all the coastal and
tableland districts of N.S.W., is also wiped out by
the fact that Senator McDougall, after a wet
spell, got very damp in the Murrumbidgee, quite
outside the proposed Federal water supply area.
In fact, the Congested Population party, which
wants to put the capital as close as possible to the
biggest city in Australia, so that it will do nothing
whatsoever towards peopling the great empty
spaces, professes to have been enjoying a great
time. Yet it is as solid as ever about the inad-
visability of letting the people decide the Federal
capital question, and no such thing as a Refer-
endum looms on the farthest horizon. There
seems to be among the Canberra push a dreadful
fear of a Referendum. The Bulletin would
gladly submit its view of the case to a popular
vote, inasmuch as its view isn’t based on a hurried
visit to ’Berra immediately after a heavy down-
pour; but Hughes and his friends shirk the ques-
tion in a curious way, as if they felt that their
story couldn’t stand public inspection.
_
Throe Famous Cases, and Some Distinctions
Between Them.
“ Lex”
It, is significant that the subjects on which “Liber-
alism ” in N.S.W. is moat touchy are (1) the McKyk case,
(2) the Coercion Act. “Liberalism ” doesn’t attempt to
seriously defend either of these unpleasantnesses. In-
stead, whenever either of them is reproachfully hurled at
it by a Labor-sympathiser, it cries tu quoque, and falls
back in disorder on the Friedman case. The line of
argument taken is that B. R. Wise, as Minister for
Justice in the See Administration, released Friedman
after the latter had been found guilty by a jury ;
that thereby British justice was flouted quite as flag-
rantly as it was in the cases of McKye and Bowling ;
that the Labor party omitted to fling Wise out of office’
for freeing Friedman—which is to say it condoned his
act, which is to say it should be held equally responsible
with Wise. There are fallacies in the above reasoning,
however. For instance, the cases of McKyk and Fried-
man differ. Both were convicted, both were handed
legal punishment, and both had that punishment, or part
of it, remitted. But McKye, who enjoyed the clemency of
Wade, was a second offender. And not only was the Judge
who tried his case notconsulted when it was reviewed, but
it is known that he was horrified at the Wade Govern-
ment’s conduct in the matter. In the case of Friedman
the initiative wasn’t taken by the See Government. The
Judge before whom that much-discussed Hebrew ap-
peared, himself wrote to the Attorney-General after the
jury returned its verdict, pointing out in strong terms
that justice had miscarried. Nor is there the least doubt
in the public mind but that it had. About the worst
offence a Minister for Justice could commit in such cir-
cumstances would have been to keep Friedman in gaol—
the Judge who heard his case having expressly reported
him to be innocent.
Again, there is a large hole, not to say a gaping chasm,
in the contention that “ the sacred principle of trial by
jury ” was as flagrantly violated by the release of Fried-
man as it was by the gaoling of Bowling. It is the
healthy practice of British law to look with a most
tolerant eye on the freeing of an accused person, while it
regards, with the greatest hostility, the frivolous gaoling
of anyone. A brand new law was invented by the Wade
Government before it was possible to get Bowling under
lock and key. As a result, Bowling went to prison, and
public opinion was so scandalised that amazed and out-
raged echoes sounded from as far away as the United
Kingdom.
No one was scandalised over the discharge of Fried-
man. The Colonial Office in London expressly com-
mended the step. The local Bar—-which recognises that
on the rare occasions when a Judge goes out of his way
to proclaim an accused person’s innocence, there is scant
doubt on the subject—was satisfied. The public, which
knew the Judge concerned in the matter for an able and
reputable official, hadn’t a word to say in protest.
The basic distinction between the cases of Bowling
and Friedman is the distinction that lies between
lightly sending a man to gaol and more or less lightly
taking him out of it. The average man isn’t
wildly interested in the merits of the case when he
learns that a Friedman has been unloosed ; on general
principles, he reckons it is all to the good. But he is apt
to be wildly interested, likewise alarmed, when he sees a
man whom one of his Judges acclaims as a person with
whom he (the Judge) would be proud to shake hands,
transformed into a gaol-bird, as it were by a stroke of the
legislative pen. Spectacles of that sort make perfectly
respectable and harmless people ponder apprehensively
as to whose turn it may be next. And the outcry against
coercive legislation, machine-made criminals and soon,
is swelled from all sorts of unexpected sources accord-
ingly.

Continuation of the Profound Silence of
Gregory Wade.
It is advisable to ask, at suitable intervals, right
up to election day, if Mr. Wade, Premier of
N.S.W., will kindly emerge from behind his veil
of reticence, and cast aside the whole garment of
secrecy, and explaiti what he meant by certain
dark allusions re Peter Bowling and a Labor
member unspecified. One of Wade’s strongest
suits is his habit of heaving around murky allusions
concerning the horrible things he could tell about
this or that opponent if he only let himself go,
but no matter how lavishly the said opponent
urges Wade to let himself go, he only retires
behind an entrenchment of gloom, and hints that
the time may yet come. It never does come,
but he always hints. In the matter at
issue, Wade suggested, as one speaking
through a glass darkly, that if he re-
vealed the machinations of some Labor man or
other re Peter Bowling, the party would proba-
bly be covered with shame like an inverted
bucket of mud. In response to a clamor for par-
ticulars, he inferred, like the sun behind a cloud,
that he was chained to a policy of concealment.
Offers to release him from any possible or proba-
ble pledge of hiddenness or reticence only caused
him to wrap himself in the toga of his frayed dig-
nity, and retire within himself, and bang the
door of his great personality. But his faithful
follower, Fitzpatrick, was less secretive. He
declared that he knew all about it, and that the
fact was that Grahame, member for the coaly
town of Wickham, had urged Wade to make the
law under which P. Bowling was gaoled even
more strenuous than it is, in order, presumably, to
get P. Bowlinga longer term of gaol than he is now
serving. This charge suggested two possibilities
—one, that Fitzpatrick was telling a plain, un-
varnished falsehood ; and the other that lie was
telling the truth, and that Wade, the only man
from whom he could possibly have got the informa-
tion he said he possessed, had broken his pledge of
secrecy. Grahame flatly denied the charge, and
asked Wade if he dared to corroborate it. He
gave Wade a full and complete receipt for any
and every possible obligation to lie low or to lurk
behind the herb of ambiguity, and called on him
to come out in the open and say if he (Grahame)
had really done the thing charged against him.
Wade, who had started the whole trouble,
only enveloped himself more profoundly in
the tattered Kidderminster carpet of his
magnificent seclusion, and said, “Why drag
me into it?”—as if he hadn’t been in it
from the beginning. There the matter rests. It
gets no “forrarder.” Wade has declared that
he knows something horrible, but is bound not to
reveal it. Fitzpatrick says he knows the hor-
rible thing that Wade knows (Wade having
apparently broken his promise to keep the hor-
rible thing dark) and Fitzpatrick states it. The
accused person (Grahame) denies the alleged
horrible circumstance, discharges Wade, who
started the whole shindy, from any real or
imaginary obligation to keep silent, and asks
Wade to say whether he (Grahame) is a
liar, or whether Fitzpatrick is one, or
whether Wade is one himself. And as the
charge against Grahame was one of shameful
treachery he has every right to demand an ex-
planation. And all Grahame gets is a back view
of Waders he retires with a limited amount of
dignity into his tent. The trouble now is that if
Wade, goaded to fury, rises up in violent corro-
boration of Fitzpatrick he won’t command any-
thing like the credence he would have done if he
had corroborated Fitzpatrick on the spot. The
faded cloak of mystery is a failure when the cloak
is too short and it is worn too long.
A Remarkable Sinking Fund.
Further details are to hand about the great
sinking fund by which Joseph Ward proposes to
pay off the Maoriland public debt inside 75 years.
The idea was so utterly unlike Joseph that every-
body who knew him expected to find it loaded,
and these people are not disappointed. In the
first place, the sinking fund arrangement doesn’t
apply to quite all the debt. By some murky
process of " transfer” the Government has found
justification for paying off only £60,000,000 in-
stead of the actual £70,000,000 or £72,000,000.
Then the floating of new loans is to go on gaily as
per usual. These two matters being understood,
the actual sinking fund question comes on the
board. A certain sum—the amount which
the Controller and Auditor-General shall de-
clare to be necessary—is to be paid each
year to the sinking fund commissioners. Having
got the money in hand there seem to be
three courses naturally open to the commis-
sioners, though, curiously enough, they are to
take none of them. They might buy up some of
the new loan which Maoriland is going to issue
regularly as aforetime, though the proceeding
would be rather an absurd sort of business; but
as that would to some extent diminish the influx
of foreign cash on which Ward has built
his boom, it is not going to be done. Then
they might invest the money by taking up
part of the renewal loans which Maoriland has to
float every year, by reason of the beastly habit of
falling due with which the old loans are afflicted ;
but that would also be sending some money out
of the country, and Ward wants to keep all the
money in the country to boom with. So that
device is also ruled out. The Commissioners are
not even to buy up Government securities in the
open market. Joseph Ward’s really brilliant idea
is that the sinking fund money is to be invested in
loans to settlers, local bodies and so on, for terms
extending up to 40 years. And apparently it is
only when the money comes back from these loans
that the real repayment of the debt is to com-
mence. A scheme like that is liable to break
up rather badly. It is one thing to with
draw, say, £BOO,OOO a year from the country
and apply it to the repurchase of Maoriland
securities in England. It is quite another thing to
invest, say, £BOO,OOO every year for perhaps 25 years
in local land-booming, and then when there is
£20,000,000 or £2s,ooo,oooinvested in the boom to
try to get it out so as to pay off £20,000,000
or £25,000.000 of debt in a heap. The attempt to
realise £20,000,000 out of a big boom is more
likely to yield £40,00Q,000 worth of smash
than £20,000,000 of cash. The business is all a
juggle and a postponement. Ward himself is to
go on borrowing for productive works and unpro-
ductive works. He will probably borrow enough
for wholly unproductive works—the kipd which
should clearly be paid for out of cover
his payments to the sinking fund—in other words,
he will probably borrow the sinking fund money.
He will use the sinking fund to keep up his
boom, and years hence, when Ward is politicallv
gone, it will be someone eise's job to try and


extricate the hard cash out of the boom and start
making the actual repayments. A marvellous
financier is .T. Ward. His motto seems to be :
“ Things will last my time.”
Speeding Up.
Now that the Richmond smash has ensured some
sort of inquiry into the whole administration of
the Victorian railways, the harassed employees
are beginning to speak openly. The worst victims
seem to be the signalmen ; the Department’s set
policy appears to be to work them at what an
engineer would call the breaking strain, but only
to pay them on the ordinary working-strain basis.
Twelve consecutive hours in a busy signal-box
seems to be a common occurrence. One man tells
of having put in 15 hours daily for three consecu-
tive days, and he has had no respite for two years,
though two lots of annual holidays are long over-
due. In the busy boxes the man has the
assistance of a “recorder” —a boy allegedly
of 16 or over, though one is stated to be
only 14—whose duty is merely to make time
entries in the books. If he asks for a
qualified adult assistant he gets no immediate
reply, but is liable to find himself transferred to
some remote station in the Backbloeks, or “ad-
vanced” to the position of general laborer at
7s. 6d. a day. Wherefore he struggles on as long
as he can—or until the smash comes. Periodi-
cally an inspector wanders round and remarks
that the employees are somewhat slow—“ the
Department thinks they ought to be speeded up
a bit.” But the case of the hapless “fog signalmen”
is even worse. The Department doesn’t employ any
real fog signalmen; it expects porters on after-
noon duty to make themselves available at 6 a.m.
If there is a fog they go on duty from that hour
until 11 or 12 o’clock ; if not, they may return to
roost again. Figures are given, and depart-
mentally admitted to be correct, showing that
porters whose ordinary hours total 77 per week
have been frequently called on, after but three or
four hours’ rest, to take up another four hours’
spell of “fog ” duty; then they rest two hours, and
afterthathave totackleordinary toilonce more. Of
course these unfortunate porters are paid for their
extra toil, but they don’t get overtime rates.
They are paid for this special, extra-respon-
sible work just the ordinary porter’s wages.
In fact,'*if a fog signaller be, say, a night watch-
man doing 12 hours for 55., he gets only ss. for
12 hours’ fog signalling. This isn’t called sweat-
ing or slave-driving ; it is designated “speeding
up.” The porter doesn’t naturally work at a
breakneck pace, because even Tait hasn’t yet got
into a regular habit "of having two trains at one
platform at once, so the Department makes up
for the porter’s lack of swiftness by extending his
hours to 14 or 16 per day, at the usual rate of
pay. It will be remembered that when Tait,
who comes from a country where a 10-hour day
largely obtains, first reached Australia, he failed
to bestow his gracious approval on the eight-hour
system, but was, nominally at least, forced to
recognise it. It seems he has surreptitiously
undermined it, and has gone several hours better.
Only he calls it “speeding up.”

More “ Federal Interference.”
The Tasmanian Assembly, having given up all
hope of being able to successfully negotiate with
Wade’s Department of Agriculture, unanimously
resolved the other day to urge the Commonwealth
Parliament to take over the inter-State inspection
of fruit. Clause 112 of the Australian Constitu-
tion provides that a State may levy on imports
and exports “ such charges as may be necessary
for executing the inspection laws of the State.”
It is claimed, however, that N.S.W. Minister
Perry’s inspection fees are so high that their impo-
sition amounts toaserious tax on the produce-raising
citizens of other States ; and it is threatened that,
if the unfederal Wade Ministry is not effectively
dealt with by the Australian Government, legal
proceedings will be taken against it, in order to
find out whether one State can levy import duties
against another State by merely calling them in-
spection dues. Meanwhile, Wade’s Minister for
Agriculture, Perry, is sending frantic telegrams
to Tasmanian and other State authorities, urging
them to join with N.S.W. in “ resisting Federal
interference.” One such telegram was received
by Premier Lewis during the debate on this fruit-
inspection matter ; whereupon the Speck’s Assem-
bly expressed its utter disgust, and unanimously
decided that the Australian Government was to
be trusted to pursue codlin moth and fruit-fly
in a fair and effective manner, while the wretched
Wade Administration wasn’t.
Sydney’s 111-Regulated Harbor.
The marine worm—which is by no means the
same thing as the sea-serpent —has turned, and is
making a fearsome row about the inefficient
lighting of Sydney harbor. Men of the profession
of which Noaii was the oldest exponent clamor
for a perfectly new system of leading lights and
beacons, by careful attention to which a ship-
master can at least clear himself of all moral
responsibility when the collision takes place. He
will probably lose his certificate all the same, for
the old, leaky, dismasted Marine Court seems to
go on two simple principles : (1) If a master
breaks the rules and there is a collision he may be
passed out for breaking the rules ; (2) if he sticks
to the rules and there is a collision he may be
passed out for not “using his discretion”
or something like that. But even if the pro-
posed leading lights and beacons don’t supply
any mental illumination to the Court, it is
reckoned that they may do good in other ways.
Sydney Harbor, into which some unspeakable
fools want to herd the Australian navy in
addition to its other troubles, is a very crowded
spot for its size. Its entrance isn’t a patch on
Port Darwin for depth, nor is it so deep as the
channel that leads across Port Phillip to Port
Melbourne; still it is a very good entrance. Inside,
however, when the shallows and islands and the
great jutting point of Bradley’s Head are allowed
for, it isn’t so wildly commodious as is generally
supposed, and it is badly lighted and badly regu-
lated. It isn’t by any means a favorite with
shipmasters, who greatly prefer the unpictu-
resque but more easily navigated waste of
grey waters which leads to Port Melbourne.
Collisions are horribly frequent, especially
in the dangerous neighborhood of Bradley’s Head,
where vessels, trying to steer in every direction at
once in order to comply with all the varied decisions
recently given by the marine authorities, come
suddenly on each other round the corner, with
the result that the fairway is fast getting silted
up with lost certificates. The sudden clamor for
a new lighting system has roused to speech the
two powers which divide control of the port.
One of these, the Harbor Trust, says a new system
is most essential, and that it has been considering
the matter itself for quite a while. The other,
the Department of Navigation, reckons the idea
to be wholly unnecessary. But this latter body is
looked upon with a good deal of scorn by sea-
faring men, some of whom reckon it can’t even
mark a wreck properly. At times, their language
about it includes expressions that they wouldn’t
use to the ship’s cat. They would prefer to
throw the mouse-hunter overboard than smirch its
young mind with some of the things they say about
the blessed Department.
How the Vic. Railway Department Treats
Its Accident Cases.
A tale of the Victorian railways :
Long before the list of sufferers by the Richmond
smash was completed, the Vic. Railway Department
started doling out “ compensation ” to claimants whose
names and cases had been sent in. The Melbourne news-
papers announced this fact without comment, and doubt-
less a number of unreflecting readers thought that the
Department showed commendable promptitude. The
truth is that in thus hastening to settle with shocked
and damaged passengers, ere they can fully estimate, or
criticise the doctor’s estimate of, the possible results of
their damages and shocks, ihe Department is merely
aggravating its offence. No man knows, much less
a woman, how much injury he or she has suf-
fered from a railway accident which happened
only a week previously ; but the Railway policy
apparently is to employ medical officers for the
purpose of judging, not how much but how
little, the mutilated claimants may have been
harmed, and for the further purpose of advising the
patient to accept the compensation which the Department
offers. Obviously, the practitioners who act for the De-
partment are engaged to be optimists rather than pessi-
mists ; also they are interposed as much as is possible
between the patient and any outside medical adviser
whom the patient might prefer to accept as an authority.
But —and there is much significance in this but —if
another doctor be in possession of a railway accident case
before an official adviser comes upon the scene, he is
liable to be “approached” with a view to a small
and early settlement of the question of damages.
He is sworn in, so to speak, as a special settle-
ment-maker, even as outside citizens are enrolled as special
constables in cases of grave emergency. If he calls in
another practitioner for consultation, it is often one
or both of the Departmental nominees whom he sum-
mons. Mainly by means of this system of working settle-
ments, the A 7 ic. Railways were enabled to escape from
the consequences of the Sunshine disaster with alossof
little more than £lOO,OOO in compensations, though more
than 400 persons were injured and 47 killed—an average of
£2O to £3O for each claimant.
In the Sunshine case solicitor Woolf distinguished him-
self by claiming to represent the public in general
whilst appearing specially on behalf of killed and
injured clients. He then argued that, inasmuch
as everybody in Victoria was a possible future
victim of the railways, everybody should be protected
against future accidents. In exacting better terms for
his clients than the Department had offered, he en-
forced the argument that nobody, save possibly the Rail-
way Commissioners, desires to deal meanly with the
victims of a smash, seeing that where the passengers, as
members of the State, provide their own railways, liberal
self-compensation is the first law of nature if self-
preservation be impossible. Therefore, the name of
lawyer Woolf was anathema to the Department two
years ago. And he is in a similar position in this Rich-
mond disaster. He was instructed to take up the case
of a girl patient. After she had been taken home from the
Richmond smash-up a local doctor was called in to attend
her, and when in due course her brother asked Woolf to
look after her interests, the tactics of the Department
were glaringly exposed. In the first place, a leading
physician whom the lawyer commissioned to examine
the patient was “choked off” by the suburban doctor
in attendance, being told that the patient was practi-
cally quite recovered, and it was therefore unnecessary
to see her. Also the suburban doctor stated in an
authoritative tone that the physician “ wouldn’t get
paid ” if he did see her. The lawyer opened a corres-
pondence with the vigorous young doc. (who seemingly
had for the moment persuaded the sick, hysterical girl to
leave her case in his hands), and finally the lawyer’s
managing clerk went to interview him re his unsatisfactory
letter-writing and his policy of obstruction. The clerk
found him in telephonic communication with the Railway
Department, and making an appointment with a railway
official to come out —presumably to fix up a settlement.
Also, he stated that he was “ authorised to settle ” his
patient’s claim against the Department. How it comes
about that a patient’s doctor should take such a lively
interest in advising his patient—while he or she is still
hardly in a fit state to consider matters —to accept a
small sum from the Department and be done with the
business doesn’t appear very clear; but for some reason
or other there are doctors who do that sort of thing.
Therefore, any damaged railway traveller would be well
advised to have dark suspicions of his or her medico
should the latter show a desire to diverge from medicine
into the paths of finance—very small and prompt finance
that is.

The Coronation Swear.
In addition to the row at our doors about
King George V.’s oath of accession—and, in all
conscience, that local row is bad enough —we now
Begin to hear by cablegram of far-off holy disturb-
ances based on the same subject. They are much
the same as those to which we are accustomed in
Australia, and any one is a fair sample of the rest.
Let this item stand for the lot:
London, Thursday Afternoon.—The Protestant Truth
Society has protested against the new Accession Declara-
tion.
The society maintains that the new Declaration does
not provide against mental reservation and Papal dispen-
sation, and is only parallel with the oaths taken by
Charles 11., and James 11., which were ineffective.
Members of the society marched in procession to
Downing-street yesterday crying out, “No Popery!”
The police stopped the demonstration.
To the peaceful agnostic, who has given up all the
creeds and churches and clamors and confessions
and catechisms as things that are beyond human
comprehension, the most impressive feature of
this business is its awful futility. These No
dfopery people apparently hold with firmness to
the following propositions :
(1) That the Romish Church may authorise the King to
tell a lie by saying that he is a Protestant when he isn't
one.
(2) But if he is asked in addition whether the Romish
Church has authorised him to tell the above-mentioned
lie, the Church will be bowled out.
Any ordinary person might think that if the
Church authorised the King to tell a lie by
saying he wasn’t a Catholic, it might just
as easily authorise him to tell a second
lie by saying that he hadn’t been authorised
to tell the first lie. But these simple-minded
people seem to reckon that that is impossible.
It is certainly a complicated situation. Assum-
ing, as the old Coronation Oath certainly does
assume, that the British Sovereign is probably
a liar, and is likely to have taken the pre-
caution to obtain from Rome a permit, not
only to lie but also to shamelessly blaspheme
against the faith which in his heart he holds
sacred, it seems quite useless to administer
any oath at all. The sort of king who requires
that sort of oath to bind him is plainly the sort of
king with whom no oath has any value whatever.
Edward VII. took the old oath, yet he made no
objection when his niece turned Roman Catholic
and married a Catholic sovereign. Also, it has
been openly declared that he died a Catholic, and
it has been hinted that the new Queen has leanings
in that direction. This sort of suspicion is out of
the reach of any oath yet invented. In any case the
whole business is lamentably unsuited to British
conditions. The R. C. Church had a fairly
dreadful record in other countries, though
even in 1 Spain, where things were at their
.worst, the Inquisition only averaged 125
executions per annum, and seeing that the
tiny community of N.S. W. had in one single year
(1332) 82 executions, together with floggings in-
numerable, it can hardly complain. But in
Britain the Church of England, and not the
R. C. crowd, was the great historical persecutor.
There were persecutions directed against the
Reformers under Henry IV., Henry V.,
Henry VIII. (the founder, in a casual sort of
way, of the present Church of England) and
Mary. (The last-named, who was distinctly a
horror, averaged 71 executions per annum.)
But all these troubles covered a very few years all
told, while the persecutions of the Roman
Catholics of Ireland lasted for two centuries, and
the evils done in Ireland to secure the supremacy
of the English Church left the proceedings of
Alva in the shade. It was nine years after
the last semblance of the Inquisition was
blotted out before the Church of England
was forced by the secular authority to leave off
being an active persecutor. The Inquisition,
that old and utterly infamous institution, founded
by the Greek emperor Theodosius in 382, burst
its last boiler in 1820, yet it was not till 1829
that the Irish Roman Catholic ceased to rank, in
the eye of the law, with a horse or an ox. And it
was not till nearly 50 years after the complete
vanishment of the Inquisition (say about 1868 or
1870) that the Church of England ceased to
have people put in gaol for refusing to
contribute to its collection plate under the name
of “ church rates.” And quite a number of people
aren’t sure even to this day that the English
Church is Protestant. It holds itself coldly aloof
from assured Protestants, such as Methodists,
Presbyterians and Lutherans. It has a High
Church section which believes in altars, vest-
ments, candles and confession, and is suspected
of wearing a hair shirt next its skin. Certain
of the adherents of the Church of England
declare that it never broke away from Rome,
but that Rome broke away from it; and
they hold that their fane is to this day
the real old church of St. Peter and Gregory the
Great, with the name blown in the bottle, and all
others are spurious imitations. Even the most
vigorous swearing of allegiance to sueh a
dubious church seems a rather doubtful se-
curity against Catholicism. William 111.,
the first British sovereign who took the Corona-
tion Oath about which the trouble has arisen, was
a Dutch Protestant of undoubted integrity, and
had nothing in common with the halting, semi-
Catholie, semi-Protestant Tabernacle of England.
But George Y. isn’t of the old Dutch school.
Perhaps the surest method is to make him turn
Primitive Methodist or Salvationist. No one
ever suspected the Big Drum of any leanings
towards Rome.

The Duty of a Deteriorating Nation.
Foster Fraser, the shallow book-writer who
rushed along the beaten tracks of Australia on a
lecturing tour, and then returned to England to
put his impressions into print, has now taken up
a critical position half-way between the Devil and
the deep sea. His summing-up of the climatic
and human evidences of this country is anti-
Australian. His suggestion for delaying the
alleged deterioration of the Australian race,
which he professes to regard as inevitable, is dis-
tinctly anti-British., For whereas F. Fraser
maintains that the demoralising effects of climate
are visible in the third generation of Australian
descendants of European settlers, he advocates a
constant emigration of British and Irish to these
allegedly lowering latitudes in order to preserve
Australian virility. And he cheerfully states
that “circumstances will always bring to Aus-
tralia good material, producing a fine race.” In
short, the fatuous Fraser predicts and advocates
a steady withdrawal of persons from the cold
countries to a land where their stock, he says, is
bound to depreciate. This country, by Fraser’s
showing, will have a fine race for just as long as
the average quality of its inhabitants is main-
tained by a supply of virile immigrants. And, of
course, the larger the old decayed population on
hand, the more virile immigrants we will want to
boost things up. A time may come when
1,000,000 British and Irish per annum will only
just till the bill. Yet as long as Peter is robbed
on a big enough scale to pay Paul,
the latter will continue to be fat and
flourishing. By transferring the whole of
Peter’s substance—all the virile population of
Britain and Ireland—to Australian Paul,
the latter’s race would be made mighty fine and
large for one and even two generations; but in
the third generation it would deteriorate, and
what it would be like in the tenth generation the
Lord only knows. And only the Lord knows
what the Australians of the future would be like
if no more Europeans were to come along
and take part in the national deteriorating. This
is a problem which in the interests of man-
hood ought to be solved. Foster Fraser’s ideas
are an insult to the uatural law of the survival of
the fittest. Why leave this land of deterioration
open to virile foreign people? They should be
prohibited from emigrating to Australia. Foster
Fraser has pronounced our doom, and though it
be delayed it cannot be averted. Where the pro-
portion of deteriorating natives is steadily in-
creasing (by Foster Fraser’s dictum), the ab-
surdity of attempting to establish a fine race is
painfully obvious. Let us be content to put our-
selves in quarantine and paste a notice on the
padlocked gate: “ This country closed for in-
evitable deterioration.” It is the duty we owe to
humanity at large.

The Crab’s Progress.
.July 24 was celebrated by the Victorian churches
as Labor Sunday, when Labor aspirations and
ideals were dealt with by a large number and
variety of parsons. And the manner in which
these parsons walked, ran and skipped round
their subject, without ever attempting to catch
hold of it and examine it, bordered on the miracu-
lous. In many instances the fear of the rich man,
who supplies the bulk of the pew-rents, was pal-
pably the cause of this manoeuvring. In other
cases the failure to grasp the subject seemed to
arise simply from ineptitude. As a fair sample of
one very popular style of futile argument this is
given :
Modern Socialism said ... Give us favorable environ-
ment, good healthy food, good clothing and good houses
to live in, and you will see the improvement in the man ;
see what a better man he will be. The Christian religion
said this was the wrong way, and that they must begin
with a man’s heart, with his inner life, his convictions,
his conscience. If they thought they were going to raise
men in connection with their higher well-being merely
by different surroundings and by favorable conditions,
that was the grand mistake. That was the rock on which
modern Socialistic theories must be broken.
And when the parson calls for this mysterious
alteration in the “ heart” and the “inner life,”
he picks out the softest pillow he can, turns out
the light, and goes to sleep happy and satisfied
that he has done his duty. If environment
doesn’t matter, he might choose a brick for a
pillow, and tiirn up the light, and encourage a
dog to howl in his ear ; but where his own per-
sonal comfort is concerned he isn’t such an ass.
But while he is careful about his own surround-
ings in that way, it doesn’t occur to him that
men and women who are hungry and badly-
clothed are bad subjects forsesthetics and theology;
and that, from the parson’s point of view, their
hearts are proba,bly a minus quantity. And this
kind of parson never seems to think that, even if
the Socialists’ programme doesn’t rank high in
an ethical sense, it is a very fair foundation for
something better. Modern Socialism begins at
the beginning, and seeks to supply man’s first and
primitive needs—food and warmth. It believes
that, until these demands are met, it is no use
talking to the man about his “inner life”; in
fact, the Socialist says that there can be no sound,
sane inner life vvithout food and warmth. Also,
instead of blaming a man for wanting food, the
Socialist says that it is natural. But to all such
reasoning this kind of parson turns a deaf ear ;
and, in a voice full of stupidity, he calls on man-
kind to begin at the top and progress back-
wards and downwards. No house was ever
built from the top ; and the only animal that
progresses (so to speak) backwards is the crab,
and even it does not go straight backwards.
Whether this plea of the Church for attention to
the “inner life” at the outset is genuine is a
debatable question. It may be just a stall to save
the Church from running counter to the political
opinions of the rich members of the fold. If it is
genuine, then the Church can prepare itself for
the inevitable time when its only adherents will
be a few rich old maids with a mania for foreign
missions. For no country nowadays has any use
for a church which considers that the supply of
“good healthy food, good clothing, and good
houses” is the wrong way to start improving the
common lot.

When Wattle’s in Bloom.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear days that are warm with earth’s gladness,
When life is a glorified thing ;
Though the parsons are stricken with madness —
Abjuring all grief and all sadness,
Let’s arise and be cheerful in spring !
Fair morns that are splendid and gracious,
That are flagrant with blossomed delight;
When the soul groweth noble and spacious,
Holding feelings serene and veracious,
Whilst the preachers get angry and fight!
Bright hours and high noons of dear glory—
Rich moments of rapture and grace,
When the cove who is holy and hoary
Resurrecteth the blood-soaken story,
And the whiskers dilate on his face !
We are sinful, they say, in our morals,
We are shipped, f.0.b., to our doom ;
But the saints ? —hear the Snowballs and Wor-
ralls.
Also Judkins— how fiercely he quarrels
When Wattle’s in Bloom !
Dear days when the voice of Dill Macky
Lifteth loudly at morning and noon ;
Hear the sectary’s frantic attack; he
Is a sanctified clerical lackey,
Who was boiling with fervor in June !
In July, lo ! the saint effervesces,
All his rancor explodes with a roar ;
In the spring-time he thunders addresses—
He alternately curses and blesses,
And he clamors for Somebody’s gore !
Bright hours when the Pope is confounded,
And the pietists caper and sing;
When the timbrel of conflict is sounded—
When the jaw and the blare are unbounded,
Glad products, dear brethren, of spring !
’Tis the time when the blear-eyed fanatic
Pursues holy spooks round the room ;
Yea, the wheezy and old and rheumatic
Hold sectarian tongue-fights ecstatic
When Wattle’s in Bloom.
Dear days when the shrill politician
Talketh loudly and needlessly long ;
When the howl of the vexed Opposition
Is like that of the fiends in perdition
Prodded deep with Beelzebub’s prong !
Very fierce is the wail of each member—
Hear the Johnsons and Kellys and Cooks ;
They will rage louder still in November—
They will roar like the damned in December,
They are moaning at present like spooks !
Bright hours of debate and division—
Hear the clang of the Parliament bells ;
“ To the right of the chair !”—in collision,
Hear the jeer and the gibe of derision
As the teller despondently tells !
Hear the cheer of the person who Sosheth,
And the wail of Freetrade in its tomb;
Though ’tis dead as the late Mephibosheth,
Still the tide of inanity rushetli
When Wattle’s in Bloom !
Dear days that are filled with the clamor
Of parties and views out of date;
’Midst the roar of the shrill gospel-jammer
And the bleating of Cooks, still the hammer
On the drill pounds an anthem elate !
Though the whiskers are needlessly waving,
And the Judkinses blare extra loud;
Though the Johnsons and Kellys are raving,
There’s a Prospect, dear brethren, of saving
All this Land from the cere-cloth and shroud !
Fair morns that are signs of the guerdon,
New hopes and New Leaders to dare ;
Whilst they rage so one can’t get a word in,
Let us march ! —bend your backs ’neath the burden,
Let’s be done with regret and despair !
Whilst the Talkers and Wowsers are vicing,
Be ye forward to act in their room ;
Hoist the Flag of our hopes— keep it flying ! —
For the old , foolish factions arc dying
When Wattle’s in Bloom !

well for granted that the “ farming industry,” and
country industries in general, must not be dealt
with at all. It got at last to be accepted, as a sort of
theological dogma or an axiom of Euclid that the

hours per day, for the munificent wages of 10s.
or 12s. per week and the calf-skins. The calf-
skins were a very precarious addition to the
employee’s wage, as the number of calves on one
dairy farm is, of course, limited, and calves only
occur at one season of the year. Further, the
unhappy employee had to skin the calves himself,
and sell the skins to the casual hawker. Of late
years the calf-skin agreement has been ridiculed
out of fashion ; but even to-day the ruling wage
for a man working the hours already stated is
from 15s. to 20s. per week and found. Even then
he is not given permanent employment. And all
the while this iniquity has been going on land has
been soaring up in value. For example, in the
Victorian Western District there is not an acre
of dairying land which is not to-day worth at
least double its value 10 years ago. But the
Fisher proposal, which will probably, in a round-
about way, end in considerably altering the awful
status of the farm hand, will be cursed by shire
councils, agricultural societies, farmers’ leagues
and the like even unto seventy times seven. And
the rage and horror of the Legislative Council
obstructionists and their allies at being circum-
vented will be a fearsome thing to witness. Alto-
gether it is a cold day for the advocate of cheap
labor and the employer of women and children,
and their prospects are just as dark as an
aboriginal’s funeral.
