Defence Made Easy.
If a Japanese armada swooped down on Australia, it
would not come in at the back door as many have sup-
posed. It would come straight to Sydney, the head-
quarters of the wealth and population of the continent.
The loss of the Northern Territory would affect us no
more than the capture of New Guinea. No enemy could
come overland from the north. — Colonel Campbell.
And on this text “D.M.W.” speaks a piece:—
The people of Australia should be deeply indebted to
Colonel Campbell for his expert opinion on Australian
defence, and the easy and pleasant, not to say inex-
pensive, manner in which he dismisses the whole ques-
tion. It will be remembered that when Hannibal began
his invasion of the Roman territories he did not (as so
many untruthful historians have asserted) bring his
armies through Spain and Gaul, and over the passes of
the Alps. Such a proceeding, as Colonel Campbell knows,
would have been utterly absurd. As a matter of
course, he went straight to Rome, the headquarters
of the wealth and population of the peninsula. The
loss of Ois-Alpine Gaul could have affected Rome
no more than the loss of Corsica. Similarly when Wil-
liam the Norman invaded England he did not land at the
obscure port of Hastings (as the absurd historians still
frequently assert) but at London, the headquarters of
the wealth and population of the island. Again, when
Britain was at war with France in 1759 it didn’t attack
that country in its remote outpost of Canada, which
would have been foolish. It sent Wolfe and his army
straight to Paris. The story of Quebec and the Heights
of Abraham is a fish narrative. As Colonel Campbell
knows, and as he has been kind enough to point out, the
invariable method adopted by an invading army is to
make a direct attack upon the chief city of the invaded
territories. Any other method would be ridiculous, and
would excite laughter, and nothing annoys an invader
more than to be laughed at. Thus it will be observed
that in the defence of England the only point necessary
to consider is the efficient protection of London. In the
defence of the German Empire, Berlin is the one place
at which an attack need be feared. In the late
war with Japan Russia triumphed through the security
of St. Petersburg because no Japanese attack was pos-
sible at any other point. The loss of Port Arthur could
have affected Russia no more than the loss of Nova
Zembla. This is the great and foundation principle that
must be borne in mind in preparing to resist any inva-
sion. The invaders must attack the headquarters of
wealth and population. The problem of Australian
defence is thus narrowed down to a very small point
indeed, and with a few strategic railways radiating from
the headquarters of the wealth and population of this
continent to bring the Federal forces through the Red-
fern tunnel all must be well. Australia is under a
deep obligation to Colonel Campbell for his timely state-
ment by which he has placed the Northern Danger in its
true light, and shown us from an expert military point
of view what we may really expect.
And yet, Colonel Campbell, with all due respect to your
superior knowledge, the loss of the Northern Terri-
tory would affect us more than the loss of Papua.
Let Northern Australia once be occupied by an
Asiatic Power, and a long and bitter and gory
struggle begins along an ever-varying boundary line.
Year by year would see the" brown or yellow
man creeping further to the south. Year by year
the dauntless few of the white race would hurl them-
selves upon the countless thousands of the alien. Year
by year the broken remnants of our armies would
struggle back to defend a narrower Commonwealth. At
last the headquarters of the wealth and population of a
continent would hear the enemies’ guns, but they would
sound their challenge not from bevond Manly and
Coogee, but from Willoughby and Summer Hill. The
Northern Territory does matter. It is at once our great
hope, and our chief point of danger.
Mostly About Boots.
“0.K.” writes:—
The “gags” of the shop windows are getting on my
nerves. Seeing that the public is supposed to be pro-
tected by the law against various kinds of impositions on
its credulity, I would suggest that misleading window-
cards or goods-tickets be added to the list of frauds. For
instance, the card which presents in one corner a bogus
original price of the article printed in black ink with a
red line running through it whilst the allegedly reduced
price in red ink appears in an opposite corner. Thus the
most gullible customers—and many of them are most
gullible—are made to believe that something previously
marked at, say, 14s. 6d. is now being offered at 11s. 9d.,
and so forth, although such cards are palpably mere tricks
of the trade. All shops, or nearly all, resort to this hollow
attempt at deception. Evidently a measure of success
is expected, or the system wouldn’t be so popular. Drapers
and fancy-goods dealers are persistent offenders in this
particular. Also they are addicted to the habit of at-
taching “sale price” cards to their goods,.as though
prices in general were not intended to effect sales. But
the bootshops are the most irritating of all in their
catchpenny devices. The card with two prices printed
on it is only a drop in the ocean of boot-sellers’ “ gags.”
You see a pair of shoes marked “For Present Wear,” as
though other pairs were not for immediate use, but were
meant to be “ laid down ” in a cellar, or somewhere, like
port wine. You see boots with pointed toes labelled
“Latest Fashion,” with round toes labelled “Extreme
Mode,” with square toes professing to be “ Up to Date,”
ranged side by side. This awful conflict of allegations
seems calculated to soften the brains of the credulous who
are looking for mere boots for wearing purposes. The
mention of “ wearing purposes” brings me to the matter
of leather, which is a matter concerning the health of
the community. Sickness often, and death occasionally,
are the results of fraudulent and inferior boot-soles. The
“ stinking fish ” criers in this country have always de.
dared that leather locally prepared is not so well tanned
as the leather (Australian-born in many cases) tanned
abroad. Knowing nothing of the process of tanning, I
can’t say whether the good Australian hides are impro-
perly cured, but I can swear that a lot of the manufac-
tured leather in many sorts of goods is insufficiently
fatted. Leather should be steeped in oil before it is
“ made up.” It will absorb much oil, which is an expens-
ive item in its preparation ; and I have proved that ordin-
ary boot-soles, like cheap leather straps, are sinfully
open to improvement by smearing them again and again
with salad oil. They suck it up as greedily as an anti-
bilious Jew or a sheet of blotting-paper. In short,
they are not good, damp-resisting leather when
they are put on the market, for the simple
reason that they have been stinted of grease
by dishonest dealers. When the soles have been fairly
fed with oil they look more leathery than before,
and become reasonably damp-proof. They are the
genuine article in an improved form—the form in which
it ought to be when the boots appear in a shop-window
as “ For Present Use.” But the soles turned out from
certain factories in Melbourne—the early centre of the
Australian boot trade—are not genuine leather. They
consist of cardboard covered with a composition of
leather filings and glue squeezed into a solid thickness,
and finished off with a black polish. Cheap imported
boots also are suspected of composition soles, and just
now a Trades Hall representative of the Tanners’ Union
in Melbourne is reviving an old discussion of this boot-
trade scandal. Five years ago the Bent Government was
asked to make the vending of bogus leather a punishable
offence under the Pure Foods Act, but the application
was refused. This time something will be done, pre-
sumably. Some test will be legally imposed on the soles
that pretend to be leather. Yet, until the standard of
quality in real leather has been raised, I cannot see that
mock flannel, masquerading as woollen stuff, is more
dangerous to the public health than the boots with
porous soles that are labelled “ For Present Use.”
Leather.
“Hermes”: There is a quaint idea that Aus-
tralian-made leather smells, as a matter of course.
But the fact that some Australian hides tanned
abroad came back perfumed and some didn’t,
long ago knocked out the idea that the odor in
question had anything to do with the hides them-
selves. The trouble was that the work was not
done properly in Australia’s early days, and that
the animal tissues were not completely removed
from the leather. To do this properly meant a
considerable outlay of capital, and the early Aus-
tralian leather-maker was usually a man with a
very small amount of capital behind him, any-
how ; and the bulk of that which he had was mostly
invested in an agency for some foreign leather.
Very few Australian tanners were wholly
tanners; they were generally hybrids, com-
prised of one-third manufacturer and two-thirds
importer. They were, in the mass, opposed to heavy
duties on leather, because such duties would kill
their agencies and lead to the establishment
of leather factories in Australia by Yankee,
English and French firms. The existing system
suited them very well, and they were "doing
nicely on it. Drastic changes, they said, were
odious. But Australia is rapidly stepping into
the foremost place as the world’s storehouse of
raw hides. America is using up all its own supply
and importing. Argentina is beginning to do like-
wise as far as concerns eating up its own supply.
English, French, German and Austrian leather
makers are at their wits’ ends to get raw material,
and Australia is the only country which has it in
large quantities' to spare. If Australia strikes
this trade with a heavy export duty, the hides
will be manufactured here, and some hundreds of
thousands of foreign capital will be spent here in
erecting tanneries, and the population will be in-
creased by an enormous influx of skilled leather
workers. The “ primary industry ” people howl
like a covey of devils about the necessity fcr
encouraging immigration, yet immediately an
export duty that will encourage it on a large
scale is mentioned, they squeal against the im-
position of that duty. The gain to Australia
would be enormous. With Australia suddenly
developed into the world’s centre of the leather
supply, every man on the land would benefit.
Stock-raising would become more profitable than
ever, and provide a good opening for the people
who will be rushing to take advantage of the
result of the progressive land-tax that now looms
on the horizon of things. It would almost cer-
tainly have a beneficial effect on the empty Terri-
tory by supplying a big market right on the
spot for what the Territory can grow in
unlimited numbers even before its railway is
finished—cattle, buffaloes, angoras and marsu-
pials. The difference between exporting the
hides and the manufactured product can be
gauged from the returns for 1908, when the raw
material that went into the Commonwealth tan-
neries was worth £1,654,396, and the leather that
came out had soared up to £2,191,689, or £537,293
more. This industry would be worth fostering
anyhow, but under the exceptional circumstances,
it would be suicidal not to encourage it by the
imposition of a stiff hide-export duty.
Attorney-General Wade, of N.S.W. has won a
law case—the appeal from the verdict of the jury
which awarded £431 damages against the State
for injuries caused to a pupil at a public school.
During a chemistry lesson the headmaster sent
an assistant teacher for a tumbler, but the order
was carried out by a schoolboy, who, finding
a liquid in the glass, threw it (the liquid)
out of a window. Unfortunately, it was sul-
phuric acid, and it landed in the eye of a boy
named Hole, with serious results to his sight.
Hole, senior, sued the Government for damages,
for the obvious reason that if there was any chance
of obtaining a substantial amount it was not from
the careless boy or the schoolmaster who
had allowed him access to the dangerous
goods. The jury found that the master was neg-
ligent, and awarded £431; but the Full Court,
while agreeing with the finding of negligence
against the master, decided that his lapse did not
bind the Government. The appeal was upheld
with costs; but the Court delicately hinted to
Wade that, in the very painful circumstances, the
Government might waive the question of costs.
And, considering the huge pile of costs that Wade
has had to pay in the past, a few more costs
would make no difference worth mentioning.

The N.S.W. Pure Foods Act made a poor show-
ing in a Sydney police court last week, when a
milk distributing co. was summoned for selling
watered milk. An inspector had bailed up a milk
cart, bearing the eo.’s name and the co.’s
manager’s name. The milk was found to be
watered to the extent of 7 per cent. This cart was
identical in appearance with dozens that leave the
co.’s yard daily, yet the defence was that the co.
didn’t sell thecowfruit. The books were produced
to show that the name of the man on the cart was
not the name of a paid employee of the co. He
merely attended daily at the eo.’s premises and
paid £1 Is. each time—lBs. 6d. for milk purchased
and 2s. 6d. for hire of the cart. The manager of
the co. in reply to the magistrate said he had no
objection to carts being hired to irresponsible
men—carts with the company’s name on them.
The Bench held that the driver was not an agent
for the co., and dismissed the case, which is a
poor look-out for the Australian infant who. by
his agent (his ma), patronises the said co. Also,
it is a poor look-out for the Bure Foods Act, if it
can be taken down so easily.

The incredible O’Callaghan, over-age Commis-
sioner of Police for Victoria, being tackled on the
subject of a notorious Melbourne hazard-school,
which is apparently run by a “ fizgig,” and is pre-
sumably a fruitful source of blackmail, diploma-
tised as follows:—
The police can hardly prevent such gatherings from
originating. They can put an end to them sooner or
later, but where the utmost possible precautions are
taken to prevent information regarding them reaching
the police, it is, of course, not easy for the police to get
on their track. Still, it is only a matter of time before
such schools are dispersed.
All of which is quite beside the point that a recent
Argus article on one particular hazard-school
gave a column of information about it to
the public and the police—not that the
latter needed enlightening. Readers of The
Lone Hand will remember how, a year
or two ago, a notorious hazard-schoolmaster
and pal of the police complained, with refreshing
candor, that he was not the most favored law-
breaker in this particular line of misdemeanor.
His “schools,” he said, had been from time to
time broken up, whilst others were flourishing
under the toleration of Melbourne’s precious
police authorities. O’Callaghan’s pretence of
police ignorance re “the existence of a large
hazard-school in a lane off Bourke-street ” was an
audacious insult to public intelligence. And the
attitude of Premier Murray, who is retaining
O’Callaghan in office beyond that discredited
official’s retiring age, is impudently defiant of
public opinion. Had ' Prendergast taken the
re-appointment of O’Callaghan as the text for
his want-of-confidence motion against the Govern-
ment, the supporters of John Weary might have
been shamed into giving him the kick out.

“Silk”: The allegations made by Meagher,
M.L.A., in the N.S.W. Assembly last week con-
cerning the selection of jurors to try Peter Bow-
ling, and the reply (!) of Wade, Premier and
Attorney-General, that he did not know what had
happened, might very properly be made the
subject of an appeal for a new trial. The charge
against Bowling was conspiracy, with a flavor of
sedition in the indictment, and every principle of
justice and law demands that under such indict-
ments the Crown should hold itself as far removed
from the jury as the accused is obliged to do.
Bowling's case comes fully within a sphere of
comparison with the case of Daniel O’Connell, in
Ireland, in 1844, when the Liberator was charged
with conspiracy, with a frilling of sedition.
To make sure of a conviction on that occasion,
the Crown authorities took pains to ascertain the
characters of the jurors and the direction of their
opinions. As a result, the jury was constituted
of men upon whom the Crown (or Government)
could rely to convict. The conviction followed,
and O’Connell was sentenced to 12 months’
imprisonment and fined £2OOO. But information
soon leaked out as to the jury taint, and on appeal
to the House of Lords that tribunal pronounced
the trial a mockery, and quashed the conviction.
O’Connell, after lying 14 weeks in prison, was
accordingly released. My opinion is that the
cases are sufficiently parallel to justify an appeal
for Bowling’s release.

MELBOURNE KING’S.
Frank Gerald as the wicked Captain Clinch.
Cold Tea Bruntnell, after various attempts,
has got the N.S.W. “Liberal” nomination for a
State Assembly seat, and is the Big Drum and
Cold Tea candidate for Annandale, which
M.ahony, the lawyer, gave up as a bad job.
Bruntnell’s political path has been strewn with
broken bottles, so to speak, and when he did
once scrape into the Assembly for Surry Hills
owing to a hopelessly split vote, Parliament dis-
solved as soon as it could after his arrival. He has
tried for nomination in several places, and the
fact that he has been awarded Annandale points
to the supposition that the seat is a rocky propo-
sition for “Liberalism.”
Wisdom and invention are directing
aviation to exalted success. A Zeppelin may go
to pieces in a heavy storm. But half a dozen of
them in a few hours of fine weather could make
things below look very small. Wherefore wisdom
inspired to its keenest vision by D.C.L., the
Whisky of the Wise, perceives that safety against
airships can only be secured by better airships.*

“TRILBY” AT SYDNEY ROYAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Act One—the studio with easels and
Long Taffy getting through some household graft,
A wheezy bellows gasping in his hand
While his blonde weepers flutter in the draught.
Mrs. Vinard—a landlady who shakes
All day with laughter, and resides downstairs—
Is showing red-faced Taffy that she takes
An interest in boarders’ love affairs.
She tells him he’s in love—head over ears—
With fair-haired Trilby. Taffy madly blows,
And begs of her to stop. He greatly fears
The secret will leak out, she so o’erflows.
Madame at last, shrieking with joy, retires ;
The Laird, in borrowed whiskers and a straw
Hat of the same, bright-reddish hue, transpires;
He’s Scottish—of the brand well known as “braw.’'
Later, a stripling about eight-stone-four,
Who seems distraught, comes bounding in to tea ;
His entrance is the signal for a roar—
It is the lightweight lover—young Billee.
He makes the Laird and Taffy both turn green,
And fall into a drab, despairful mood,
By telling them that Trilby he has seen
Posing for other artists “ in th’ nood."
Whilst they are reeling ’neath this shocking blow,
The model Miss arrives to have a yarn ;
They strive to look as if they did not know
Or care a single Nonconformist darn.
But the grim secret later comes to light,
And Trilby, weeping, leaves the studio ;
Whereat the “pig-dogs ” groan as if a blight
Had fallen on their rotten picture show.
And little Bill for Italy departs—
His youthful heart with such resentment burns ;
But he comes rushing back. Two loving hearts
Are fused—for Trilby’s there when he returns.
Act Two—the studio on Christmas Eve ;
High kicking is the order of the day ;
On ev’ry player’s part one can perceive
A stern determination to be gay.
Into the maddest of the revels spins
A black-draped figure, short and rather fat;
’Tis William’s uncle who goes hunting sins
With tearful snuffles and a soup-plate hat.
Bill’s grey-haired mother trembles at the door
Until the revellers are put to flight ;
Then Taffy and the Uncle take the floor,
And argue as to whether it is right
That young Billee, who is a youth well-born,
Should wed the girl on whom he’s wholly “ gone,"
And earn the Truly Good’s glad hoot of scorn
Because his wife once posed “ mid codings on.”
Ere the row ends. Trilby herself comes in ;
To her the mother pleads and wins at length
A promise that she—Trilby—will not sin
By wedding Billy. Taffy prays for strength
To keep from bursting, shows the old pair out,
And calls the festive crowd to gather round ;
The atmosphere is one of gloomy doubt
Until the one and only way is found.
“ Elope, then! ” someone shouts ; “ the Belgian law
Is kinder than the French ! ” To search the street
For carriages, the joyous mob withdraw.
Svengali rises from the window seat.
He is the wireless station. From his eyes
He sends his ethergrams to Trilby’s brain ;
She writes a farewell note ; he bids her rise,
Then leads her out. They don’t come back again.
Five years elapse, and all the same old crew
Are found in ev’ning clobber at a show ;
When Trilby’s mentioned, wistful tears bedew
All eyes, and spirits dwindle down to low.
The famous La Svengali sweeps at last
On to the gauze-dimmed stage and starts to Tril.;
The Laird’s and Taffy’s wonderment is vast,
But greater far the agony of Bill.
Between the numbers the Svengalis rest ;
Young Bill attempts to bring his Trilby round;
Sven, strikes him ; Taffy fiercely drags Sven.’s vest,
And throws him and his whiskers on the ground.
Svengali, whose infernal dynamo
Is quite exhausted, struggles to a chair
And gasps while Trilby busts the stylish show
(The good old wireless is no longer there).
The crowd’s loud hoots arouse Sven, from his trance ;
He staggers up and once more tries to beat
The absent band, whilst in a slow, sad dance
He circles on two large, reluctant feet.
At last he falls—as ail Svengalis must—
Backwards across a table with his head
Hanging some inches from the fine floor-dust.
A very awkward way to drop down dead.
Poor, half-awakened Trilby by the Laird
Is taken from the room ere one can wink ;
One feels relieved that she the sight is spared,
But notices the Laird’s new beard is pink.
Five days go by, and at a good hotel
A rather stronger Trilby with her Bill
Is found most sweetly promising, when well,
To say with due solemnity, “ I will.”
Two encore verses of “ Ben Bolt” she sang ;
Then fell and died. To her young corse so white
Young Billy rushed and sobbed. I said, “ Oh, hang !
That is the third good cry he’s had to-night."

After a lapse of several years, ‘‘Trilby
was revived on Saturday last by Nellie Stewart
and Co. A houseful of people came along
t i the revival —some of them to watch the
experiment and compare the Australian star s
methods with those of Edith Crane and Nance
O’Neill; others to make the acquaintance of the
drama itself,.which seems to have developed into
a theatrical evergreen. The play is capably pro-
duced for the new firm of J. C. Williamson,
Ltd., by Gaston Mervale, who plays Svengali.
The Mervale production departs from the usual
setting in the third act, because, on a curtain
being drawn aside, Trilby and Svengali can
actually be seen performing. the
audience had only the applause ‘ off
and the distant warbling of ‘“Ben Bolt
to go upon; the rest had to be imagined.
Miss Stewart, as Trilby, has in an amaz-
ing degree the freshness of youth ; but her
acting in the first of the two studio scenes, on the
opening night at any rate, though finished, was
lacking in joyousness. As the play progressed
and the Trilby horizon grew darker, she took
hold better, and at the end of the second act she
had both the part and the house well in hand.
Nellie Stewart, when she is pathetic, would drag
sympathy out of a stone; and as the last two
acts of “Trilby” are rather more sorrowful
than the average, she scores heavily to the
end. Then she has to resurrect herself from
the death-couch to bow her acknowledg-
ments and gather a harvest of flowers. Gas
ton Mervale’s Svengali is a finished portrait,
heavily retouched and painstakingly horrible
down to the last detail. Harcourt Beatty, in his
new Scotch accent and a light beard, makes a
cheerful and, at times, hilarious Laird ; and
Eardley Turner acts with life-like restraint and
heaviness as Taffy. A. L. Pearce, the Little
Billee of the company, is emotional with reluct-
ance ; and this paper doesn’t blame him for it.
He-is sufficiently ardent in the love scenes, and
altogether his distressful row is hoed with com-
mendable evenness. J. B. Atholwood, as the
Rev. Bagot, plays in a spirit of broad comedy, and
does it excellently. Gregan McMahon makes a
rather coldly sober Zou-Zou ; and A. J. Patrick,
as Dodor, is colorless ; while Norman Campbell, as
Colonel Kaw, talks through his nose harder than
he need. Madeleine Meredith, who plays Mrs.
Bagot, is quietly ladylike and limp; and Rossl.yn
Vane is a satisfactory Mrs. Vinard, except when
she laughs too long at Taffy in the first act.

“The Brass Bottle” is being opened
nightly at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), amid mur-
murs of awe. All students of Holy Writ who
yearn to realise the exact sort of Green Jinn it
was who exasperated the late Solomon—he of the
Proverbs and the polygamous ways—are accom-
modated in the most entertaining fashion at the
Pitt-street playhouse.
** m *
The spruce Besses o’ th’ Barn came trium-
phantly back to Sydney last Thursday and piled
their curled and glittering brass instruments on
the platform of the Town Hall beneath an
immense striped canopy that hid the mute and
inglorious organ. The first six or seven rows of
floor seats were occupied by a solid wall of uni-
formed local bandsmen who had come to admire
and drink in the genuine old ’54 port of melody
that the Besses cause to flow from their
golden-toned cornets, trombones and horns.
Under the baton of Mr. Chris Smith, who has
temporarily taken up the duties of conductor
Owen, tiie skilled instrumentalists played
through an impressive programme which began
with Meyerbeer’s “Schiller” March, and in-
cluded a solo each for the euphonium and cornet
and one for the.xylophone. The amazing purity
and richness of tone attained in the softer passages
occasionally deluded the ear into the belief that
the instruments were really made of 18-carat
material, till the sharp clash of the cymbals or the
electric rattle Of the kettledrums broke the spell.
Sometimes the flowing music in its volume re-
sembled the Snowy at sunset; at others it rippled
like a mountain Creek ; and when it swelled and
crashed it thundered majestically like surf. But
it was always soothing, with a deep mellowness
compounded of exquisite skill and incessant prac-
tice. It is the unofficial but glorious mission of
the Besses to go about the earth educating the
rank-and-file brass bandsman and the public
generally to the fact that the cornet and its
shining brothers, when blown with art, will coo
as gently as doves, send top-notes, sweet and clear
as a tenor’s soaring to the roof, and soothe the
savage beast who pays. The Sydney season is an-
nounced to conclude on Saturday night.

** * *
There was some fine music at the Lieder-
tafel concert, held at Sydney Town Hall on
Wednesday evening, and the date being near
enough to what would have been the one hun-
dredth birthday of Schumann, were that once-
adulated and now somewhat old-fashioned com-
poser still composing, the occasion was made a
Schumann concert. L. Godfrey Smith at the
piano, E. Mowat Carter leading the orchestra,
and Arundel Orchard as conductor were the chief
bigwigs in a programme which bristled with the
names of our music-making aristocracy. After the
concerto (the big item of the list) the audience
grew most excited, and signalled its enthusiastic
approval of Godfrey Smith’s piano-playing by
making that already hard-worked person come
back and play some more. G. Smith should take
care of himself, because good men like him are
very scarce. He plays the right things in the
right way., Schumann doesn’t matter quite
as much as he did when most people
who called themselves musical, in mid-Vic-
torian days, were thinking him rather awfully
advanced. He became the fashion, and not
to admire Schumann was to be counted right
out in musical circles. But now he has been a bit
outclassed, though of course he has his niche. He
belongs to the Carlyle-Murger-Thackeray period,
andif the young musician hasn’t already made his
acquaintance, Schumann may safely be skipped,
with merely a passing nod. Miss Mylor Burge
sang “ The Shadow Song ” in a trilling soprano,
and was encored. The concert, taking it by and
large, was one, of the best.
** * *
“D.” was among those present:—
I \fent to Mr. McCabe’s lecture on “Spain and the
Martyrdom of Ferrer” last week, prepared, as it were, to
pray. (I was twice in Spain, once for nearly three
months, and it impressed me on both occasions as an in-
sanitary, bathless, flea-haunted, and generally uncivilised
land that merited the worst a lecturer could hand it.) I
stayed to scoff. There seems to be some occult influence
hovering around all sectarian discussions that impels the
controversialists to go in for the cheapest variety of
Bilandersonian claptrap. To my grief, McCabe started
his discourse this way
“My desire to return to you has been accelerated by
one Sydney gentleman who has written that I shall not
leave your Commonwealth alive, because I have lectured
on Ferrer and the martyrdom of Ferrer.”
The name of the alleged homicidal maniac wasn t
specified. .It never is. No details were given of
where, or when he wrote his murderous threat, so
that the authorities might take steps to iden-
tify him and run him in, and thereby vindicate
the reputation of Australia as a land which, even if
it is afflicted with Joe Cook, Land Monopoly and Debt, is
progressive enough withal to chastise criminals who
lightly threaten harmless visitors with assassination.
When some old Dill accuses Tamworth, or some other
inoffensive N.S.W. town, of discharging firearms at him,
and blandly produces a riddled kerosene tin by way of
proof, we understand and, smiling tolerantly, pass on.
But we expect one of McCabe’s mental calibre to be
different.
There was a depressing amount of blood, mystery and
melodrama during the evening. For instance, McCabe
told how a young SDanish officer “ braved the anger of
the R.C. Church” and defended Ferrer. “From that
day to this,” said the lecturer, amid shudders, “he has
not been heard of. What has happened? I forbear to
say. I leave it to vou.” Another military defender
of Ferrer lasted three weeks, it appeared, and then
blew h' s brains out. “ Why do I not go to Spain?” in-
quired Mr. McCabe. “Ahha ! You may possibly guess.
In any case, the people whom. I would have liked to meet
are ail dead-r-slain by the Romish Church.” Followed
some marrow-freezing details of dungeons on the Spanish
coast, in whose recesses innocent, well-meaning people
are immured till they get consumption, or are shot, or
hanged—all at the instance of the Church. “How did
Ferrer die?” perorated the lecturer. “Muttering to a
priest? No ; with his face to the soldiers’ rifles." Which,
after all, is the way people mostly do die who have been
sentenced to be shot, and have failed either to escape
or to dissuade their captors from pursuing the unpleasant
business to a finish.
The audience was, of course, of the militantly wowser
variety, and McCabe seemed to argue down to its intelli-
gence. Amid cheers he remarked that 68 per cent, of
Spaniards couldn’t read or write, and referred doubters
to “ the text books.” Certainly the text-books note that
the last time the Census officers concerned themselves
with this question 68 per cent, of Spaniards neither read
nor wrote. But that was in 1889 when education was in
a bad way elsewhere than in Spain. To-day, in Spain.
(vide “Statesman’s Year Book,” 1909) there are 26,000
public schools (attended by 2,000,000 pupils), and 6000
private schools (attended by 350,000 pupils), and the
population of the country is only 20,000,000. There are
no recent illiteracy figures; but, taking their schooling
facilities into consideration, Spaniards would need to be
a demented race to stand, educationally speaking, where
they did 21 years ago.
But what futile, resultless, time-wasting performances
these sectarian outbursts seem, anyhow,, when they are
engaged in by men capable of useful work. Most of
McCabe’s remarks, last week, dealt with the moral de-
pravity of Europe between 500 A.D. and 1500 a.d., alleg-
edly as a result of the existence of the R.C. Church.
Yet, during that period, the R.C. Church and the Greek
Church divided most of Europe pretty equally, and what
they didn’t divide was plain heathen. As an instance, it
was as late as 1225 when measures were taken for the
conversion of the Prussian idolators. And an. inspection
of the results suggests that every church is just as bad
as its period, and as bad as the local civilisation around
it. The nation raises itself or more or less agnostic
statesmen who are free from clerical influences raise
it, and it drags the church up with it. Or else
it sinks, and the church slides down with it. It
wasn’t the R.C. Church or the Greek Church or the
bogus gods of Prussia that made Europe what it was in
the 12th century ; it was a wretched system of govern-
ment which made the churches and the wooden gods
what they were. And they were a very bad lot.

The Dardinis and their crockery-smash act
are to drop off Sydney Tivoli programme after
the performance on Wednesday evening, some
hours before this Bulletin perches itself on the
bookstalls for sale. Prophecy is a precarious
animal, so this scribe hesitates to thrust his pen
into the future and write in cold ink that they
actually dropped. The week-end show, from pre-
sent indications, will consist almost entirely of
people who have received more or less honorable
mention several times before—the Charlenes,
Powell and Vesey, De Lyle and Brunin, Frank
Travis, and the dancing McLeans, who still hang
on to their old unwise signboard—“the Australian
Dartos.” Further consideration of comedian Fred
Keeton and his painful song about the half-witted
youth impels one to ask if the grimaces and facial
contortions are absolutely essential. In the
Writer’s opinion the ditty would be quite shudder-
some enough without the singer’s “business.”
“THE ARCA-
DIANkS,” AT
SYDNEY
CRITERION.
Essie Perrin
in Arcadia.
The danger of fooling around with gipsy
girls when one is in a good billet is borne out by
the Pathe Freres film at West’s Palace show,
wherein the narrow escape of Napoleon 111. is
shown. Nap., it seems, was a gay dog who
recked little whose preserves he poached upon.
West’s are also showing a series in connec-
tion with the Richmond disaster. An In-
dian drama, which reveals what may
happen to a man of the conquered race
who happens to fall in love with a woman of
the conquering race, is dramatically revealed. It
also shows what happens to the rival of the con-
quering race. He gets bitten by a snake
left in his lady love’s bag. The Hindu
gets shot, and the way that the dead
body of the “snaker” is carried across the
curtained doorway is a most artistic touch.
West's have now taken to a sort of weekly sum-
mary of the notable events in Sydney, and
last week’s little lot showed the Boy Scouts
at the Centennial Park, the iriter-State hockey
match, and a few other trifles. This draws
Society, which gazes intently to catch its .own
phiz on the same film as that of the Governor.
Mr. Higgins, Charles Spencer’s chief
operator, was travelling in the train that was
smashed up at Richmond (Vic.) and had his tools
with him. Scrambling out of the wrecked train, he
upended his machine and leaned heavily on the
legs of it while he whirled the handle taking
views of the efforts of the rescuers to recover the
mangled. This dreadful series is now running at
the Lyceum, much to the disgust of all railway
men. “Oliver Twist,” staged by a French co., is
at present chasing the Richmond horror across the
fleeting screen, and, as is usual With French
actors, the facial studies are a sight for the gods
and the artistic. The advisability of always be-
friending pretty women in distress is driven home
by a series, which shows how the girl who
was late for rehearsal through attending to her
sick mother, was saved by the intercession of
the leading man. Later on, when she is a star
and making millions with her own company, she
finds the former benefactor in a mighty bad way,
visits him in his dingy quarters where he lies at
death’s door, and puts her arm round his neck.
It was a long while to wait for the arm, but he
got it in time. As said before, always act the
good Samaritan to the pretty girl in distress.
** * *
“Aladdin ” has left Adelaide Royal affcer-
an enthusiastic farewell evening. Some of the
jokes with which the piece started last Christmas
—concerning Reid’s departure, for instance, or a
certain Strike—are grown up now and have to
shave daily ; but these are only small woes at the
worst. The chorus-ladies have one great chance
when they parade as savages, each in a plain
brown garment with her hair down. To the male
eye, at any rate, they look more charming then
than in all their elaborate costumes; but women
will never appreciate that simple truth. “The
Night of the Party” is the present bill at the
Royal. It is somewhat too English for Australian
tastes. In England there are just three classes—
one that employs a crowd of domestics, a second
that supplies the crowd, the third (in between,
and chief supporters of the drama) that can’t afford
such a crowd, but vaguely hopes to be able to
some day, and meanwh'le loves to hear about the
few that can. Billy Williams has left the Tivoli,
taking his Wilson Barrett figure and his confiden-
tial ways with him. Many—women especially—
get enthusiastic over him ; but people who don’t
like him use the most injurious language concern-
ing his turn. The strenuous young person calling
herself Lillie Langtry has been singing and dan-
cing ; but the charming Olga Grey has wisely
moved on, just as she was tending to monotony.
Fred Bluett, at this house, has been giving a song
that is either innocent (and very nearly meaning-
less in that case) or guilty (and in that case alarm-
ing). The singer himself turns on the innocent
stop, and gets through without offence ; but it is
as well to keep the ambiguous off the stage. One
Morris Cronin has just stepped off the steamer
into the Tivoli, and his uncommon juggling gives
the programme a needed “ snap.” Also, Calvh is
at the Exhibition, and Joseph McCabe is in
various places, and the moving picture is every-
where.
** * *
The first Melbourne performance of
“Butterfly” at Her Majesty’s seemed to con-
vince a much-expecting audience that the orches-
tration of Puccini’s famous opera, which is the
first consideration, was exceptionally melodious
and expressive, and was, moreover, treated as
fully by conductor Hazon’s forces as any audience
could expect at the prices charged. The feast of
music was ample and the staging admirable. Only
from a dramatic, critical point of view was the
general effect unsatisfactory. The heroine rarely
suggested a 15-year-old Jap girl. The alleged
American naval lieutenant, who made belief
to marry her, was a plump, soft, curly,
unemotional hero, who never gripped one’s
attention; and the middle-aged paternal con-
sul, who should have bound the audience’s
sympathies to the deceived “wife” by seem-
ing sincerely sorry for the 'misalliance was
presented in a dry inflexible manner by Arthur
Crane, a good enough baritone with a thin person-
ality. There were plump people in the light and
irresponsible characters, and a thin young man in
the heavy father part. For the rest, Miss Buck-
mann, Edward Wynn and Ferguson filled their
minor places creditably ; whilst the lady choristers
wore Australian faces above their kimonos. No
Jap-cum-Yankee atmosphere was created by the
three leading performers in the story or by the
women attendants of Butterfly, whose introductory
chorus is the longest sustained “ theme,” or what
not, in the opera. The orchestral music, wherein
the voices are mostly so submerged that the Eng-
lish translation of the words becomes a clumsy
farce, gives all the atmosphere and most of the
human feeling to the work. It is, however, never
so full of feeling as in its nearest approach to
silent effect at the close of the second act, where
Butterfly’s maid and child grow weary of watch-
ing through the eyelet-holes in the paper win-
dows, and leave her to a lonely vigil.
Puccini is quite a Shakspeare among the
new school of broken-melody composers, but he
hasn’t the wide range of harsh, old Wagner. His
ingenuity in expressing all things sweetly,
save the tragic denouement, is as remarkable as
his audacity in taking whatever he wants
from popular airs. He cribs a bit of “ Star
Spangled Banner ” to emphasise the presence of
the Yankee Consul, and draws on “The Mikado”
from time to time, especially when the serio-
comic Prince happens along, with (presumably) a
proposal to extricate Butterfly from her pitiable
state of grass-widowhood. Yet in Puccini’s
saccharine and poetic music there is perhaps a
sameness that palls somewhat on the ear. This
opera revives memories of the lovely duet between
Mimi and Rudolph in “La Boheme.” As a tone
drama, it is more completely harmonious and
cohesive, but it lacks the novelty of “La
Boheme” of yore. The needless transla-
tion into English is particularly unsuited
to Miss Bel Sorel, the heroine of last Satur-
day’s performance. Her accent is French,
her singing method Italian, and her natural
vivacity refuses to be Japped out of recognition.
Her acting and appearance suggest Blanche
Arral tempered by Tittell Brune ; and, although
she plays and sings the part artistically, she never
appears thoroughly dominated by it. Blamires,
the tenor lover with a non-incisive voice, is hardly
more successful than Crane, as the important
Consul. To-night (Thursday)and again on Satur-
day, another pair of lovers will be provided in
Miss Amy Castles and John Zerga.
At Melbourne Royal the sinful inmates of
Mrs. Sharpe’s boarding-house continue to be
chastened, one at a time, by mysterious Matheson
Lang. The city is not unanimous in accepting
the Stranger and his influences as an impressive
object-lesson. Some regard him as a professional
hypnotist dealing with a round of subjects who
return to their normal condition after the show.
What strikes the present writer forcibly is the
maintenance of class distinction in the Stranger’s
conversions. Certainly the waster Majah goes
commercial-travelling as the result of the’new
influence, but the Jew still refuses to work
for his income. He merely advertises his
bogus silver mine as a bona-fide dairy farm,
and gets a promoter’s profits under another
heading. And Miss Britton, as the slavey,
becomes only a humble toiler with a cough,
instead of a healthy, hard-working sinner. She
gains nothing but a nasty chest complaint and a
lovely Tory faith in the goodness of the social
system, and she is patronised accordingly by the
other Christians. When they talk about her
health she says: “Anyone would think I was a
bed-ridden old woman past her work. I ain’t got
no korf.” And she goes away to scrub harder
than ever. What a lesson to Australian domes-
tics is “ The Passing of the Third Floor Back ” !
With a view to promulgating the doctrine of hard
work for slaveys and easy Christianity for lodgers,
the management is inviting local parsons to
attend matinees gratis on presentation of their
cards. At these times the free-pass pressman
feels drawn to the Church by a bond of mutual
interest, as it were.
** * *
“The Chance of a Lifetime” is now
putting in its third week at the King’s, but has
more nights to gallop ere it reaches the finishing
post. Meanwhile, the crowd on the far side of
the racecourse preserves the same unconcern as
before. Nothing can shake the stolidity of a
scene-painter’s crowd. The people just stand
there like a Chamber of Horrors, and the fact that
their average height is Bft. or so worries them
not at all.
* * *
In their third concert at Melbourne Town
Hall (last Saturday) the Vic. Professional Orches-
trians went one, or even two, better than their
previous best. The first item was the “Pathetic”
Symphony of the deceased Russian genius whose
name belied his work, because it was something
to sneeze at. Such things must be well done to
sound reasonable, and this performance was
powerfully reasonable, and, in many parts of it,
delightful. The “Carnaval Romain” Overture
of Berlioz was spiritedly played ; likewise the
catchy numbers in the “ Nut Cracker Suite.” The
orchestra gave a fine accompaniment to Seg-
mund’s love song from “ The Valkyrie,” sung by
Oswald Rippon in pure tone with the unruffled
serenity of a seraph in specs., and the programme
ended with the overture to “ Meistersinger.”
This didn’t seem to come up to the printed de-
scription of its Wagnerian meaning, but that pro-
bably was because it came after five o’clock. No
matinee concert should try to impress an audi-
ence after 5 p.m. Even a football match doesn’t
presume so much on public enthusiasm. The
next Vic. Pro. Orch. concert is fixed for Satur-
day, August 27, and the brethren have invited
Slapoffski to trip over to Melbourne to conduct
it. When will they get Government permission to
preach their gospel to paying congregations on
the blessed Sabbaths ?
** * *
“St. Elmo,” the old maid’s Bible and the
young maid’s rapture, is now presented as a. pic-
ture at Melbourne Olympia. Though refreshingly
brief, it retains much of the novel’s starchy piety.
Of the other items, “The Ranger’s Bride” is a
mirthful episode, while the absurdities of “ A
Smoked Husband ” should divert the morbid and
sweeten the sour. There is a cheerful wildness
about “The New Sign of the Globe Hotel ”; and,
as a counterpoise, “ The Two Brothers” is a tine
contribution to the “ preachy ” branch of fiction.
Shortly stated, it is the story of a horrible boozer,
who leaves the sin of the pewter for the peace of
the pew.
* * *- *
Tails’ programme at Melbourne Athen-
seum this week is wholly devoted to vehement
tales. “ Davy Crockett ” is the best story, and
shows how Davy wins a wife by cunning and
energy. As for the other pictures, the humor of
“ Tombolini in Love” is rough as compared
with the satire of “ Baby’s First Tooth.”
Exceedingly fatuous are the exploits of “Absent-
Minded Max,” while the yarn of “The Little
Mother ” is a more artistic item than “ Saved by
a Dog,” And, really, this last picture makes
human credulity throw in the towel. It ex-
hibits a dog that can apparently tow a boat and
two people over the waters of a tumbled bay.
** * *
At Melbourne Opera House, the bill is a
passable copy of last week’s document. The
Kremos are still the strongest attractions, but
much ecstasy is aroused by Bert Terrell, who
looks like the apotheosis of a “ Willie Boy.” Of
some others, Mendel continues to introduce his
disturbing keeper ; Fanny Powers is repeating
her imitations; while Billy Williams has returned
with his snigger and his giggle.
(For “Poverty Point ” see pages SO and 53.)
The International Harvester Co. tells
another story concerning some of its huge finan-
cial operations reported lately in the Aegus and
mentioned in The Bulletin (14/7/TO). The co.’s
Stock is 120 million dollars, half 7 per cent, pre-
ference and the other half ordinary. For four
years after the reconstruction of the co. the
ordinaries got no dividend, as the directors
reckoned that they would be wanting the money.
They did want it, and put it in the business ; and
at the end of the four years they issued to the
ordinary shareholders stock representing this
accumulated profit to the extent of 33J per cent, —
equal to an annual div. of 8 per cent, and a frac-
tion. There was, therefore, more than “water ’’
behind the new issue ; there was the profit accu-
mulated in the four years in which no div. was
paid. Though the International people don’t put
it that way, it was practically a bail-up of the
ordinary people for a third more capital.

would be glad if you will kindly make inquiries with a
view to locating it.

General, in moving a vote of thanks to “Major ’’

Army outburst, seized his opportunity and spoke
a piece as to the “ social evil.” In the opinion of

suburb where—(Order ! Chair! Or -der!) . . .
Well, anyhow, there are—(Uproar, and “Throw
: ’im out!” Thrown out according!}').

* * * *
The Rev. T. S. B. Woodfull, discoursing
at Bendigo on the Richmond railway catastrophe,
said : “ Why that fog? Why did God allow it to
come? I don’t think these things happen
casually. I believe the disaster was a message to
Victoria.” From subsequent remarks of his we
gather that the smashing of two trains, the kill-
ing of nine people and the great damage of many
others was prooably intended as a warning from
Heaven against the policy of the Railway Com-
missioners in running Sunday trains. Provi-
dence, it seems, wrecks trains on Monday as a
protest against trains on Sunday ; and because
some people travel on the Day of Rest it
butchers some other people when the Day
of Rest is over. Truly, the parson makes us
tired ; likewise, he gives us cold feet. He has a
steady theory that the Almighty is more muffled
in His language than even a hawker with an im-
pediment in his speech. Though He could tell
His purpose clearly it seems that, out of pure
cussedness, He won’t do it, but simply kills a lot
of people and leaves the community to guess why
He did it, and howto prevent Him killing a lot
more. This arrangement may give the parson a
steady job as interpreter; but it seems a pretty
rough thing to say about God. And one begins
to feel doubtful about the interpreter when one
notes his lamentable tendency to decide that
Providence always meant the same thing that he
meant himself.
* * * *
Melbourne’s mortal fear of its railways
was shown on Tuesday night last week. On that
evening a cold, thick fog descended and wrapped
itself round the city of Dreadful Smash, and
people on their way to the railway station halted
and looked at each other with alarmed faces.
Was it “good enough” to get into a railway
train? “Not good enough,” they decided, for
you couldn’t see half-way across the narrowest
streets, and whoever travelled in a train was
haunted by the vision of a hostile engine pouncing
on him from behind. Those who could afford it
called hansoms, and so risked the Cabby’s neck as
well as their own ; others chose the trams, which
went like noisy wraiths, with banging bells and
bellowing gripmen, through the blind, damp veil.
Many who lived within a mile or two of the city
groped their ways homeward in the mist, and
sneezed and coughed for two days aftewards. It
was all very illogical, and very understandable,
for Melbourne has a dreadful funk. The crews
of a fleet could have had a healthy jamboree with
the ticket-money the Victorian railways lost on
Tuesday night.
* * * *
The Outback can still breed some true
mates. One of them was heard of at Parkes
(N.8.W.) the other day. With another man—a
good deal older than himself—he had tramped
into Forbes looking for work. On the way the
older man’s boots gave out, so the mate bought
him a pair, and then had only a few shillings left.
They didn’t get the work they were after, so they
decided to give their feet a rest and take the rail
to Parkes. The older man’s fare was fixed up all
right; but the young one quietly took a ticket
as far as his money would stretch, and then iwith
a breezy “So long!” he got out and walked.
The older man rode on ; but bad luck had got him
down, and when his mate turned up at Parkes he
was a corpse. The Coroner’s Court said it was
suicide on the part of one, and mighty fine and
generous behavior on the part of the other ; and
witnesses and others insisted on dropping their
fees and some odd coins into the white man’s
empty hat.

“10-2-4”; The “last wishes” of James
Ralli, a Paris artist, who died last month, ap-
pealed to me. At the end of his will he said :
Let me be placed in my coffin as soon after my death
as is possible, and let nobody be let into m3' room before
lam put in my coffin. Let no picture be made of my
body', and let me be buried in the shortest time possible.
And do not weep for me. I have lived a happ3’ enough
life. .. . And I should be thoroughly pleased if no one
wore marks of mourning for me. j
To me, one of the main terrors of death
has always been the knowledge that when
I am an icy and horrible corpse those
whom I have liked best in life, and
whose kindly remembrances I most
crave, will be driven by an idiotic
custom to come and visit me. (Of
course I may get drowned at
sea, or blown to pieces, or
incinerated, or eaten by some-
thing or someone, or hanged,
in which case my friends
will be spared a post-mor-
tem view of me ; but I am
assuming my end to be
normal.) The scent of
the flowers, whose
hideous significance
is missed by so
many, won’t avail to
drown the marrow-
freezing odor of
death; the only
effect will be to make
flower-lovers loathe
certain blooms till
the recollection of
the conditions under
which they last saw
them shall have
faded away. In my
opinion, none bu t
doctors, nurses and
undertakers should
be suffered to con-
sort with corpses,
and even they
shouldn’t be allowed
a moment longer with
the remains than is
necessary to identify
them as genuine and
pass them on to the hone-yard. The last lingering
interview with the cadaver is an affliction to
those who indulge in it—whatever they may
hypocritically allege to the contrary. It doesn’t
gratify the corpse, and, as above mentioned, to
many of us who are prospective corpses it consti-
tutes a very real unpleasantness.

* * * *
The British New Guinea Co.—the big
over-capitalised concern, which paid out an un-
necessary third of a million to promoters and
vendors—has started off just as that kind of co.
might be expected to do. It bought or chartered
—apparently bought—a steamer, the Bentinck,
which turned up at Port Moresby the other day,
laden with foreign goods, clerks, and other alleged
necessaries for the proposed rubber plantations.
Soon after the ship had dropped anchor the hands
mostly went off to see what Papua was like;
and after they had priced a few things
and made an inquiry or two about the
local rates of wages, eight of them made
vigorous remarks about the pay at which they
had been induced to sign on. Also the eight
decided that the best time to kick was the imme-
diate present, and they kicked accordingly. Of
course, they expected to be run in, and they
weren’t disappointed; and, at latest advices,
they were more or less cheerfully doing the 14
days that had been allotted to them. Port
Moresby had viewed the big co. with a lot of sus-
picion before; now the suspicion is deepening
into scorn.

A DREADFUL DITTY.
[FOR THE BULLET IN.]
It, is proposed to ask on tlie census-paper, “ Do you
habitually use alcoholic liquor as a beverage?”—
Argus, 26/7/10.
Can 3-0 U see the census-taker standing firmly on the mat
In a suitof serious pattern and a sober sort of hat,
With his fingers in his whiskers, asking questions cool
and calm.
Of the housewife and her attitude regarding liquid balm ?
“ Tell me, missus, are 3-ou frequently or ever, on your ear ?
Are 3*ou gay and loud and jo3'ful when the bloom is on
the beer?
Do you take it as a tonic, or a luxury or what?
Is your beer-imbibing chronic?
Do you swill, or do you not ! ”
Is the census-taker likely to retain his equipoise
If his bold and pointed questioning the householder
annoys?
Do yon think he should continue when his quarrv, now at
bay,
Grips the door-post as he queries in his too-persistent
way ?
“ Do you never lift the Bottle with a firm, unshaking fist,
Or observe square gin as sugar on your grocer’s weekly'
list?
Do y'ou wander ivhere the beer-leaves grow, or take a
parlor tot ?
Please inform me, do you ever go
Upon the burst or not?”
Do you note the census-taker’s eager, frightened, up-
ward look
As he writes all sorts of figures in his census-taking book ?
Do you notice how he shivers in his plain, unvarnished
shoes,
As he tries to land the lady' in his census-taking noose?
“ Would you tell me, do you soak all day, or only off and
on ?
Do you mix your drinks with water? Do you house a
demijohn?
Do you ever run the rabbit? Do you like it cold, or hot ?
Have you got the sipping habit?
Are you sober, please, or not?”
I can see the census-taker bolting madly for the gate,
With his whiskers waving wildly, in a far from placid
state !
I can hear his hurried hoofbeats grinding down the
gravel-track,
As the perfect temperance lady slings the fire-irons at
his back.
Still he tries to do his duty, fleeing fast, and full of pace—
Can you catch his broken accents as he bolts to save his
face?
“ Pray, madam, do not look upon my questionings as
rot!
Do you shicker very often,
Are you tanked, or are you not ? ”
victoria.

Breathing Disease.
Infectious diseases are breathed into
the system from those affected with disease
or from bad smells; yet how many women
breathe daily the offensive steam from
common soaps made from rancid fats, and
keep their hands for hours in such solu-
tions, and the clothing from such soap
suds is worn next the tender skin. No
wonder disease and eczema are prevalent!
Users of Sunlight Soap know the differ-
ence between that and the pure, healthful
smell from the vegetable oils and pure
edible fats in Sunlight Soap.

“ The Heathen ” : I take this opportunity
of thanking Heaven that Bishop Frodsham, of
North Queensland, has fired a broadside into im-
ported Archbishop Wright over the interminable
row about the Costumes. Mr. Wright, who
comes from a cold country where people take all
sorts of duds seriously, is prepared to argue about
them at great length ; but Frodsham, who labors
in a hot casual corner of the Vineyard, says he
doesn’t care how much official Clobber his men
put on or take off so long as they work steadily
at keeping the phylloxera of Sin off the plants.
Frodsham’s splendid indifference to the Rags of
Contention is a refreshing affair. I’m sorry,
though, that the Townsville pressman who asked
him for his opinion about the vestments didn’t
tackle him also on the subject of candles. A
busy, tropical bishop of the Frodsham type could
have said something memorable about hot, un-
necessary things like candles. Personally, I
think the vestment and candle question would be
solved by sending all the Anglican clergy to
Bourke in the height of the summer and forcing
them to preach the Gospel to one another for
eight hours a day. By the end of that time the
pulpit costume would be a light bathing suit, and
the verger who attempted to set fire to a candle
would fall ’neath a volley of hymn-books.

“The Shover ”: The Richmond (Vic.)
railway wreck has produced the customary crop
of newspaper letters. Per-
haps the most disagreeable of
these was that of an anony-
mous writer to the Argus,
who grunted indignantly
concerning the improper
action of sufferers from shock
in putting forward claims
for cash—actually for hard
cash—when there were more
deserving people with
broken bones to distribute
John Weary’s money
amongst. This letter was
endorsed by another screed
from a Remarkable Lady
who wrote: “It seems a
most extraordinary thing
that people away in the
front of the first trains hould
lodge claims for shock or
spinal complaint, when those
in the rear, where my hus-
band was, should not feel any
ill effects. Although he lost
his hat, and had his clothes
torn in his efforts to relieve
those who were not so for-
tunate as he, there was no mur-
mur of complaint from him, as
he fully recognised that he was
well paid by escaping scot free.”
Well, I know of a Sunshine case
in which the victim was appar-
ently undamaged by the collision,
was able to walk home (from
Sunshine to a southern suburb)
after the accident, and, after a
sleep, went out to do riding work
on the Caulfield racecourse.
This young man sent in no claim
for damages ; but, no less than
12 months later, he began to
suffer from delayed shock, and
his doctors’ bills were con-
siderably more than the
price of a lost hat and a
damaged coat-tail. And
la3t Saturday the papers
reported the death of a
man who was injured in
the Sunshine accident,
and who just crumpled
up and died when his
reoovery was appar-
ently complete. The
man who lost his hat
in the Richmond hor-
ror and left his weskit
impaled on a hat-rack,
ought to restrain his wife from writing foolish
letters. She may be a lone widder, earning her
scanty crust for herself, within 12 months’ time
.'olely by reason of that accident.
The “ Old Soldier ” writes :
It is bard to keep a straight face when reading a paper
in the Fortnightly Review for June by “ S. M. Mitra,’
who ought, judging by his name, to be a high Brahmin,
one of the class which is at the bottom of all the present
Indian rumpus. His idea is that, while Britain is reform-
ing its House of Fords, it cannot do better than call up
some of the alleged “Indian Princes” to sit in it, and
this is why :
“ No one can better explain the thoughts of the Indian
millions than their natural rulers, the Indian Princes.
For, through confidence, the latter know the innermost
thoughts of the former.”
“ Natural rulers ” is good. There is only one native
state in India 200 years old—that of Hyderabad—and the
Nizam is a Turk without a single natural tie to bind him
to his people. He is a Muhamadan, and a foreigner
governing a population of Hindus, to whom he is an un-
clean beast. All India was in the melting-pot during the
Mahratta war, 107 years ago, and all the other States
came into existence since then. As an example, the
great state of Kashmir, with its solid Muhamadan popu-
lation, was sold bodily in 1850 for cash by John Company
to old Gulab Sing of Jamu. In 1825 Amir Khan, an
Afghan chief of mercenaries, or bandits, or whatever you
like to call them, took possession of the native State of
Tonk, and his descendants are Nawabs there to this day.
Here is a hint dropped by Mitra that might be worth
considering by the British Government: —
“The Indian Princes can work through religious influ-
ences, which operate speedily and effectively on the
minds of the masses ; such influences can be put in mo-
tion by the priestly class at the will of an Indian Prince.
And this might be done secretly without the knowledge
of the British Government, who have been before now
taken by surprise.”
It reads mighty' like a threat.
* * * *
For 20 minutes, on July 12 last, Frederick
King, of Alexandria(Sydney), beat the horse he had
charge of with the butt end of a whip ; and part of
the time he belabored the wretched animal about
the head. He was proceeded against under the
Traffic Act last week, and fined £lO, plus costs
and witnesses’ expenses, in default two months’
hard labor. On his asking 14 days to pay, the
magistrate said :
I’d give you double that time in gaol if I could do so.
You are a coward to treat a dumb animal like that. I’ll
certainly give you no time to pay.
King got the maximum penalty allowed by the
law, which was all to the good, but the penalty
isn’t nearly big enough. Any day in the streets
where carters congregate, the spectacle is offered
of horses having their heavily-bitted jaws torn at,
or their faces lashed or punched, or their
stomachs kicked. Anywhere about the city a
horse has to be almost larne enough to lie down
before the police interview the driver. Galled
shoulders and sore backs are common ; and hardly
anyone gets called to account for them, for the
local John Hop knows little about horses, and,
anyhow, is largely occupied raiding fan-tan
establishments. If a private citizen interferes he
gets no satisfaction and a heap of blasphemy from
the horse-torturer, and the minimum of public
sympathy. Sydney would be a more edifying
place and a pleasanter one to live in if people
who were convicted of needlessly ill-treating
animals could be handed, say, six months without
the option.
FILIAL GENER-
OSITY.
“ Your father looks
very nice with his grey
hairs.”
“ Yes, dear old chap! I
gave him those.”
Adelaide has had an aviation horror on a
small scale. The first public attempt at flying
in S. Aus. was made on Cheltenham racecourse,
and the arrangements for controlling the crowd
were primitive. Also it appears, obviously
enough, that when a long, light body rears its
front part into the air, then, if there is any wind
at all, it must pivot round on its back
wheels before they have a chance to rise.
That is what happened. The machine slewed
and charged the crowd. Nevertheless, only one boy
was badly hurt. He got into the propeller, which
was revolving at several miles a minute. The
collision smashed the light walnut blades; but
the boy lost his right hand first. This tragedy of
popular science recalls Perceval Landon’s grim
horror of the gyroscope which, exhibited in a
town hall, slipped off its stand, took charge, and
killed the whole assembly.
ON THE NOD.
THE STONY ONE: “ We've been nodding to each other for some time now. May I ask you to
lend me half a crown! ”
THE OTHER: “I think we had better keep to the nodding system.”
Between Attorney - General Drysdale
Brown, Premier Weary, Coroner Cole and a shoal
of legal sharks, the inquiry into the Richmond
(Vic.) railway disaster has got itself into a tangle.
Coroner Cole first set out to hold his inquest, and,
having opened it, promptly adjourned it for an
unconscionable five weeks, chiefly at the desire of
numerous Gents One. This extensive adjourn-
ment meeting with public disapproval, Brown
and Weary spoke firmly to Cole, who thereupon
reopened the inquest next day, and adjourned it
for 10 days. Also, he excused one juryman who
desired to take a holiday, and said he would pro-
ceed with six. This second adjournment dis-
pleased Weary and Brown; so, after getting
“ eminent counsel’s ” opinion, they immediately
appointed P.M. Moore, Port-Engineer Maclean
and University-Demonstrator Higgins to sit as a
Board of Inquiry. This leaves two presumably
legal bodies inquiring into one catastrophe.
Brown and Weary are, however, working on the
opinion of “eminent counsel” aforesaid —Mit-
chell, K.C.—to the effect that Cole’s re-adjourn-
ment of the inquest and discharge of a juryman
have invalidated the whole proceedings, and that
to hold an inquest now a body must be exhumed,
a new jury empanelled, and the whole business
started afresh. Coroner Cole, however, dissents
from this view, and proposes to go on with his in-
quest as if nothing had happened and the Attorney-
General and Premier didn’t exist. He has
power to call witnesses, and to gaol them if they
don’t appear. Also, he can commit for trial, for
murder, manslaughter or any other felony;
whereas Brown’s Board cannot punish contemptu-
ous or recalcitrant witnesses save by fining them
£lO a-piece, and it can’t commit anybody for trial
for anything. It can only make a report and
recommendations; but on those recommendations
Attorney-General J. Drysdale Brown can act
pretty much as he likes. And the point of that
will lie in the application thereof.

The old, old policy of baby-prohibition,
this time from the Victorian police department: —
WARDSMAN wanted at Police Hospital, Vic-
toria Barracks, St. Kilda-road. Salary, £75 per
year with board. Applicants must be single, etc.
It is a wonder the unfortunate baby ever con-
trives to get born at all, when one considers the
number of awful bosses who fine the father in his
whole salary if baby happens.
Melbourne City Council may not be the
most gradual body on earth, but it is as near the
limit as anyone need wish to get. Two years ago
the Taxicab Co. asked it to draw up regulations
and issue licenses for its vehicles, and the regu-
lations haven’t been drawn up yet. If the council
hasn’t brains enough to accomplish that small
job, it could borrow the by-laws of London or
New York, or go-ahead little Toowoomba,
and adopt them, but it doesn’t even do that.
Also, it has now spent three years pondering over
some up-to-date building regulations, those now
in use being 40 years old, like the city’s water-
mains. And it still ponders ponderously.
If Hawley Crippen, the man whose wife’s
remains were found in the cellar of his London
house, comes to the gallows he will have the dis-
tinction of being the first man to be hanged by wire-
less telegraphy, just as Tawell was the first to be
scragged by the ordinary wire. It was by the
sending of airgrams to vessels at sea that the
authorities first got on the track of Crippen.
Then will follow the first murderer captured by
airship, or the first one identified by having his
photograph telephoned or telegraphed. Quite a
large new field is opening up before the active
homicide. The world has moved since he used to
flee on foot over a roadless land with the avenger
of blood howling personally in his wake.
* * * *
One has to go abroad to get patriotic sen-
timent, as well as news, nowadays. The keenest
piece of criticism on the gorgeous new infant
school, opened last weekatFootseray (Vic.), came
from Scotchman Gibson-Carmichael. He ob-
served the choice wall decorations, depicting an
English “meet” and fox-hunters lounging round
an ale-house, and remarked: “They might have
painted a scene more typical of Australia.” That
was all; but, coming from an imported Governor,
it ought to be enough.
JOE COOK.
(An Uncharitable Rhyme.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If you hear a voice complaining: like a hoarse and angry
rook,
That’s Cook.
He wails and shrieks and yammers like a voluble yahoo,
And his speech is Lamentations, chapters 1 to 42 :
Did you catch the name correctly ?—l’ll repeat it just for
you :
Joe Cook !
Does an awesome ululation come from out a darksome
nook?
’Tis Cook.
And no plan is ever mooted but he moaneth his dissent ;
It is always frosty Winter in his bitter discontent.
Who’s the Gnawer and the Nagger and the Nark in
Parliament?
Job Cook !
He gazes upon Office with a sad and hungry look,
Does Cook.
Ten years of weary drudgery his heart was forced to
know
For one sweet year of office with a big port-fol-i-o;
But whom did the electors mean when fierce they
shouted, “ Go ”?
Joe Cook.
Once more in Opposition, by Fortune quite forsook,
Sits Cook.
He cannot cease to whimper, and he will not cease to
nag,
For he fain would be a Martyr to the torture of the Gag ;
And, therefore, who has got the tongue which oftenest
doth wag?—
Job Cook!

At the Beaufort Police Court yes-
terday William Bell was charged
with cruelty to a horse. The con-
stable had to get permission to
destroy the animal. He stated that
the harness and dead body were
covered with clotted blood and
wounds. The P.M. imposed the
maximum sentence of a month’s im-
prisonment, with hard labor.

ABORIGIN
ALITIES
“ Gr.R.F.”: In the ’Bos of last century,
when the Torres Strait shellers first invaded
N.-W. waters after the valuable golden-lipped
shell, Broome, the now prosperous township on
Roebuck Bay, was a wild and woolly place. The
divers in those days were mostly white men, who
were paid £3 per 100 for the shells won.
They made big money, but the most of it
went on “square face,” and whilst under
the influence they played havoc with the
nerves of the more sedate residents, ihe Gov-
eminent vessel that had control of the pearling
grounds was called the Meda, and was com-
manded by a genial mariner named Smith. Ever
so many people are named Smith. Prior to its
first visit to Broome, which was at that time so
isolated that it did not even have telegraphic
communication with' civilisation, a rumor was cir-
culated and believed that some pi metical pearler
was about to make a raid on the place, as in the old
buccaneer days, and lift the shell, which in those
times of faulty steamer-service was stacked on the
beach in large quantities waiting shipment. Dire
were the threats that were uttered by the shellers
of what they would do to any vessel that entered
the bay with felonious intent, and their rifles and
revolvers were kept handy. One morning, on the
top of the flood-tide, the Meda, then a stranger to
all Broomeites, was seen coming in from sea
straight towards the creek where the shell was
stacked. Everybody and his dog turned out, and
speculation ran high. Then their worst suspicions
were confirmed, for a black flag, emblazoned
with the skull and cross-bones, was broken
out at. the main truck. “ She’s a pirate!” was
the cry ; and as the astonished skipper conned his
vessel to the anchorage, he was greeted with a
perfect hail of bullets. “Damn it, are all those
fools drunk?” he asked, turning to a diver named
Hatch whom he had picked up at Whistler’s
Creek to pilot him into Broome. “ What the hell
are you firing at ? ” he yelled to the crowd ashore
as a second volley blazed forth (the vessel by this
time had swung to the rush of the tide, and was
pulling heavily at its anchor close to the shore).
“ Who are you?” yelled back old Walton of the
Vixen, gazing in bewilderment from Smith’s smart
uniform to the black flag aloft. “What are you
flying that damn thing for?” Then Smith saw
the, joke that had been perpetrated on him by
Hatch, who', whilst Smith swore in a manner
most unbecoming of the master of a Government
ship, sat at the table in the cuddy below drinking
gin and singing in a sweet tenor voice, “There’s
a Light in the Window Burns Brightly for Me.”
* * * *
“ Maitai ” : Beer, wine, whisky and pain-
killer—l’ve tried ’em all; but the queerest Tangle-
foot I’ve swallowed is a concoction —branded
wine—made by Austrians. Whilst north of
Auckland (M.L.) I struck a camp of Austrian
gumdiggers celebrating, in the old way, the birth-
day of some Austrian potentate. I quaffed a
couple of glasses of wine made on the premises.
The first glass maefe me just mellow ; but when I
had lowered the second glass I couldn’t stand —
my legs seemed as if they had broken their moor-
ings. Peculiarly enough, my head remained un-
affected. Following morning the sting of the
wine had left me, but later I had a glass of
temperance liquid, and again gradually the power
stole out of my legs. The porter—a melancholy
cuss—carried me to bed and said a prayer on my
behalf. By next day I had recovered ; but this
Austrian liquid has given me blue funk, and I
reckon its originator ought to be flogged with a
bunch of open razors.
A FEMININE SUGGESTION.
MR. JUMBUCK (proudly): “ Ain't he a beauty!"
CITY VISITOR: “ Yes, indeed; but he'd look ever
