“ Yectis ” : The “ paper trick ” for drinks
is an aged and respectable swindle, and deserves
honorable mention among ingenious devices for
beguiling the innocent. Take a strip of paper a
foot long and an inch wide; join the ends with
stamp-paper so as to form a loop, but before join-
ing give one end a half turn, so that you have a
loop with a spiral twist in it. Now, cut with
scissors all round exactly half-way between the
two borders, and what do you get ? Two loops,
of course, say nine mugs out of 10 ; but you don’t.
The astonishment of the mug who has backed his
ooinion is worth seeing. Also, his beer is worth
drinking.
ANOTHER PROVERB GONE WRONG.
TOWNIE (in the course of conversation): "Yes, but you
know familiarity breeds contempt.”
WANDERER: “ Oh, that’s a lot o’ bosh! Take pubs, f' instance. I’ve bin familiar with pubs
fer the last 30 years, and I ain’t got a scrap of contempt for ’em!”
“ Maitai ” : I’ve seen a score of quaint
horse-meetings, but the one I saw in the King
Country (Maoriland) t’other day lowers the others
to zero. It was run entirely by the brown man
on brown man rules. I had a pass to the grand
stand reserved for fashionable rangitaras. The
stand was an ill-us,ed hay loft of a forgotten
period, but its importance was freely referred
to. In the left-hand corner three musi-
cians were ensconced. They designated
themselves “The King Country Or-
chestra.” Each had a concertina.
and by the attitude they struck
they were prepared to play all
and sundry into eternity. The
paddock—which hadn’t been
cleared of stumps and cow-
wreckage—held about 500
brown men and their wa-
hines. They were all agog
with a genuine brand of
nigger excitement, and the
scene resembled more a
muster of gay old canni-
bals than a horse meeting.
At the sound of a heavy-
tongued hell the concertinas
started their soul-destroying
music. Shrieks from the
crowd injured the atmos-
phere. “ Kia Ora!” jelled
the nigger gathering, and
away went a dozen steeds.
As the winner was coming
in—more than 30yds. ahead
of the second nag—a pakeha-
Maori whispered something
to, me about oats. His
speechlqt was overheard by
those next us, and it almost
caused a revolution. Prompt-
ly a half-dozen foaming war-
riors grabbed him savagely
and emptied him on the earth
below. I climbed out of
the stand and joined the
surging, furious crowd, on
principle. I discovered that
a protest had been lodged
on the ground that the winning horse had
had a feed of oats the night before. A fearsome
harangue started among stewards, judges,
jockeys and lookers-on, and several were either
injured or threatened with injury. When it was
announced that the protest was disallowed there
was pandemonium. For 15 minutes everybody
pummelled everybody else. Just as the local con-
stable began collecting an account of the affair I
skedaddled.
“ Puketapu’s ” orgie :
At the time I write of, Karamea was a special settle-
ment on the West Coast of Maoiilarid. Once every three
months a small steamer called at the settlement with
provisions, including liquor, and the day of its arrival
was the occasion of a public spree on an extended scale.
I reached there one evening, all rags and tatters, in com-
pany with a surveyor—a very religious man but, strangely
enough, a very good man—and as we crossed the creek
we knew that the periodical steamer was in. Nothing
could have been clearer. “Drunks” were everywhere—
some lying on their ears in the roadway, some capering
on the verandah of the little pub, some half-naked,
some fighting, some cursing, some singing, some crying
—a rare old twin-action jag. As we walked towards the
shanty a local celebrity known as “The Gunner” met
us, and pulling a bottle of whisky from under his
whiskers, invited us to “pouch a drop or two.” We
did ; in fact, we pouched the lot, and left “The Gunner”
sitting on the roadside weeping bitterly beside the empty
bottle. I’ve been in some hot schools one place and
another, but that bush joint in Karamea secures my re-
commendation as the star item. The grog would have
burned its way through an ice-chest; the tap-room and
bar were crowded ; the smell of booze-sizzled humanity
was thick and blue and fur-lined. As a man in a
dream or a stupor, I found myself playing Yankee
grab for drinks with a big Dutchman and the
parson’s son. The parson’s son threw 15 twice running,
after throwing 10, 8, 17. I pulled put, and started to
back his dice at a quid a throw with the landlord, who
was half-sozzled himself. 1 forget how much I won, but
it must have been considerable, because for several
thousand years I sat there while round me grew piles of
gold with drunken demons dancing on the top of them.
Grotesque faces slipped into the lamplight, and were
pursued by giant fists that were attached to no arms.
Time was not, but the crash of worlds (or bottles) was
marked by the dying yell of the Obliterated. And then
1 fell, and fell into Space that changed its hue as I sped
to its foundations. When I woke it was broad daylight.
A kiwi was picking at my boot, and the snow-wind from
the ranges had frozen a great splash of blood across my
face.
* # * *
“ Clay pan ” : The absence of locks on the
Darling lias a sad effect on the shipping of the
Big Trickle. At almost every town from Bre-
warrina to Menindie I saw large boats moored
from the wharves, trees, etc., waiting for a rise.
The smaller boats, however, manage to get a
river all the year round. Many of these are
family-owned. Among the fleet is a small boat
known as the Wave. It is managed solely by mem-
bers of the skipper’s family. His daughters can
be seen taking their turn at the wheel by day,
carefully steering their floating home clear of all
danger, while the old man takes charge of the
barge they have in tow. The hoys take the wheel
through the long night. One hears a lot regard-
ing the hardy bush womenfolk, but little is said
about those brave little women whose destiny it
is to “ take the wheel,” “flush the decks,” etc., on
some screeching river tub in the interior of this
land of vast resources. ,
AN OLD MASTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We were cartin’ lar.hes and palin’s from the slopes of
Mount St. Leonard,
With our axles near the road-bed and the mud as stiff
as glue ;
And our bullocks weren’t precisely what you’d call con-
ditioned nicely,
And meself and messmate Mitchell had our doubts of
gettin’ through.
It had rained a tidy skyful in the week before we started,
But our tucker-bag depended on the sellin’ of our load ;
So we punched ’em on by inches, liftin’ ’em across the
pinches,
Till we struck the final section of the worst part of the
road.
We were just congratulatin’ one another on the journey,
When we blundered in a pot-hole right within the sight
of goal,
Where the bush-track joins the metal. Mitchell, as he
saw her settle,
Justified his reputation at the peril of his soul.
We were in a glue-pot, certain—red and stiff and most
tenacious;
Over naves and over axles —waggon sittin’ on the road.
“’Struth," says I, “they’ll never lift her. Take a shot
in Hell to shift her.
Nothin’ left us but unyoke ’em and sling off the blessed
load.”
Now, beside our scene of trouble stood a little one-
roomed humpy,
Home of an enfeebled party by the name of Dad McGee.
William was, I pause to mention, livin’ on an old-age
pension
Since he gave up bullock-putichin’ at the age of eighty
three.
Startled by our exclamations, Daddy hobbled from the
shanty,
Hobbled out and over to us on his old rheumatic pins,
Shadin’ his old eyes and peerin’ here and there around
the clearin’,
While we watched his consternation with half-sympa-
thetic grins.
“Eh! Wot’s happened now?” he quavered, in a weak
and shaky treble,
Gazin’ where the stranded waggon looked like some half-
foundered ship.
Then the state o’ things he spotted. “ Looks,” he says,
“like you was potted.”
And he toddled up to Mitchell. “Here,” said he,
“ gimme that whip.”
Mitchell, bein’ out o’ patience, flung a glance of anger at
him,
Followed by some fancy language of his very choicest
brand.
Then old Daddy seemed to straighten. “ Now,” he
yelled, “ don’t keep me waitin’!
Pass that whip, you blarsted blue-tongue!” Mitchell
put it in his hand.
Well! I’ve heard of transformations; heard of fellers
sort of changin’
In the face of sudden danger or some great emergency ;
Heard the like in song and story and in bush traditions
hoarv,
But I nearly dropped me bundle as I looked at Dad
McGee.
While we gazed he seemed to toughen ; as his fingers
gripped the handle
His old form grew straight and supple, and a light leaped
in his eye :
And he stepped around the waggon, not with footsteps
weak and laggin’,
But with firm, determined carriage, as he flung the whip
on high.
Now he swung the leaders over, while the whip-lash
snarled and volleyed ;
And they answered like one bullock, strainin’to each
crack and clout ;
And he kept his cursin’ till old Brindle made a
blunder ;
Then I thought all Hell had hit me, and the master
opened out.
And the language ! O, the language ! I have known
some noble eursers—
“ Hell-fire ” Mac and “ Cursin’ ” Brogan— men of bound-
less blasphemee,
Full of fancy exclamations, trimmed with frills and
decorations ;
But their talk was childish prattle to that language of
McGee.
In a trance stood messmate Mitchell; seemed to me I
must be dreantin’;
While the wondrous words and phrases only genius could
loose
Roared and rumbled fast and faster in the throat of that
Old Master —
Oaths and curses tipped with lightning, cracklin’flames
of fierce abuse.
Then we knew the man before us was a Master of our
callin’;
One of those great lords of language gone for ever from
Outback ;
Heroes of an ancient order ; men who punched across the
border;
Vanished giants of the ’sixties ; puncher-princes of the
track.
Now we heard the timbers strainin’, heard the waggon’s
loud complainin’,
And the Master cried triumphant, as lie swung ’em into
line,
As they dug their toes into it, lifted her, and pulled her
through it:
“That’s the way we uster do it in the days o’ sixtv-
riine ! ”
Near the foot of Mount St. Leonard lives an old, en-
feebled party
Who retired from bullock punchin’ at the age of eighty-
three.
If you seek him folk will mention, merely, that lie draws
the pension ;
But to us he looms a Master —Prince of Punchers, Dad
McGee!

“ Dux ” : The best perfume for the Maori,
after eating dried shark, is violet. Henare, the
bloated landowner, drops from his motor-car, and,
striding into the chemist’s shop, demands: “Te
pottle of scent —you know—te goot tink (stink).
Te taughter she go to big tarnce, Waitara. She
liket te wiolet. That te good tink ; smell ’trong
(strong). Orrite.” I’ve sold hundreds of bottles
of scent to the tribe, but never anything save the
violet “tink.”
ONE OF THE BEAUTIES OF
TEMPERANCE.
WILLIAM WOWSER : “O , my pore friend , look at
me. I have never touched a drop of drink
in my life.”
THE SATURATED: “I shee (hic). Anurrer
’orrible exshample, etshetera! ”
At the one lone hotel which decks the
town of Port Moresby (Papua) there are two
tariffs. As the proprietor puts it, the price is
“£5 10s. with the blokes, and £6 10s. with
the gents in the coffee-room.” The differ-
ence lies in the fact that a gent gets a serviette
and a bloke doesn’t. On the other hand, there is
a serious drawback about being a gent. The
gents’ food has to be carried by the nigger
through the blokes’ dining apartment on its way
to the coffee-room, and if anything is passing
which doesn’t smell so bad as usual, it is liable to
be rushed by the blokes and confiscated. Taking
one consideration with another, it is perhaps best
to be a gent-bloke (he might be called gloke
for short)—that is, to be a bloke at meal times and
a gent in the coffee-room on all other occasions.

Marloo ” : George Simpson and Paddy
McAlone were swopping lies by the fire; and
when it came to Simpson’s lie he fired off the
following : “ Funnell and me was drawring wheat
into Tamworth about 20 years back. One night
we was camped five miles out. In the morning,
when we started to round up the teams, I hap-
pened to forget me weed, so I canters back to the
waggon for it. A big Chow, who must have
watched us leaving, was just doing a grease with
our tucker-bag and billycan. I jumped off,
grabbed a bit of rope, copped the Chink and tied
up his hands and legs. When we had yoked up,
I put it to Billy Funnell that we dump the bloke
in the creek, but Bill said he was too
scared after the last one that we dropped in
and never saw no more. So we stood
him against the back wheel, lashed
him to the spokes, and brought him
down to ther Johns.” McAlone, who
was jealous of Simpson’s reputation as
a liar, thought his chance had come
to bowl his rival out, so he remarked :
“ I suppose it was a corpse you handed
to the cops? You couldn’t rope a big
Chink to a wheel without him overlap-
ping.” But Simpson, who was a man
of resource, said : “Well, yer see,
Paddy, it was all downhill, and we had
the wheels chocked.” But Paddy had
another bomb. “What game,” he
said; “there ain’t no road with a
five-mile descent leading into Tam-
worth.” “ Tamworth,” snarled Simp-
son; “did I say Tamworth, boys”?
The boys agreed that he did. “My
mistake; I’ve never been there. It was
Toowoomba I meant.” McAlone hadn’t
been at Toowoomba.
* * * *
In reply to “B. Cabonne ” re
Lightning Ridge (N.S.W.) Quadrille Club. There
is no quadrille club in Lightning Ridge, and never
has been. He probably refers to the Entertain-
ing and Debating Society, initiation fee 2s. 6d.,
and Is. per month membership fee. Male mem-
bers occasionally try to teach their friends how to
dance. No ladies are admitted on these occasions.
I can only find records of two persons who asked
for a refund and were refused. One was “fired
out” of the Institute for drunken and black-
guardly conduct; the other for having been dis-
covered abstracting petty cash from the care-
taker’s till. If “B. Cabonne” was either of
these the position of affairs becomes clear at once.

“ Edyson ” : Oliver Lightbody was the
most impulsive man I ever knew. His im-
pulsiveness made history at Queenbee. The
affair was largely due to Kyley’s capacity for
making mischief. Kylev was not too sober when
he came to the sleeper-cutter’s camp at Two-up,
and his eyesight was never good anyhow. He
said he had seen Mrs. Lightbody running off with
Heimer, the young Dutchman at Bark Hut.
Here Lightbody’s impulsiveness got to work. He
knocked Kyley out for insulting his wife and
then started off. He returned in 11 months and
four days—returned to his own skillion home at
Queenbee, and found his wife plunging in a wash-
tub at the kitchen door. Mrs. Lightbody
was the reverse of her husband. “ Hello,
Ollie,” she said calmly, and went on soap-,
ing the goods. Presently she added: “You
back?” She rubbed for a minute, and then
asked: “Where yen bin?” Lightbody was
gaping at her as if she was a ghost. “I’ve been
chasin'Heimer,” he said ; “Heimer and you. I
chased him to Brisbane, an’ from Brisbane to
Katoomba, ’n’ from Katoomba t’ Day Dawn.”
“ You never caught him,” said Mrs. Lightbody.
“Why, he’s never bin away. And here iv I bin
all this time wondering what had become of yell.”
Then Lightbody leaned up against the house and
kicked himself vehemently for three minutes.

‘ ‘ Logan ” : Where is this Outback I read so
much about ? I have been west as far as Boulia,
and even there people waved their hands west-
ward and talked of Outback. I believe the man
from the east coast goes looking for Outback till
lie reaches Perth, and the man from the west goes
looking for it till he sights the outlying tramcars
of Sydney, and neither finds it. It isn’t there.
There never was any such place.
“F.J.U.”: The curse of Cow, which
blights the existence of so many youngsters in
New South Wales (vide the periodical reports of
inspectors of schools on the compulsory clauses of
the Education Act) roosts like a cwt. of devils
on Taranaki, the great cow-spanking portion of
Maoriland. Hundreds of children in that district
are stunted and dwarfed mentally and physically
before they are in
their teens. I have
seen them hoard
,the train at way-
side stations on
their road to
school, staring
red-eyed at les-
sons they should
have learned over-
night, or drooping'
towards the sleep
from which they
had been driven at
homeby the national
dragon. Another re-
sult of the she-bull
curse is wife drudgery.
Numbers of young Couples
get married every year
and go milking on shares with cow-owning
cookies, getting two-fifths of the monthly milk
cheque (£4O in the £100) and half the proceeds
from the sale of calves and pigs. A few years of
this cow drudgery age the women prematurely,
and make them haggard and unfit to rear
children. In M.L., the fearfully inflated values
are responsible for many of the evils of cowism.
Half the small dairymen are struggling under
a crushing load of interest due to the stock
auctioneers, who have liens all over the
country. While the price of butter holds
up, the load can be carried somehow ; but if
there comes a slump, the crash of fallen
bundles will be like a glass factory going
home drunk.
In Bathurst (N.S.W.), recently, the Liquor
Defence League members unanimously,resolved—
To withhold their patronage and support from trades-
men or others who are known to be in favor of reduction
or no-license, or who in any shape or form support the
no-license party.
In fact, the Rechabites are to be boycotted. If
the movement spreads it will hit Cold Tea hard.
The up-country pub-keeper wields a lot of influence
in his own sphere. At present he feels he is up
against it. His livelihood shudaersin the balance.
He may be relied on to battle desperately. Not
only will he callously decline, henceforth, to
supply militant teetotal families with alcohol, but
he may be relied on to interfere with their com-
fort in other ways. Even if he doesn’t refuse
accommodation to the bagman who does business
with the enemy, he will make things so dashed
uncomfortable for that trader that he will shy off
blue-ribbon establishments with horror. In the
average baekblocks town about 75 per cent, of
adult males frequent one or more of the hotels,
and use them as clubs. Nothing will be simpler
than for Bung to enroll these patrons of his as
members of a Booze Protection Association, and
pledge them to withdraw their custom from
saintly Mr. Brown, who vends groceries and
loathes beer; and his chapel mate, good Ber-
ruther Jones, who bakes for a crust and regards
fermented wine as an abomination beforethe Lord.
Even the Rechabite who drops public proselytising
and seeks to smite the Der-rink fiend sub rosa , as
it were, will not be safe. His whispered words
of warning will be fraught with financial peril.
The Snares and pitfalls, lurking ready to betray
him to the ungodly, will be as numerous as the
local sly-grog shops.
“ Developer ” writes
Hill End (New South Wales) figures by “Fossicker”
are, in the main, correct. Australia’s" first alluvial
field was in the district, and the first Australian void
in matrix was found on Hawkin’s Hill by Bill Adams
some considerable time after the Battle of Waterloo. He
is still alive to tell the tale. Alluvial regulations were
used, hence more than 20 claims in about 20 acres and
they produced over 20 tons of gold. Bill was too young
to get a claim. Very little gold was got near the surface.
The rich gold started in Carroll and Beard’s claim at less
than 100 ft. The run of gold unmistakably dipped
north with an eastern strike and underlay;
hence it was a little deeper in Krohmann’s, and'
deeper still in Beyer’s and Holtermann’s, and was
worked down to 850 ft. in the Monte Cristo, Rawsthorne’s
and Brown’s. From this end of the Hill, quartz contain-
ing 7J tons of gold was packed to the batteries higher
up. In the middle claims—Rampant Lion, Paxton, Star
of Peace and others —the run of gold was worked nearly
another 100 ft. deeper, and so on through Fisher and
Beard’s, Hermann’s, Mathewson’s and others. The rich
gold was continuous in one vein or another for 1500 ft. in
length. Then the hill rises suddenly about 160 ft. At
this end none of the shafts are deep enough to pick
up this run of gold, if it exists, and there is
no break in the country to prevent it existing.
True, the veins are all small, but as they were enriched
they increased in number. In Krohmann’s and in
several other claims there are workings still standing
from 20ft. to 30ft. wide. Hill End had a fine short-lived
boom. It was pegged for 20 miles, and there were over
1000 companies. Though nine-tenths of the gold came
from the claims in the 20 acres, not one of them pros-
pected 200 ft. deeper than where the gold gave out; and
in several of them the veins from which something like
£2,000,000 were taken can be seen in the bottom levels,
and although not rich, they are all gold-hearing. In the
light of the present, Hill End is a monument of the
appalling gold-mining ignorance of 40 years ago.
Nearly every claim in that small area' was sold
in Sydney at prices running from £BO,OOO to
£160,000, and none had any working capital. Brown got
£60,000 for his 30ft. area. They have never been aban-
doned, and several have been held for many years by
culling over mullock. There were half a dozen companies
formed to drive tunnels great distances, and they all
died with the boom. One of these tunnels, after four or
five of the claims had amalgamated, has been extended
through the schistose slate belt containing the veins. A
drive was taken north towards the Golden Ville. Here a
vein that had been poor for 100 ft. above again made gold
to the extent of 12oz. to the ton. The famous area
is now held by about half a dozen different parties,
and it is reported that, with the ground further
north, it has been bonded by somebody who
has put up money and hopes to do something.
If this is true, then it is the best gold mining prospect in
Australia. There is only one way to prove the field. The
Turon River tunnel scheme, which the Government
offered to subsidise to the extent of £30,000, is too costly,
and is not warranted. My way is this: Clean out the
Star of Peace shaft before fixing the site of the new one.
It will probably be several hundred feet further north.
Sink it 1500 ft., size 15 x 5, cost £15,000, equipment
£5OOO. Crosscut through the belt every 200 ft. below
500 ft. By these means do 10,000 ft. of development work,
cost £30,000. Half a ton of gold would pay for the lot,
and surely a field that has pro-
duced nearly fifty times that
quantity within 400 ft. of
the surface warrants the
work. Meanwhile, put up 10
fairly heavy stamps and run

NEWCHUM : “ Are those the big mosquitoes I hear

WAYBACK : " No. them’s just plain crows.”
through the many thousands of tons of mullock on the
surface and in the stopes. It will pay. Also prospect
about the old workings now they are drained by the
tunnel. This might pay for everything.
Adelaide is now so crammed with people,
and houses are so scarce that boarding-house-
keepers spend their spare* time going round
pricing motor-cars and other trifles. In one
dwelling, which claims to be fashionable, there
are 40 men, women and children lodging,
where half that number could not be comfortably
accommodated. Small rooms* have two or
three beds in them, and when a house becomes
vacant, or a rumor circulates that it is liable to be
vacant shortly, the landlord gets up a step-ladder
for fear of being trampled to death.
THE USURPER.
[FOR THE BULLET IN. ]
Old John goes raging up and down, with red fire in his
eye 1
The dry-hash is in other hands ; the grey corn-beef no
more
Wobbles beneath his hasty fist—old Johnny is laid by,
And alien feet are thumping now upon his kitchen-
floor.
Fat -tsak, with onion-garnishings, no longer fills his pan ;
To-day we feed in horrid doubt. With John there was
no fear
That you might miss your duff ; he was a plain, white
sort o’ man
Who loved to call his soul his own—and sometimes
take a beer.
With sago from the flour-mill clean, and, from the
saffron-jar,
Hen-fruit, and sweet milk out of tins, he fed his hungry
crew—
He made us custards, yellow-brown on top. Such
puddings are
Quite pleasing to the eye, and often to the palate, too !'
And now he’s raging up and down, with red fire in his
eye
He’s been like this since j’esterday, when first the row
began.
It seems a shame he’s lost his job. Although, when he
is dry,
He sometimes takes a drop o’ beer, he is a good, white
man 1
To-day we watch our Ohow-made soup with wild and
fearful eyes.
The suet-puddings roll upon the platter, soapy-white.
As we surround rude fragments of pale Chow-bedevilled
pies
We shudder, and we fear there may he dreams for us
to-night 1
Ah, yes, our pub’s in trouble—lots ! —fair in a sordid row,
For Jimmy Chong is cooking here—and old John,
raging by,
Is full of beer and wrath! I thank my stars lam no
Chow,
When John comes cursing down the track, with red fire
in his eye 1

HOOH, DER KAISER.
[For The Buhbti n.]
The Kaiser is convalescent. . . . The Kaiser sleeps with
a Bible alongside his bed. . . . The Kaiser is suffering
from a boil. . . . The Kaiser is drinking mineral waters to
counteract a tendency towards gout. —A few of the
numerous recent cablegrams about the Kaiser.
Ain’t you feeling up to snuff,
Kaiser Bill ?
Is it true or is it bluff,
Kaiser Bill ?
Oh ! dark fears my heart assail,
For perhaps you’re looking pale,
Can’t spring off your royal tail,
Kaiser Bill.
Yes, indeed, it’s very sad,
Kaiser Bill;
And I bet you’re feeling mad,
Kaiser Bill.
It is very hard, I know,
For the ruler of the show
To be told he must go slow,
Kaiser Bill !
And what about that boil,
Kaiser Bill ?
Oh ! it makes my heart recoil,
Kaiser Bill,
To think of all your care,
With that large carbuncle there ;
It’s a very big affair,
Ain’t it, Bill ?
And what about the Book,
Kaiser Bill ?
Is it in the same old nook,
Kaiser Bill?
Does it by your bedside stand
Close beside the royal hand
That guides the Fatherland,
Kaiser Bill ?
Are the Dreadnoughts going well,
Kaiser Bill ?
Are you building them like hell,
Kaiser Bill ?
“ Peace on earth,” the Good Book says,
But perhaps you’ve missed the phrase,
Or your sight is dim some days,
Kaiser Bill !
But you’re not a bad old sport,
Kaiser Bill ;
Though you sometimes do cavort,
Kaiser Bill!
There’s no doubt you are all right,
Though you recognise your might,
And you like the old limelight,
Don’t you, Bill ?
MAORILAND. H. E. BOON.
J. S. Clark, wiiose sovereigns were once
his comfort, has died in poverty at Maryborough
(Vic.). He was on the Victorian goldfields in the
“ roaring ’50’s,” and there laid the foundations
of his fortune. It was a very great fortune, but
it oozed through his trusting fingers. Jabez
B tlfour, of the Liberator Building Society, was
the siren who called to poor old Clark, and left
him with nothing to look forward to but an old-
age pension.
“ Ballon ” disinters a parson :—
Re “Oby’s” par. (B. 28/7/10). The Anglican clergy-
man who invaded Norfolk Island in the ’30’s was the
ltev. H. T. Stiles, not Styles. He was incumbent of St.
Matthew’s, Windsor, at the time. The letter that Samuel
Marsden wrote to him re his appointment to the grue-
some job is somewhere in the Mitchell Library. One of
Mr. Stiles’s sons was an inspector in the Bank of N.S.W.
Another had a property on Monaro and exported cattle ;
and a third was in charge, until recently, of tliedoxology
works at Watson’s Bay, Sydney.
John Forrest laid up much future trouble
for himself in the Reps, last week. Being
in vigorous mood after his dinner, he told the
Labor party that they and their supporters were
fellows of no enterprise; said that Australia was
a fine country for the loafer; and, in general,
created the impression that the several hundred
thousand people who voted Labor last April were
a lot of wasters. The arrogant Bigjohn has three
years before him, in which he may explain this
away, but he will probably find the time insuffi-
cient. His idea of "enterprise” is apparently to
arrive in a country in its infancy, and be there at
the first big land-grab.
“Bloggs” : I have read several hundred
utterly pointless anecdotes about the new King
since Ed. VII. s demise, but in the matter of
hopeless and unashamed asininity the following,
which I have hewn from Sydney Sunday Times,
impresses me as a winner :—
Many stories of his Majesty’s earlv days in the navy
are now being retailed. One of tlie best, of these has,
however, not yet found its way into print. It was in the
days when he was a lieutenant, and he was temporarily
in charge of the navigation of a cruiser. The vessel was
not one of the best for answering its helm, and yawed
badly once or twice. The Admiral of the squadron was
rather a sharp-tempered individual, and at length he
signalled angrily from the flagship for “ the young fool
who was trying to navigate the — to come aboard at
once and explain what the deuce he thought he was
doing.” The Admiral’s astonishment to find Prince
George before him a little later may well be imagined.
“The Admirals astonishment may well be
imagined.” Wlij'? Are embryo kings super-
naturals, who navigate faultlessly from birth ? It
may be urged that there is some joke underlying
this depressing effort. I can’t glimpse it. I wish
the item were alive that I might poison it or kick
it to death, and afterwards deny it Christian
burial.
A gloomy suspicion arises that no great
traveller has really ever been anywhere. Now it
is Sven Hedin, the Swedish explorer of Thibet
and the Pamirs, who has the mud of obloquy
thrown at him, one Scindberg, who has always
stayed at home, declaring that Hedin borrowed
his ideas front Renarts, who hung out his ex-
ploratory shingle a full 190 years ago. And per-
haps Renarts also never went anywhere.
A long-deferred patient from the Sunshine
(Vic.) railway breakage of 1908 has just reached
his end in the Melbourne Hospital. The de-
ceased, Herbert Loughton, was one who emerged
from the gaudy horror with apparently trivial
injuries to his body and nervous system. He
never properly recovered, however, and tubercu-
losis supervened and carried him off. He was
only 39, and, by the way, only received compen-
sation because he was damaged. His widow, it
it said, has not yet received any extra payment
because be is dead.
J. H. SCULLIN, M.H.R., a somewhat vigorous
Labor orator.
Andrew D., one of the landed Badgery
family, died at Sutton Forest (N.S.W.) the other
day. The original Badgery came to Australia
early in last century, and was one of the settlers
who signed an address of welcome to Governor
Bligli in 1800. Alongside of James Badgery’s
signature is that of Thomas N. Pitt, and a cen-
tury after the names of the Pitts and the Bad-
gerys are still connected. But the Badgery
opinion of Bligh changed apparently, as he later
on signed an address to Major Johnston, demand-
ing the arrest of the nautical Governor.
The wonderful collection of Australian
flower pictures, painted by Mrs. Ellis Rowan, is
being sold piecemeal on the premises of Angus
and Robertson, Sydney. The story of the col-
lection is something of a tragedy. Mrs. Rowan
did for Australia’s flowers what Gould did for its
birds. She travelled great distances to paint rare
wild blossoms in their own environment, and very
often one picture would cost £5O or £6O or so in
expenses. Many of them represent rare and
scarce flowers, which are vanishing like the
aborigine—stifled by pricklv-pear, crowded out
by cultivation, or trodden to death by sordid
cow-hoofs. Twice at least Mrs. Rowan received
very large offers from abroad—offers running
into a good many thousands ; but she didn’t
want her works to leave Australia, and
she always reckoned that, one day or another,
some Australian Government would buy the lot
so that they might be kept together and made
available for the public. No such Government
eventuated, however, and things are not so good
with the talented artist as they were ; so one of
the finest collections of flower studies in the
world is being scattered to the four winds —in
many cases at absurdly low prices.
The departure from earthly trouble, at the
age of 89, of Henry Scotland, for 42 years a mem-
ber of the Maoriland Legislative Council, removes
one of the last of the life members of the House
of Decay at Wellington. Theold gentleman—anda
very courteous old gentleman he was—saw much
of the rows and bickerings that took place be-
tween the Provincial Councils and the central
authority, or General Assembly, a position of
things analogous to that now existing in Aus-
tralia between the Commonwealth and the
States. Occasionally the differences of opinion
were of a substantial character, but generally
they were the imaginary grievances of persons
of town-pump importance who couldn’t see fur-
ther than their own back-yard even with a tele-
scope. Scotland (who was a son of George
Scotland, long-ago Chief Justice of Trinidad),
was appointed to the Legislative Council of the
General Assembly in 1868, seven years before the
Provincial Councils were abolished by a popula-
tion that had grown weary of the town-pumpers.
And now, if the deceased gentleman finds any of
them where he’s gone, he’ll have to start empty-
ing them out all over again.
This is a business age. The Taranaki
Anglican Cuukch Magazine, in reporting the
resignation of the vicar of St. Mary's Church,
says :
The following resolution was passed : “ That the vestry
expresses its deep regret upon learning that the state of
Mr. Evans’s health make it necessary for him to take this
step, and trusts that he will benefit by the enforced re-
tirement from his labors in the parish, and be very soon
restored to health and strength.”
It was resolved that an inventory be made of all the
portable property of the church.
The survivors of John McDouall Stuart’s
expedition across Australia have been celebrating
the 48th anniversary of their arrival at the top of
the map. There are five survivors holding on for
the Jubilee celebration. Auld, King and Nash
were present on the 25th ; McGorrery was de-
tained up country by floods, and Billintt is in.
England. On the table was shown (in bottle) a
fragment of the dried beef used on the trip, and
the flag carried through was also on view. Pat
Auld made a fighting speech against the Australian
Government’s plea that it has no fund out of
which to pay pensions to the widows of explorers
who are dead, and also against the transfer of
the Territory to the Commonwealth. “If our
Parliament hands over the Northern Territory
for about 2d. an acre, they ought to put a sum on
the estimates for enlarging the Lunatic Asylum.”
(Cheers and Laughter).
A scrap of old biography :
The death of Sir Henry Tichborne, aged 45, was cabled
last week. Photos of him as a fat-faced small boy ap-
peared in thousands of shop windows and hundreds of
newspapers in the English-speaking world of 40 years ago
No country had a wider knowledge of his infant physi-
ognomy than Australia. For he played the part of “ the
Rightful Heir” in the famous Tichborne comedy drama,
where fat Arthur Orton, the Wagga Wagga butcher,
figured as the Claimant— alias “The Real Sir Roger.”
Roger Charles Doughty Tichborne, who probably got
drowned at sea, was the thin young man whom fat Orton
so ludicrously impersonated, whilst Henry A. J. Doughty
Tichborne, nephew of the missing Roger, was the nipper
who succeeded to the old baronetcy and estates which
Orton claimed. The fat man’s claim, by the way,
had a very small beginning. He merely read, in a
Sydney paper, an advertisement for information re
the missing Roger Tichborne, and having got
hold of a few facts re Roger’s disappearance
he wrote to silly old Lady Tichborne (the mother) in
England, stating th.it he was her long-lost son, and
would trouble her for £5OO to start him in a butchery
business in Wagga. The impostor originally had no idea
of venturing to England, but Lady T., being determined
to “ mother” the first Roger who turned up, sent an old
nigger servant out to Australia to recognise the Claimant.
The doddering hlack man staggered him by crying,
“ Dear young master ! ” as soon as he knew where to look
for the alleged Roger. And so the Wagga butcher took
courage to go to England and start the legal proceedings
which ended in his conviction for perjury. It was a ridi-
culous fraud at the outset, but it gathered a lot of
credence and imposing evidence as it went roiling on.
When it finally blew out there still remained the possi-
bility that Roger Tichborne had not been drowned at sea,
but did actually reach Australia, to the knowledge of
some people, one of whom had given Arthur Orton a few
scraps of information about the shipwrecked wanderer.
OFFICIAL HYPOCRISY.
Following on questions put to the Chief Secretary
recently, as to officers being compelled to work a 24-
hour shift at police stations, thei Inspector-General of
Police asked the men for a “ straightforward and manly ”
statement on the matter. By a four-to-one majority the
officers have declared themselves satisfied with existing
conditions. It is stated, however, that the replies do
not represent the true feelings of the men.— News Item.
The Boss: “ You're 'perfectly satisfied , ain’t
yer ? ”
The 24-hour Shifter (sighting the waddv) :
“ Certainly, sir. Never felt more contented
in me life.”
“Morty Muirhead, the handsome, reck-
less Adelaide lawyer who married John Robb’s
daughter and went down in the Federal Bank
crash, has got his three years’ suspension of a
second-class insolvency certificate reduced to 18
months, of which a year has already run. Chief
Justice Way handed out his judgment in the ap-
peal with a collection of frigid reasons for upset-
ting the Insolvency Commissioner’s judgment.
Muirhead, he remarked, had allowed three friends
—ladies—to pledge their £25,000 fortune for him.
Yes, but that was not an offence in insolvency,
for the other creditors actually benefited. He
had travelled and law-suited recklessly? True,
hut that was all done against his brother-in-law,
in the expectation (unfounded) of getting some
considerable fraction of Robb’s money. lie had
delayed the insolvency proceedings by contu-
macious behavior? True, but he (Way) would
not have visited that on the certificate; he would
have either called in a specialist to see whether
Muirhead was really right in his mind, or would
have sent him to prison till he consented to give
sensible answers to the questions put to him.
S.A.’s Methodist Chief Justice never Damns;
but as a collection of Faint Praise his latest
utterance could hardly be beaten.
“ Speaker’s Gallery ” : Concerning the
father of the N.S.W. Assembly :
The Bulletin (21/7/TO) suggests a possibility that
N.S.W. Assembly will have to “hunt for a fresh dad"
after polling day. A false alarm. Harry Levien sits
tighter to-day for Tamworth, after 30 years’ continuous
representation of the Northern Division (allowing for re-
distributions), than ever before. He has already topped
the poll 13 times. He knew one defeat—when he was
beaten by a single vote. That was when, as a boy
politician, 33 years ago, he stood for Wollombi against
Janies Augustine Cunneen (first P.M.G. under Re-
sponsible Government) and Joseph Eckford. Eck-
ford vvon. At the general election in 1880 the
candidates for Tamworth were: Levien, Sydney
Burdekin, Henley Bennett and Michael Bourke.
Levien headed Burdekin (junior member) by nine.
Levien’s subsequent colleagues were John Gill, Michael
Burke and William Springthorpe Dowell (of the melli-
fluous voice). He has never had a walk-over, but, since
Wollombi, he has always won in in a canter. In the fine
art of electioneering Harry Levien is only equalled by
Bill Lyne, who, by the way, entered the N.S.W. Assem-
bly with him. He, also, in 30 years, has not known
defeat. Touching Levien’s House-in-Macquarie-street
record, it is interesting to know that of the 103
members elected to the 10th Parliament, he alone sits
in the Assembly. The great majority have gone
over. Survivors include Francis Abigail, H. S. Badgery,
Edmund Barton, H. H. Brown, J. F. Burns, H. C. Dan-
gar, Jacob Garrard, L. F. Hevdon, John Kidd, Dan
O’Connor, C. E. Pilcher, G. H. Reid, T. M. Slattery,
Bruce Smith, Sydney Smith, T. R. Smith, F. B. Suttor
and W. J. Trickett. Death has claimed J. P. Abbott,
George W. Allen, Alexander Bowman, S. C. Brown, J. N.
Brunker, David Buchanan, Burdekin, Angus Cameron,
Edward Ooombes, Copeland, John Davies, J. S. Farnell,
Michael Fitzpatrick, Jas. Fletcher, Wm. Forster, Garrett,
Garvan, Jacob, Jennings, Lackey, G. A. Lloyd, McElhone,
Ninian Melville, Mitchell, T. 0. O’Mara, Parkes, Renwick,
Robertson, Dr. Ross, See, Burdett-Smith, Stuart, Dr.
Tarrant, Teece, S. H. Terry, R. M. Vaughan, Watson,
Wisdom, F. A. Wright and Young.

The WILDCAT
COUMN
The Australian Bank of Commerce.—This is
the bird that has risen from the ashes of the Australian
Joint Stock Bank. It wasn’t an early riser ; the old
A.J.S. had, in fact, been ashes for a good 17 j'ears before
the Phoenix took to the wing. But having made a start,
the A.B.C. does so with a good deal in its favor. It has a
“cleaner” balance-sheet than the management of ihe
old bank at first ventured to ask for. The original idea
was to wipe out the old paid capital arid alleged reserves,
convert some of the old deposits into shares, and lock up
the balance. The idea eventually adopted was to wipe
out not only the paid capital and fe&rves, but also one-
eighth of the old deposits and one-half the amount to be
called up from the old shareholders. Taking the figures
as at June 30,1909, this writing off would amount to the
following:—
Capital £154,570
Reserves .. .. .. 60,036
One-eighth of old deposits .. 456,259
Total £670,865
To that must be added the amount to be paid up by old
shareholders and written off—say, £190,000. The old
advances as at June, 1909, totalled £1,882,570. Thus
these old assets could be and no doubt were written
down to the extent of about 7s. 2d. in the £. The
prer'-r-'-s stood at £367,693. These apparently were not
\vrifß‘“ down at all: they stand now at £367,727. But
furth'** sums, to be paid hyoid shareholders, will be avail-
able for writing down : and this item may have some
attention.
As at June 30, 1909, the old deposits totalled
£3,750,068. One-fourth of the total of these deposits is now
represented by shares ; the balance of the capital is repre-
sented by the interest of the old shareholders in the
business. Approximately, the proportion is as follows :
Old shareholders .. .. .. .. £184,328
Old depositors .. .. .. .. 858,632
Total subscribed capital .. £1,042,960
Of the subscribed capital, in £1 shares, £958,172 is actu-
ally paid ; the balance of £84,788 is still to be got in by
instalments from the old shareholders. The authorised
capital is £2,000,000. There are tints 957,040 unallotted
shares. But until October next holders of the old
deposits have the right to exchange their deposits for
shares, so far as there is enough authorised capital to
satisfy the applications. The old deposits were disposed
of in this way
2/6 in the £ written off.
5/- ~ ~ shares.
2/6 ~ ~ 3 per cent, deposit for two years.
10/- ~ ~ inscribed deposits, 4 per cent.
It is the last two that maybe exchanged as mentioned
just now. This transaction means the swapping of a
practicallj 7 certain 3 or 4 per cent, for whatever dividend
the co. may be able to p:ij'. It will probably suit many
depositors ; and it will certainly be safer for the bank to
get rid of the liability by turning the debt into capital.
But while it will be safer for the bank, the enlargement
of the capital will of course have a steadying effect on
the rate of dividend.
The profit shown for the past half-year is
£11,670; and out of this a 3 percent, dividend is to he
paid, absorbing £8441. There is some doubt whether a
dividend should he paid. In ordinary circumstances,
some sort of a reserve should he built up first. But
the management may have reserves that they don’t
show. If they were satisfied to go ahead without writing-
off one-eighth of the deposits, then the position after the
writing-off of that half million or so should be exception-
ally strong. It is quite possible, therefore, that there
are reserves, represented by under-valuations in the
books. Further, it should suit the bank to get rid of
some of the liability previously mentioned; and the ex-
change to shares wouldn’t he likely in many cases if the
shares were not paying a dividend. On the whole, then,
if this paper were inclined to throw a brick at the A.B.C.
for paying a dividend, it would be the very softest one on
the premises.
The paid capital on June 30 was £958,172. The
liabilities were:—
Inscribed deposits .. ..£1,859,022
Special deposits (3 p.c.) .. 429,316
New deposits 1,751,994
Notes .. 79,447
Bills, etc 218,531
Total £4,338,310
Including the inscribed stock, which is not withdraw-
able, the bank has the use of £4 10s. 6d. of the public’s
money for every £1 of its own. Apart from the inscribed
stock, the bank owes the public £2,479,288, and it has no
less than £1,185,979 in cash and liquid assets. New de-
positors are therefore exceedingly well protected. The
bigness of the capital, which will prevent the rateofdivs.
from climbing to dizzy heights for many years to come,
is, on the other hand, the best guarantee that those who
drop their coin into the A.B.C. will be able to get it
when thev are entitled to it.

For the past three years profits have scarcely budged an
inch. Certainly, on the face of things, there seems to
have been a drop of £lO,OOO one term and then a re-
coverj 7 ; but that was due to the directors voting the
sum mentioned to set the Officers’ Provident Fund
on its feet. For the past six months there was no
forward movement at all; in i fact, the declared
profit was the least for 2£ years, although, the
bank should have automatically increased its aggre-
gate profits on account of having accumulated
more reserves. Nevertheless the directors pop up the
dividend 1 per cent, all round ; and that means a big
sum in a bank with so large a capital as the Victoria has
got. How this move will turn out in practice The Bul-
letin doesn’t profess to know. Theoretically it i& a
wrong step to take; for dividends should not be in-
creased on falling profits, especiallj 7 when the increased
div. absorbs very nearly 90 per cent, of the said pro-
fits. One thing may, however, be fairly said in favor of
the prisoner at the bar. The co.’s capital in proportion
to the business is unusually large, and there is conse-
quently less immediate call than there otherwise would
be for reserves. Yet the prudent investor is bound to
take into account the fact that, in order to pay the 6 per
cent., so much of the profit is absorbed that onlj 7 a paltry
£5672 is added to what is really an insurance fund for the
protection of capital. There is too big a tendencj 7 to dis-
regard the lay-by and to glue the eye only on the divi-
dend paid. Apart from everything else it is clear that
the bigger the lay-by the easier and more likely an in-
crease of dividend, and the less likely a reduction thereof.
As mentioned above, the capital is, compara-
tively speaking, very large. When the troubles hap-
pened in the early ’nineties the capital was £600,000 in
240,000 ordinary shares. This amount was added to (1)
by issuing £416,760 of pref. capital in exchange for
deposits, and (2) by calling up another £2 10s. per share.
Holders of 27,750 shares either couldn’t or wouldn’t pay
up, and thus the £79,584 of capital thej 7 had paid was
forfeited. Deposits have crept up steadily but not
rapidly; 10 years ago they were £4,646,794, and now
they are £6,014,441. The aggregate in proportion to capital
and reserves is still small. For every £1 the shareholders
have in the business the public (depositors, etc.)have £3
17s. 6d. That means comparatively small risks all round —
verj’small risksindeed for the depositors. But it also means
comparatively small profits for the shareholders. Some
big Australian banks have between £6 and £7 of deposits,
etc., for every £1 of capital and reserves; and though
that is verj 7 awkward for the shareholders when things
go wrong with the securities into which the money has
been put, it is very pleasant for them when things are
going- all right.. This is a case in which very big risks
don’t exist; wherefore the profit calculated on capital
and reserves combined has been modest: —

The bank has been letting out money prettj 7 freelj 7 , and
it is at present left with only 29 per cent, of its lia-
bilities represented by liquid assets. Under normal con-
ditions, that is not quite enough ; and with its present
resources the hank can’t safelj 7 increase its advances fur-
ther. No doubt, it hopes by putting up its dividend to
attract more deposits. Probably it will ; for in this
matter the foolish public is much more inclined to
rush along with its money to a bank which is paying
out a big dividend than to one which pays a small divi-
dend and puts by a large amount to strengthen its re-
serves, and thus guarantee the return of the depositors’
money. It, is a good deal like the folly which makes
people rush for an overerolvded tram, and hang on to it
by the skin of their teeth, when thej 7 might ride in much
more safety on the empty car that is bringing up the
rear.

“The recent coal strike entailed a loss to the co. of
over £70,000.” Nevertheless a profit of £lB,OBl is
shown. Which makes one wonder whether, if there
hadn’t been a strike, the directors would have shown a
profit of £BB,OBl. Judging bv their previous published
accounts thcv wouldn’t have done anything of the sort.
Further, though the co. only, made £lB,OBl of visible
profits for the half-j 7 ear, trie directors apparently
didn’t hesitate to pay away £62,400 in dividend.
The truth no doubt is that, if they felt that
way, the directors could generally show a great deal
more profit than they do show ; and what they don’t
show is being stuffed away into extensions of main and
plant, etc., and is regarded as’ written off to cover depre-
ciation. Doubtless it was because they had this internal
reserve that the directors didn’t hesitate to pay the big
dividend even though not one-third of it was visibly
earned. The same rate of div., by the wav, has been
paid for over 25 years ; and that the co. can go on paying
it in the face of a loss of over £70,000 through one trouble
is calculated to give shareholders a very comfortable
feeling.
Wliat amount of profit the co. does write off
ostensibly to cover depreciation it never even hints at.
Here, however, are a few comparisons. Seven years ago
the fixed investment item in the balance-sheet (buildings,
plant, land, mains, etc.) stood at £1,268,939. It now
stands at £1,243,886, or £25,053 less. Yet the “ sales of
gas, etc.,” for the past half-year were £291,734 as against
only £188,005 seven years ago. Either, therefore, the
old services supply over 55 per cent, more gas. etc., than
they did seven years ago, or a great many new ser-
vices have been provided. Anyway, there is the fact that
a plant alleged to be worth £25,053 less is supplying
over £200,000 worth more gas per annum. The most
reasonable explanation is that a great many new mains
and a great deal more plant have been movided, but
that the whole of it has been paid for out of revenue,
while £25,053 of revenue has also been written off the old
plant—in other words, has been put into stores and
liquid assets. One can’t say how well the co. has been
doing ; but it is pretty certain that the published ac-
counts err on the side of excessive modesty, and that
even if the co. did lose over £70,000, that and probably a
great deal more has been provided by the public during
the past few years, and has been stuffed away by the
directors to meet just such emergencies. In a manner
of speaking, therefore, the strike didn’t cost the co. any-
thing, since its div. wasn’t touched ; the bill was really
footed by the public.

The latest shuffle was explained six months ago. At that
time the capital was £200,000 in £1 shares. Having, how-
ever) something up their sleeve that thej 7 didn’t quite know
what to do with, the directors decided to issue another
100,000 shares, to be for the time being paid up to 10s. ;
and in case the emaciated shareholders might not know
where to turn for the 10s., the Board decided to pay 6s.
of it out of reserves. That left only 4s. per share of
new capital for the shareholders to actuallj’ supply. The
present capital is therefore £250,000, or, to be exact,
£249,965, some verj’ gradual gentleman having failed up
to June 30 to send along the odd £35. So the historj’ of
this Remarkable concern can be brought up to date this
way :
1893 Old co. in existence; capital, £40,000.
1894 Still the old co. ; capital increased to £60,000.
1899—New co. formed ; capital watered to £120,000.
1902—20,000 shares issued at par.
1906 20,000 free shares issued.
1907 40,000 shares issued at par.
1909 Authorised capital increased from £200,000 to
£400,000, to allow of more watering.
1910— 100,000 new shares issued; 6s. per share paid on
them as a bonus.
So, of the present capital of £250,000, only £140,000 has
been put up by shareholders, even counting the old co.’s
£60,000 as having been all actually paid by them, and
the 10 per cent. (£25,000 a year) is really 18 per cent.
After the recent watering transaction the re-
serves look wretchedly small. But the Sydney Ferries
don’t necessarily wear their ace of hearts on their sleeve ;
thej’ probably keep a good card or two inside it. Thus
“ship property” is valued in the books at £85,386 —
£85,386 for a mere matter of 27 passenger steamers, five
horse-ferries, and three cargo boats. It looks painfullj’
modest. In June, 1903, the capital was £140,000. Since
then £60,000 of capital has been got in ; and with that
£60,000 of capital plus profits, the co. has in seven years
added the Kulgoa, Kareela, Kaikai, Kailoa, Kirrule
(building), and a few other trifles ; has added £55.000
of landed propertj’; has put £6OOO into wharves, etc.
(probably more, but that is the added book value); and
has turned £7lBO of cash into £45,041 of cash and Gov-
ernment debentures. And, meantime, it has been
steadily paying out the 10 per cent. div. Exactly how well
the co. has done it would be impossible for the outsider
to say. The table at the head of these remarks probably
doesn't get near the truth because of the huge sums of
profit written off and never shown, allegedly to cover
depreciation. The foregoing hint as to the extent of
seven years’ hoarding gives the only suggestion possible
as to the real position.
Yet there are insects even in this ointment,
especially for the person who buys at present and pays a
price which sometimes doesn’t yield 3 per cent. If the
future were going to be anything like .he past, the stock
might be dirt cheap even if, to begin with, it only yielded
2 per cent. But things may wilt as well as grow. The
investor may not approve of Socialism anj r more than he
does of gout; but he is liable to both, just the same.
And there are hundreds of thousands calling themselves
Anti-Socialists who can’t stand the idea of the link
between the State railwaj’and tram sj’stemson the north
and south being in private, profit-grabbing hands. Of
course, the State isn’t at all likelj’to take without pay-
ing ; but, even though it pays full value for everything
tangible that it takes, it isn’t likely to pay for intangible
goodwill. The book value of the assets (less liabilities
and current dividend) is £266,552. The market value of
the stock is about £690,000. So the investor is paying at
the rate of £424,000 for something that is not in the
balance-sheet—either goodwill or something else. If the
State or the municipality doesn’t step in, he will no
doubt get his money back in time, with interest. If it
does step in—well, that’s where the gamble arrives.

This co. is getting pretty close to the end of its tether.
Twentj' years ago the N.S.W. Parliament gave it 28
years, suit, has only the odd eight left. The capital used
to he £200,000 ; but. as the table on top of these remarks
shows, the shareholders have already had £150,000 re-
turned. Yet. though the capital stands now at
£50,000, or ss. per share, the market values
the stock at the same old £1 or thereabouts.
At the end of eight years the whole undertaking
has to be passed over to the State. Besides cash and in-
vestments there will be a few assets which won’t have to
be handed over. Value these at £lO,OOO, and add
£54,194, the net value of the liquid assets, and there is
thus £64,194 available for the shareholders. To eventu-
ally return them the £1 per share at which the market
values the stock, about £136,000 will thus have to be gath-
ered in during the next eight j’ears—an average of
£17,000 a j'ear, in addition to the dividend, which
at the present rate is equal to 74 per cent, on £l.
In the latter years of the co.’s life its profits will also
most likely be seriously affected by the State water
supply which the Works Committee has agreed to recom-
mend. Meantime, however, the co. is quite capable of
putting up its price to the limit, as it did three or four
years ago when opposition seriously threatened, on the
plea that in the interests of shareholders it was compelled
to make hay before its sun was eclipsed. And, further,
of course, the earning of the necessary £17,000 a year
will be a much easier matter a few j r ears hence on ac-
count of the big interest receipts there will be by that
time. It is a scandalous tiling that any town should
have to pay so much for its water that the co.’s shares
should be quoted at 400 per cent, premium ; yet, though
there is a lot of the gamble about it, it is highly probable
that the gamblers who pay even that price will get their
money back and very good interest as well.

A southern trustee office has a northern branch ; but
the concern on the table is the only purelj' Queensland
trustee co., and with so big a field it ought to have a
rosy future, provided it doesn’t lose its head and rush up
its dividends. It isn’t only that a man wants to see a
co. possessed of good reserves before he makes
it his trustee and executor ; good reserves also generally
mean cautious management, and he naturally wants
his estate cautiously managed after he is dead and when
he can no longer retrieve mistakes. So far, Queensland
has no reason to complain of its trustee co. It got into
a difficult position, certainly’, after Queensland’s great
boom collapsed ; but it pulled itself through very well,
and the reserve additions during the past 10 years have
been at any rate reasonable. Further, the assets don’t
now appear to be of the very shrinkable variety ; the
only line about which there could be much doubt is—
“ Secured loans, investments, and sundTy debtors,
£7385.” The balance of the capital and reserves is practi-
cally all in public securities. The co. appears to deserve
its State’s confidence, and it is evidenrlj’ getting its
deserts ; for the trust estates in hand have increased
from £1,528,345 in 1908 to £2,135,244 in 1910, and that
ought to mean more profits, more reserves, still more
confidence, and still more business. Calculated on
capital and reserves combined, profits are only moderate
as trustee cos.’ profits go ; last year they amounted to
£8 12s. 4d. .percent. But a high rate of profits isn’t
what testators want in a trustee co. In fact, a high rate
of profit may result from excessive charges, or from small
capital in proportion to the trusts in hand ; and those
are certainly not features that should strike the testator
favorably when he is looking for a trustee.

“Owing to the high price of ingredients which ruled
during the greater portion of the half-year, the profits
have been adversely affected,” the directors report.
Curiously, however, the managing director of another
hotel and catering concern, the Hotel Metropole, told his
shareholders the other day that profits were a bit better
because prices had favored them. Whatever the
explanation, the profit figures of Sargents, Ltd.,
are moving down in a way that ought to
make various people restless: £6865, £6433, and
£6038 isn’t the right kind of progress, especially in
view of the large increase of capital, which ought to
mean more and not less profit. The view this paper put
forward at the outset was that the co. w'as over-capital-
ised, and that the business was asked to carry far too much
“paper” in the shape of goodwill. Not one penny has
yet been written off that item, which still stands at the
original £45,000; j 7 et, judging by the decrease of profits,
goodwill isn’t worth what it was 18 months ago. It is
quite true that the bun-shop business show’s huge profits
occasionally. Lyons’s, in London, lately reported over a
million sterling profit for the year—a fact which was
carefully passed on to Australian investors and specula-
tors. But the sudden and tremendous success of Lyons’s
proves how erratic a thing this bun-shop business is,
and how uncertain a thing bun-shop goodwill is; for
while Lyons’s has gone up like a rocket, others, whose
lunches'were in everybody’s mouth a few years ago, have
come down like a stick. It is too early jet to say
whether Sargents is going to be the rocket or the stick ;
but the profits certainly are not so far behaving as a
self-respecting rocket should.

These figures (reserves included) are as at the date of the

dend. In 10 years £22,103 has been added to the reserves,
and if that money earns 7 per cent, it is enough to pay
2 per cent, on the capital. In other words, other things
being equal, the co. can as easily pay 12 per cent, now as
it could pay 10 per cent. 10 years ago, assuming that its
money earns,7 per cent. Still, the additions to reserves
have been small lately ; and, unless the co. has been
doing a great deal of internal writing down, it will
have to show better results to justify the dividend
it is paying at present. Probably it knows that, and for
that reason brands 2£ per cent, of the dividend as
“bonus.”
• * * *
Holders of copper scrip felt a shade better last
week on cable news that American producers were going
to knock 12£ per cent, off their output, which means
about 7 per cent, off the output of the world. Copper
went just past £llO in 1907, and has been creeping down
ever since, until now it is at about £55, or exactly half of
the top price ; and the cost of production hasn’t eased
at all. The distressing foundation fact is that between
1902 and 1908 the production of the metal moved
up from 541,100 tons to 734,545 tons, and is still
moving up. It is true that many old mines,
which were reworked a few years ago, have fallen
idle again ; but there are many other mines, like those at
Cloncurry, which were opened at that time and have still
to turn out their blocks of rich surface ore. To reduce
the output of American mines by 124 per cent, may stop
the fall; but on the statistics—which admittedly' are
tricky things to stand on—it doesn’t look as if it could
send the price up very far. For it has to be remembered
that the present is only a poor price compared with the
high prices of the 1906-7 boom years : before that mining
engineers had for a long while mostly figured on copper
at £56.
* * * *
A serious tone prevails in Broken Hill mining
circles to-day. But there is one source of joy ; and that
is the Murex process plant, which has been erected with
great solemnity on the bottom floor of the Block 14 mill.
In London, where directors see things “as through a
glass, darkly,” there is said to be a belief that the
process is revolutionising ore concentration in Broken
Hill, and that fat dividends are going to be paid out of
the results of the treatment of 1000 tons of Block 14 sul-
phides. Locally a different view is taken. Months haX e
elapsed since the unit was erected—it is only a pocket
plant, anyhow—and nobody seems to know w'hether the
machine is refining fat for the manufacture of candles or
is simply mixing oil for the lubrication of Block 14
machinery. It is about time, anyhow, that the practical
results of some of the ore-dressing work were made
known.
* * * *
Theß. H. Junction has shut down, ostensibly
for a few weeks, to allow of certain repairs to the shaft
and alterations to the mill. The latter include the in-
stallation of three more grinding pans. This work can
be completed in three weeks ; bht what then ? There is
a little financial bother, and this is going to take more
than three weeks to settle up. Shareholders will be
lucky if they see the mine working again within three
months.
* * * *
At the B. Hi South Extended good progress is
being made with the east crosscut, which is to prove the
mine another Broken Hill Prop. The same thing has
been said about a dozen abandoned “shows” around
Broken Hill, and it will go on being, said about many
more as long as there is any smoke coming out of Broken
Hill chimney-stacks. The “ South Extended lode ’’—-that
is, the big lode, of which nobody knows anything at pre-
sent—should be cut very shortly. If it is proved that it
really does exist, there will be a flutter along the White
Leads country that stretches out towards the Pinnacles.
*■ * * *
Main Ridge (N.S.W.) has had itrfirst crushing
of wash, and has cleaned up 790 z. from 203 tons. This
supports engineer Nardin’s estimate of 7dwt. ; it works
out, in fact, at 7dwt. 18gr. Also, the very small loss of
7gr. in the tailings is satisfactory. Now, therefore, it is
a matter of costs—and not so much 'treatjfient dosts
either as mining costs, which are still the ipost doubtful
feature of the business. /
* * *
J. C. Watson, P. W. Tewksbury and R. C.
Dixson have done just the sort of thing that might have
been expected of them. People bought shares in the
Mynpachts Dredging Co., not because they knew any-
thing about the concern, but because they knew a good
deal about the men behind it. The plaims, on which
J. C. Watson sweated off a couple of stone weight in
S’Africa, didn’t come up to expectations ; and the direc-
tors decided to pull out before the co.’s money was gone.
Then, having a dredge on hand, they looked round for a
place to put it; and Tewksbury assigned an option he
had over half of the leases of the Afaluen Red bank
Co. Then, further, so that the people who had
bought contributing shares and shares at a premium in
the Mynpachts might lose as little as possible, the three
decided to transfer to them a block of their own shares,
equivalent in face value to the amount contributed. That
done, everybody—promoters and contributors—will be
on exactly the same footing. The new co. will be called
the Victorian-Araluen Co., in 30,000 shares, 10,000 of
which will go to the Redbank Co., and the balance will
be available for shareholders in the Mynpachts Co. The
Redbank Co. paid £5600 in divs. in the past 12 months ;
and there is no apparent reason why the new co., with
its powerful dredge, shouldn’t do quite as well.
* * * *
Boulder Perseverance (W.A.) has resumed ore-
treatment ; but as the solutions will probably absorb
over IOOOoz. to start with, the first month’s return from
the reorganised mill isn’t likely to boom the shares. The
new plant is officially described as interesting from a
technical point of view, for the reason that Krupp have
been substituted for Griffin mills, and tube mills added
in preference to extra grinding pans. It has a bigger
crushing capacity than the old one ; but as the roasting
furnaces can’t make a good job of more than 20,000 tons
a month, it is improbable that any attempt will be made
to handle more than this quantity.
Retiring superintendent Doolette estimates the
actual ore reserves in Boulder No. 1 Co.’sSt. George mine
(W.A.) at 16,000 tons, worth 7Jdwt. In backing up this
estimate, general-manager Hamilton says it is conserva-
tive, and adds that the payable ore occurs so irregularly
in the very wide lode channel that a lot of prospecting
work is needed to find it. He opines, however, that all
the pay stuff has not yet been found in the levels already
opened up, and that further development will reveal it.
As regards the co.’s Kalgoorlie lease, the chairman
mentioned at the recent annual meeting 1 that this pro-
perty would get the whole of the underlay of the feouth
Kalgurli’s western lode at 1500 ft., and that later on some
working arrangement for dealing with the ore would
probably be made with the latter concern.

Oroya Links (W.A.) is beginning to find its
level. The Oroya-Brown Hill section of tne property is
about depleted ; atid as the Central Boulder block is in a
similar plight, the co. will have to shortly fall back on
the Golden Links for sole support. Unfortunately the
latter is not developed to stand the strain of furnishing
the present output of 11,000 tons a month, so a drop in
the tonnage is inevitable, and the probability of the grade
coming down on top of this has caused a lot of nervous
holders to get out.
* * , * *
The apparently hopeless task of making the old
General Gordon (W.A.) pay still keeps the Sirdar Co.’s
nose to the grindstone. For the half-year to the end of
Jane, 5810 tons were raised and treated for £4892; and,
by the co.’s own system of accountancy, a net profit of
£490 is shown. But, with all outgoings charged up, this
surplus is turned into a deficiency of £454. The cash
balance on June 30 amounted to £173 ; but, as the co.
owed £BlO to sundry creditors and hasn’t paid off any-
thing since, its financial position is, for a small concern,
decidedly “ rocky.” Both directors and manager look
forward to a “ profitable return ” from the current half-
year’s operations, and the shareholders are entitled to get
all the comfort they can out of that statement.
* * * *
With its customary backwardness, Peak Hill
(W.A.) has just issued its report for the year ended
June, 1909. This shows that the co. had got still deeper
in debt; and as things have since gone from bad to
worse, time is again asked to pay interest on the prior
lien bonds and first mortgage debenture stock. The
mine is. done; and further work simply means further-
waste.
* * * *
Preparations are being made to tap holders of
Gwalia Consolidateds for more money. The failure of
this co. to make a do of things, in spite of enormous ex-
penditure and favorable reports by mining experts and
others, has been frequently alluded to on this page; so
further reference to the worn-out subject is superfluous.
Perhaps some day the show will be made to pay ; but no
one in the West expects to see the co. get back what it
and its predecessors put into the mine and its patchwork
equipment.
* * * *
The old Santa Claus at Randells (W.A.) is to
be looked at by Hans Irvine with a view to forming a co.
to work it. This mine has been tackled off and on for
some years past, but up to the time the last co. gave up
the struggle it had cost considerably more to get the gold
than the stuff was worth. It would take at least £20,000
to prove and properly equip the property; and in view of
that fact, coupled with past failures, the experts will no
doubt look very earnestly at the property before recom-
mending it.
* * * *
Hampden, Cloncurry (Q.), reports that the rise
680 ft. north of No. 2 shaft has holed through to the level
above. Assays have been running 10 per cent, copper
over a width of 3£ft. Another rise 910 ft. north of the
shaft is up 60ft., and prospects 6£ per cent, over a width
of less than 2ft. At the Duchess mine, the north drive
continues to carry low-grade ore (about 1 per cent,
copper), but the rise started 220 ft. north of the shaft is
showing some good prospects. The rise at 21ft. showed
bornite and copper pyrites, and assayed 19J per cent,
over a width of 3ft. The latest telegram states that these
rich assays continue to 30ft. above the rise. Nearly
everywhere the rich stuff in both mines is narrow ; they
contain a great deal of very low-grade ore, with a con-
centration of copper in a comparatively small area. And
Hampden, it has to be remembered, is a quarter-million
co.
* * * *
New Brilliant Freehold, Charters Towers,
crushed 1046 tons for £3494, making £7173 in four weeks.
But some people think they can see the end of these
good crushings, for the market is weak, partly in conse-
quence of the drop in the value of the stone treated and
partly because the bottom levels do not look too bright.
Still the reef is over 1000 ft. long in N.B.F. ground, so there
is plenty of scope for developments at depth. Quality
varies from £l4 to as many shillings, which makes it
hard to value reserves.
* * 4 *
New Einasleigh (North Queensland) is sup-
posed to have 141,500 tons of reserves, half of them
valued at 8 per cent. New financial arrangements in-
clude an advance of £30,000 to help to pay for
plant and equipment. An option has also been given
over a block of 67,500 shares; but the right to call on
these shares at par is not likely to be exercised unless
the market shows better form. All that can be said
definitely is that recent development's have increased the
reserves, and that the co. is paying its way with its ship-
ments of 7 per cent. ore.
* * * *
For the last four weeks Chillagoe (Q.) did its
best for this year ; 5990 tons ore gave blister and bullion
containing 249 tons copper, 342 tons lead, 50,5060 z. silver
and 13780 z. gold. The co. is now tapping the Etheridge
regularly, but it isn’t to have things all its own way, for
the Sulphide Corporation is on the field as a buyer of
concentrates. The Chillagoe railway returns now include
the traffic over the Etheridge extension, so there are
more costs to set against last month’s good-looking rail-
way revenue ; and the debenture interest has also in-
creased for the same reason.
* * * *
Costs at the Birthday Tunnel, Berringa (Vic.),
got down last half-year to Bs. 2d. a ton, made up of
mining 4s. Ujd., haulage Is. 7d., and crushing Is. 7jd.
This is less than 2dwt. ; and seeing that 11,672 tons were
treated, and mining was done down to 700 ft., it is a bit,
of a record. The fire in December 'stopped work till
March, when stoping- was resumed. The 1 only spot that
looked well was in the north end, but the poverty even
there can be judged from the fact hereinbefore hinted at
that 11,672 tons only yielded £5778 worth of gold—practi-
cally 10s. per ton. Though the paid-up capital was only
£12,500, altogether £231,520 of gold has been got out of
the mine, and £66,000 has been paid in divs. Revenue
has, however, been drawn upoti almost to the last shilling
to disburse profits, and now the co. is in debt £2300 and
needs many things. Well, that is the way of most Vic-
torian cos. None the less, the Rake’s Progress is not fair
to the manager or to the industry. If 10 per cent, had
been cut out of profits for a reserve, £6600 would
have been to the credit of the fund, and scrip
would not have run down just because some
worn-out machinery had been blotted out by a
fire. Imagine what will happen if the crosscuts now
being put, out 200 ft. below the No. 8 level hit poor stuff
right off in the Tunnel and the Kangaroo lod s. It would
be quite in keeping with the history of Berringa if this
happened, and yet the only provision for a trouble of the
kind is a bundle of debts.
* * * *
The manager of the South German, Maldon
(Vic.), is heard piping : “ Please give me a new battery."
In return, the shareholders pipe : “ Please give us a
decent reserve of stone first.” There is justice in both
demands, but Maldon folk don’t like paying Ihe needful
calls. They won’t see that if they want stone opened up
and crushed cheaply they must put up cash for dead
work and plant. A diamond drill scheme is on the
tapis for the South German, to find out what has be-
come of the shoot of gold that cut out on the bound-
ary of the Deep lease, as well as to do prospect-
ing at other levels. Minister Mcßride ought to spare
a plant from the Powlett now that he has. got things
in full swing there. A strike at Maldon would open up a
lot of pockets for calls that have been closed to Castle-
maine, Bendig-o and Ballarat. Finances cannot improve,
though, until shareholders give the South German
managet a chance to deal with low-grade ore. That
means recognising that the mine is a poor proposition
and that bulk must be handled.

If Senator Clemons is satisfied with the form in
which the accounts of the Tasmanian Copper Co. are
sent out to Australian shareholders, it is a pity he ever
got the control of the office shifted from London to
Launceston. If he is satisfied with the result of the past
year's work, or with the general outlook in respect to
the process introduced at Rosebery (Tas.) by lawyer
Thyrine, of Queensland, then doubly is it a pity. A loss
of £5168 sums up 1909. Lately, it seems, Thynne has
done with the process co., so Horwood may yet
get his foot in with his process. There ought
to be something in the mine. It has 250,000 tons
proved, and it has been sending ore averaging oTsoz.
gold, 13'450z. silver, 9TI per cent, lead and 30 per cent,
zinc per ton to the smelter. This is not the poorest ore
in the mine by a long way ; but, with anything like the
recovery and the costs claimed by Horwood, even lower-
grade stuff would give a profit. Everything depends to-
day on the way the Londoners, who are supposed to be
putting up the money for the Extraction Co.’s bi-sulphite
process, like the outlook now that Thynne has gone out.
Much of the dead work has been done, so it would be a
pity if they should cry enough when the world wants to
see if their promises are of the piemrust order.
