Mfc. Bischoff (Tas.) is a fine example of a mine,
supposed to be worked out, resurrected by modern
management with the aid of a favorable metal market.
So far from keeping its mind fixed upon the graveyard,
as it used to do a few years ago, the co. has burst out into
new equipment, to which 10 stamps have just been added,
while an aerial tram service is to follow. In the past six
months £25,760 of profit was made ; £lB,OOO w'as divided,
£4585 written off, and £3175 added to reserves.
* * * *
Tongkah Harbor having dodged round an
awkward corner, the Age, with sweet veracity, calls out
that shareholders are eating- their tongues out with im-
patience to get David Currie’s report on the co.’s claim.
Melbourne hasn’t got half a dozen decent-sized holders
of Tongkahs. Hobart and Sydney nurse that baby.
What shareholders in those places want to know
most is how long after the experts got en-
throned at Tongkah was the first condemnatory
wire sent by any one of the party to Collins-
street. Further, was such wire handed on instanter
to the Board? Most people had forgotten that Currie
ever wrote a report. Even if they recalled the fact they
knew that he could not break fresh ground after the story
of J. L. Wharton, his colleague, had been printed. So
no matter what the Age may say or the Leader may tip,
no one is worrying much about Currie’s report, except
the man who is short of scrip and who doesn’t like the
prospect of the new dredges being landed at Tongkah
before he can cover up. What the Board ought to do is
to get a report from Boyd, the co.’s old second in com-
mand, who, having worked the ground, and more or less
bored the harbor, can tell from hard experience whether
occasional boring is trustworthy, and whether he thinks
the show the “goner ” that the visitors assumed.
Waihi Grand Junction (M.L.) cables the news
to J. Bull, who owns most of it, that a make of quartz
50ft. wide is showing in the crosscut from No. 5 level
(698 ft.). The Royal reef has been a consistent producer,
but has never shown such a great width before. It is
the Martha that has been mainly responsible for the out-
put from Waihi for so many years. But forcing the pace
and the payment of large dividends carried Waihi shares
up to a high price, arid at the first sign of poverty in the
deep levels there was a bad slump. This development in
the Royal reef may' shake up the market again, because
the Waihi. has been contemplating a crosscut near the
Grand Junction’s boundary.

The N.S.W. mining law is a tangle full of
knots, but barrister R. E. Kemp has straightened it out
in “ The Law of Mines and Minerals,” published by the
Law Book Co. All the Acts are printed, with notes,
decided cases, and a valuable index.

* * * *
S. Aus. has a wild hope that it has—somewhere,
in parts unknown—-a gold mine at last. The perversity
of Providence in placing all the payable gold shows out-
side its border's in every direction has long worried the
Ceil i ral State, so it was ready to have its leg pulled when
a half-caste with the suggestive name of Joe Miller pro-
duced a nice 20oz. nugget, and told his story of a Magic
Cave. This was at Denial Bay, a port far out west, and
the cave is placed about 460 miles up country. “ Pendent
from the roof was a mass of gold like a bunch of grapes,
which could be rocked to and fro with the band, but
could not be dislodged. The natives regarded it as sacred,
arid worshipped it. No lubra was allowed to approach
within half a mile of the cave on pain of death.” It
sounds as if J. Miller had been to see the “Aladdin”
pantomime; or he may have been reading a 20-vear-old
novel, The Wings of Silence,” by’ a local writer, George
Cossins. Anyway, the grape-bunch nugget legend is
common among the natives, and various parties are going
out per camel to give S.A.’s craving for a real goldfield
one more chance.
* * * *
Some mining cos. are whales on decimals.
Mungana (Queensland) frequently carries its calcu-
lations to four decimal places. A recent fortnight’s output
appeared in one of the papers as 1303'8375 tons.
* * *
After some pretty hard fighting, Wallaroo
Phosphate Co. (S. Aus.) announces a dividend of 6 per
cent. This co., which works on friendly terms with the
big Wallaroo Copper Co., has put up a sulphuric acid
plant capable of producing 10,000 tons a year, and it,
together with the plant operated by the Wallaroo Copper
Co., gives a total supply of 15,000 tons. Besides some local
product, the Phosphate Co. is importing shiploads of
pyrites from Spain, where the stuff can be bought for about
£1 per ton. There is plenty of dense pyritic ore in Aus-
tralia just as good for acid manufacture as the Spanish,
but the cost of transport blocks it. The acid is used
mainly’to make super-phosphate and other fertilisers, a
business which is growing big in Victoria and South Aus.
* * * *
Every engineer in charge of factories and mills
should give close attention to the power wasted in trans-
mission of power. The Hyatt Flexible Roller Bearings
save 20 per cent, power and 75 per cent, of oil, so one of
the first duties is naturally for engineers to direct their
special attention to these bearings and to car boxes for
trucks. Hyatts are in continuous use in Cement Works,
Ore Dressing Mills, Factories, etc., in Australia. Bush-
ings of flexible rollers may be applied to loose pulleys or
truck wheels, etc. Should engineers require further
details, they can apply' to the Australian Agents, W. &
J. Lempriere, Melbourne; or E. W. llursthouse & Co.,
of Wellington, the Agents in New Zealand.*

Individual Treatment works wonders. Hundreds

hairs have mostly fallen out, but those that remain are
getting their color back very noticeably, and the
whole of it looks lovely and bright and glossy, and I
am Indeed most pleased and satisfied.”— Mrs. R.
Springsure, Queensland.

ROBUR
It’s all nonsense for anybody
to say that the public don’t
know when tea is good value or
when it isn’t —you’ve only got
to note how Robur has “ caught
on” to be convinced on that
point —the customers say they
like it because it has a distinct
flavour —but the truth is they
prefer it because it is good value
—to my mind no tea in the
place shows better value for the
money. And that’s why there’s
more of it sold than all the
others put together.
I’ve had a lot of experience
and I’ve tried hundreds of times
to get my customers to take
other teas —and even now, when
a new tea comes along, 1 get
a little of it and push it out
with the hope that some of my
customers will like it and use it
instead of Robur because
theres’s so little profit on Robur
—but they don’t seem to stick
to new brands for longpr
they always go back to Robur,
and l don’t blame ’em.
1 reckon the No. 2 Grade, is
a good family tea, but the No. 1
Grade is the tea 1 would buy
if 1 were you.
The Grocer.
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE.”
A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, August 2, 1910.
My Dear Arini,—
The Governor of Victoria and Lady Carmichael
came over the border on Thursday last, and are
now staying at Federal Government House. A
Melbourne woman who travelled on the same
train lias been telling me things about Lady Car-
michael, and declares that she is one of the most
level-headed vice-regal women ever imported
here. Her “ Lady Carmichael Guild ” is a
society of the highest practical usefulness ; also it
is run on thoroughly business lines. Having
for its head a Scotswoman it naturally would
be. _ Its aim is to put home industries on a paying
basis, and it takes the little industries, one at a
time, and puts them on the basis firmly and
gently, while the other industries wait on the
mat until their turn comes. It was found, for
instance, that several thousands of pounds’ worth
of Irish crochet lace are annually imported into
Victoria, which money might be spent in the State
if the lace were made on the premises. Women
are making more indifferent crochet lace all
over Australia than any other thread tangle-
ment that was ever taken up as a means
of slaying time. A visit to any up-country
show proves that the will to make lace is
here, also the energy to evolve miles of it,
also the ignorance of how to do it well. The
Guild claims to teach the art and also to place the
work when it is worth placing. The Arts and
Crafts Guild works with the League, and they
have got into touch with the trade. Consequently
the local lace industry is beginning to get on that
paying basis aforementioned. Until that is done,
no other industry need put its head round the
door and ask, “Is it my turn now?” Mrs.
Clapp, Mrs. McDevitt, Miss Mary Chomley and
Mrs. Shields are helping the work along.
Another Government House guest just now is
Governor Harry Barron, of Tasmania. He is
one of the new school of working State Governors,
as distinguished from the old ornamental or hen-
raising school of which the bygone Augustus
Loftus was a specimen—Governors who try hard
to make it appear as if the figurehead were in
reality part of the engine of the ship of State.
His Ex. is game to open more sheep-dog trials in
a given time than any other viceroy of his weight
and proportionate salary throughout the Com-
monwealth. As for church fairs, he revels in
them. He has come solus, leaving behind him his
Missus, who is the despair of overworked A’s.D.C.
because she will do unconventional things.
Mount Kosciusko is the happy hunting-ground
of good fashionables in these winter days. Some
of the carnivallers who took part in the fancy
costume ball and other jinks have returned to
town, and they tell of gay doings among the
snows, and report that Nature’s refrigerating
plant is in good order. Everything that could
freeze was “ friz ” hard the night of the ball, and
among the attractions were fireworks on the
ice. Pretty little Lady Morvyth Ward,
with a governess, is sampling our local
Switzerland just now. She is a fetching
youthful skateress in white serge, jaunty cap and
woolly “ sports ” coat. All the women are wearing
those long tight-knitted coats, from neck to
ankle. They look like a stocking stretched over
a carpet broom. So a man told me, and on that
unreliable authority I pass on the fashion note.
The men’s ski-ing club has a uniform of white
sweater, and knickers, with red cap and facings.
The hotel is very full of guests, and bookings are
already made for next winter by reckless persons,
who may be sampling a very different climate
when the date falls due. A lover of things on
ice is purser Williamson, of the Mongolia, and he
has passed a word of wonderment on the clever-
ness of the authorities who cater on such lavish
lines in the midst of a wilderness. As an expert,
his approval is worth having.
There are plenty of pretty girls to play about
with, and this abundance of girl makes Kosciusko
life attractive this season. The belle is, by almost
unanimous vote, Miss Beetham, a gilded maiden
from Maoriland, who is under the wing of Mrs.
Bidwell. Then there are Miss Eileen Maiden,
who is a leading skateress; and Misses Bell, Irene
Lees and two Knoxes. Other visitors include
Mrs. and Miss Burdekin, Mrs. Alec Hay, Dr.
Graham Bell and Mrs. Bell, Mr. and Mrs.
Neville Dangar, some O’Donnells of Min-
gay (well-known squatting folk), the Hud-
sons of Burwood, the Wunderlichs, Dr. and
Mrs. Cooley, the Playfairs, Phizackerleys and
McCathies. From these names you may judge that
no Sydney guest could feel lonesome at the Polish
mountain. You are bound to meet somebody you
know. One of the best catches in Australia is
there to give the girls a treat —“ Gam ” Walker,
who generally lives with his relatives, the Dr.
Lamrocks, of Kogarah. He is not yet 21, and he
will have an unwieldy income when he comes of
age (from Walker’s whisky, and he is the only
one in it). Ocher men are Dr. Paul and Dr.
Schlink. These two belong to the ski club, and
are regular visitants.
“ Cam ” Walker, who has been spending a short
time at Kosciusko, as above alleged, is leaving on
Saturday, per Otranto, for the land where his
money comes from. Some of his fellow students
(medicals) gave him a farewell dinner at Paris
House on Saturday night.
The great social test nowadays is at Bose Bay
Links. The Golf Club House is on the hill which
climbers are for ever striving to mount, both
literally and the other way. It requires some
nerve to walk up the long path to the pavilion
when hundreds of coldly critical society starers
are gazing stonily at the intruder. To give a golf
tea is now the summit of social desire. Mrs.
H. G. Greene gave a tea last week, which prodded
the family into the halls of fashion for keeps, and
shut the door behind them with a loud, firm clang
on the outer world of nobodies. The Greenes are
“new” people, dating from the season before
last. They live at Dunara, the big house among
many other large abodes in Wolseley-road.
Mr. and Mrs. Arthur Cooper gave a reception
on Wednesday evening at the Australia behind
the grille, which the manager of that hostelry
always erects on the winter-garden balcony when
revels are held upon which the profane vulgar
may not gaze. At the Cooper reception the grille
looked like a bower of roses by Bendemeer’s
stream. All Sydney was bidden—that is, all
Sydney that counts. The remaining half-million or
so don’t count—they merely exist. Some of
the existers—and a tidy few at that—pay
rent to the Coopers for the privilege of
existing on Cooper land, for the family owns
so much of the earth around “our” (note the
irony of the quotes) harbor, that the place might
just as well be called Port Cooper for short.
Arthur Cooper, when he’s at his own domicile,
lives in England, where he married a wife. His
mother was a Miss Hill, of Sydney, and sister of
Mrs. Macnamara, that well-known and popular
society matron, the mother of handsome twin
daughters who were married simultaneously.
Either housekeeping is a surprising business in
Canada or else a lady who recently wrote me from
there is an astonishing Sapphira. She was going
to give a party. The previous night it froze to
some small extent, and she was awakened by
little tinkling sounds, like those that Matthias
heard in “The Bells.” As it was the depth of
winter she did not go down to investigate.
In the morning the mystery was explained. Be-
fore every shelf in the pantry were neat little
piles of powdered glass that had once been bottles.
Champagne, whisky and all the other drinks
stood up frozen in bottle form, and had to be
broken with a hammer. It seemed rather a good
story to me.
The coming season is likely to bring much joy
to the hearts of dry cleaners, also much grief to
women who don’t want to spend too much on the
rather expensive practice of having gowns
cleaned by a dry blow instead of in the family
wash tub. Foulard and crepon are already de-
cided upon as the leading materials for spring and
summer frocks, and this means less washing but
not less cleaning. The lingerie gowns of last
summer were as easy to get up as the nighties
which they so much resembled. Crepon and
foulard crumple just as easily and look just as
touzled after a day’s wear as the muslin and
linen confections, but the home laundry is not
for them. Only the woman who doesn’t worry
about paying her bills is feeling happy at this
change of fashion.
Weekly meetings are held to push forward the
Lorna Pounds benefit, which will come off some
time early in September. Joseph Carruthers was
bagged by the committee last week, and he pro-
mises to help as much as he can. Also other in-
fluentials were “among those present.” Mrs.
Shaw, of Ashfield, gave a function at which Lorna
and her sister shone, and it is expected that so
many friends as the fortunate Lorna possesses
will not fail to roll up at the right time. Mrs.
Shaw is the woman who went on a pa sear in
Japan a year or so back with her daughters, and
lectured pleasingly on the subject of Nippon at
several women’s clubs on her return.
The returned “ Akenehi ” has many wonderful
stories to tell of wanderings in the moist and
spicy lands to the north of us. In Port Moresby
she met with Mrs. Lett, whom Sydney folk will
know better as Florence Everett, a girl who worked
hard with the pen and at the piano. Eventually
she turned press agent for Maskelyne and Devant,
and left these parts for England, home and a hus-
band just in time to see the Messina earthquake,
which she described for one of our papers. Then
she was married to her Mr. Lett, who is in the
rubber business, and they now live in one room
at Port Moresby. Houses are very scarce there,
it seems, and have to be let in single-room flats.
Mr. and Mrs. Lett are apparently enjoying life
immensely, and flourishing like the green bay
rubber-tree.
The society which exists to befriend animals
had an annual meeting last week, and an exhi-
bition of horrors displaying the different ways of
torturing beasts now most in vogue. But the
society missed a notable exhibit—a short, three-
act tragedy of bird-life, now shown in Mrs. Ellis
Rowan’s collection. The first picture (Act
I. shows the white heron on her nest. Act
11. pictures the bird lying dead, torn and
bleeding, where the exquisite feathers that form
the coveted ospreys of the fashionable woman
have been wrenched from the beautifully helpless
creature by a so-called civilised human being. Act
111. depicts three baby egrets slowly starving to
death in the nest to which the parent birds will
never return. To see these three little pictures is
to shudder at the thought of beholding an osprey
stuck in a woman’s hair. They are no morbid
exaggerations, but were painted by Mrs. Rowan
from photographs taken by Arthur Mattingly on
a Murray station belonging to a relative of Mrs.
Rowan. When staying at this place on the
Murray, the artist saw hundreds of these birds
lying dead on the ground after the slaughterers
had been busy. Next year the herons were almost
extinct. Is there any wonder ?
Every night is like a first night at “The
Arcadians.” That tuneful and cheery piece is a
boom. You feel as soon as you enter the crowded
theatre that you are in a happy land. The
atmosphere diffused all around the auditorium is
brisk and exhilarating. Women wear their best
theatre frocks and chatter pleasantly in the in-
tervals when they are not laughing at that jewel
of a jockey, Peter Doody. Old fogies Say this
opera reminds them of the days of the first Gaiety
Co. and the late but very much lamented Fred
Leslie, while the young and giddy playgoers are
content to make up theatre-parties that sparkle
at subsequent suppers. Maie Sydney is an
Arcadian in green and white lattice work and
sandalled trilbies ; and Florence Gilliam is a
fetching mundane in white, with a blue scarf,
which she knows how to wear. The pro-
gramme shouts aloud the names of the
front-rank costume-builders who supplied the
gowns that grace the racecourse lawn, but
they look as if the good old evergreen dressmaker
had" turned them out as per usual. For they are
distinctly not epoch-making, and only for the
megaphone aforesaid, would pass muster as nice
graceful gowns. When attention is thus forced
upon them, the still small voice of truth compels
the statement that they are just “ or’nary.” One
night last week a girl sat on the rocky seat that
looks o’er the stage from the centre of the D.C.,
and drew all eyes. The magnet was her hair.
Was it the latest coiffure from Paris or was it a
doormat? So gazers pondered, and most decided
it was odds on the latter supposition. I simply
couldn’t attempt to describe the construction.
An older companion looked like a matronly
geisha dressed for the part by a French maid.
Fashion is more freakish than ever. The
Turkish togs worn in “ The Brass Bottle,” at Her
Majesty’s, would not be so very unlike the mode
of the moment. Judging by what one reads of
the recent Grand Prix race-meeting at Long-
champs (France), the gowns thereat were as sensa-
tional as Lemberg’s defeat. The women wore
“audacious hats surmounted by lovely feathers.”
One young woman, who attracted much
attention, wore a confection of white and
gold, which, from the waist down, closely
resembled a Turkish lady’s dress. And as
she hobbled about in the paddock, with her
feet imprisoned at the ankles, quite a crowd fol-
lowed her, anticipating her immediate downfall.
It is the “tie band” round the skirt, already
seen in Sydney at the preliminary spring fashion-
peeps, which accomplishes this hobble. On the
imported model costumes there seems to be too
much top-hamper above the knees. Then they
take a reef or two in the mainsail, throw a line
around, and make all fast with a clove hitch.
This is a sailor man’s description of the new
spring styles, and it’s just as explanatory as any-
thing a dressmaker will tell you.
The most sensational event in “ The Brass
Bottle ” at Her Majesty’s is Dora Rignold’s white
gown, which looks as if she hadn’t anything else
on. Her tall and willowy figure is apparently
elongated by about 3ft. more. She has added
several superfluous cubits to her stature by wear-
ing that tightly-fitting robe. The women of the
cast are well robed. Mrs. Futvoye is a stylish
mother-iri-law that any man might be proud of. A
well-worn plum-colored tunic with a deep fringe on
the same colored skirt suggests silk cashmere
in its soft drapings. Apart from the Turkish-
bath-like gorgeousness of the palace, sent as a
surprise packet by the Jinnee to Horace Venti-
more, the two prettiest scenes are the Futvoye
drawing-room and the Savoy “Pinafore” suite.
In the drawing-room there is more comfort, if less
cushion, than in the gorgeous Bagdad apartment,
and the western mode of eating is de-
cidedly cleaner than the dish-grabbing -form
of meal practised by the sons of Islam.
Though, as a freak dinner, the Jinnee’s feast
would be an improvement on some American
notions in that line. Anyhow, the coffee
must be all right. As the mysterious stranger who
was so fond of bottles that he lived in one for
thousands of years, Evelyn Howard looks his
part in a misty green bath robe and a largo coni-
cal turban. The fashions in Jinn land don’t alter
much in a millennium or two.
Yours, as of old,
CLEO.
“ Permanent ” writes
In its annual report the N.S.W. Public Service Associa-
tion is making 1 the dust fly with its feet because there are
2000 temporary employees under the Board, most of
them holding jobs that could be filled by permanent
hands. These latter find promotion stopped because of
the way the Act is evaded. The journey up the Public
Service ladder is a tedious business for the permanent
hand. Having passed a competitive exam, young Red-
tapk gets a job at £5O a year. Statutory increments take
him gradually to £llO. Then he has to leap another
hurdle in the shape of an exam., and after that he can
attain the dazzling pinnacle of £l5O. Still another
exam, is necessary before he can shift off this mark, and
even then he depends on the whim of the Board, as no one
has a right to go up over £l5O. Then Redtape has to in-
sure his life for a heavy sum, and pass the doctor regard-
ing his general bodily condition. On the other hand, the
temporary man knows a member or a Cabinet Minister,
and this person gets him in at a wage of perhaps 10s. or
12s. 6d.,0r 15s. a day. He has no exams., no insurance,
no doctor. He may start beyond the point where the
Permanent leaves off. All he has not got is security of
tenure, but as some of the Temporaries have 10 to 20
years’ service that isn’t a very bad worry. It is easy to
see that this temporary employment business strikes
at, the heart of the Public Service Act. And
the Temporaries who have obtained good jobs
by calmly ignoring all the requirements of the
Act are a constant source of irritation to the Permanents
whose promotion they are blocking. The Association,
which is cussing as above, calmly proposes, and Wade has
acquiesced in the proposal, to appoint to the permanent
staff all the present temporary 2000. These men cannot
be npw subjected to the examination tests, certainly not
the competitive entrance exam. If they are all appointed
at their present pay, and not reduced to the £l5O mark,
there will be trouble ahead for the P. S. Association. It
cannot serve two masters, and in its anxiety to secure the
subscriptions of the temporary crowd it overlooks the
antagonism of the permanent staff. These last don’t see
their danger yet, but when they wake up to it I prophesy
battle, murder and sudden stoush.
The monopolistic Melbourne Tram Co. an-
nounces its intention to pay a bonus of 5. per cent,
to employees of two years’ standing who are not
connected with any trade union. This co.
doesn’t give bonuses to employees for amuse-
ment, or because they look well. It only does
such things because it reckons they are good
business. But, if it pays this co. to aim such a
blow at trade unionism—in other words, if Mel-
bourne really likes a co. that will throw mud at
trade unionism in this way—then Melbourne is
in a pitiful state. One suggested explanation
of the mystery is that the bonus is intended
to prejudice Hannah’s Bill for a Tramway Wages
Board. As a matter of fact, it ought to have
precisely the opposite effect. In a business
in which a strong trade union was encouraged
a wages board might not be necessary to secure
for the workmen a square deal. On the other
hand, the men who work for a concern which
bribes them to keep out of a trade union are
almost certain to go to the wall unless they have
a wages board. Men must have either a strong
trade union or a wages board ; and if their union
is weakened through a system of bribes, then
their only hope is through a board or some other
sort of arbitration.
N.S.W. Treasurer Waddell banged the show-
man’s big drum one day last week, and asked the
electors to observe that the Government had pro-
duced a surplus of £989,000 for the past year.
Waddell naturally didn’t mention two or three
things that are worth remembering. He didn’t,
for instance, allude to the fact that the receipts
from the sale of land and other assets were pro-
bably in excess of the whole £989,000, and that
receipts from the sale of land and other assets
shouldn’t be regarded as ordinary revenue at all.
The Anthony Hordern people could, no doubt,
sell their big shop next year, and if they threw
the proceeds into the cash-box, the “surplus,”
after paying wages, etc., would be swollen by
about half a million. But they wouldn’t be any
better off ; they would have the half-million, but
they wouldn’t have the shop, and thereafter
their real surplus would be less because their
expenditure would go up through having to pay
rent. As long as Governments sell land, etc., and
throw the proceeds in with the ordinary revenue,
a Treasurer can make his surplus pretty well what
he pleases. The amount is only limited by the
quantity of land he has to sell and the number of
buyers at the price he asks. Also, as long as a
Government goes in for “ a vigorous loan policy ”
its Treasurer can make the surplus pretty well
what he pleases, by the simple process of paying
out of loans for things that ought to be paid for
out of revenue. If a man with £5OO a year pays
out £4OO in the ordinary way, and borrows £2OO
to pay the grocer and the landlord, he can
show a surplus of £lOO provided he forgets
all about the 1.0. U. But if he is honest enough
to remember the 1.0. U., then he won’t show
any surplus at all, but a deficit of £lOO. N.S.W.
finance has been run on those lines lately. Wade-
Waddell have sold every foot of ground they
could get a buyer for, and their borrowings have
been tremendous. In fact, Waddell has bor-
rowed a great deal more than he did in the year,
not so long ago, when the Wade crowd, then led
by Carruthers, threw him out on the ground
that he was too dangerous a financier to be left a
minute longer at the Treasury.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, August 1, 1910.
Mr Dear Yarrie, —
Mrs. Mayoral Burston gave a party to speed
the parting Asches. Instead of employing the
cavern of the Town Hall itself, Mrs. Burston
annexed the Council Chamber and two other
rooms of importance upstairs, where the beautiful
square balcony makes an extra apartment with
grey columns and walls of striped awning. The
corridor there makes further spreading space—so
the affair was sumptuous yet comfortable, and
carefully shovelled into corners. Dago Di Gilio
was given the landing and corridor therein to
“maka de music” and “playa de fid,” while on a
platform in the large reception room, the large
Maggie Stirling boomed her large voice and
“Trally” Foster (that darling of the girls)
warbled in his divine tenor.
Mrs. Burston glittered in black top-dressing
with creamy feathers in her hat. Miss Burston
hit up the corn color of her hair with a grey garb
in the most approved poetic fashion. The Guest
—“Lil ” of the Asches—simply rode over every-
body else. The undermost garment visible to the
naked eye was clinging green satin. Over this
hung Paisley—no matter what material; it was
silken and Paisley ; therefore it shouted. If any-
one wrapped herself in grandmamma’s Paisley
shawl she could rise with the Cream without a
push. The Lily Brayton Paisley pretentiousness
was grappled at the waist with cords (just like
grandpa’s dressing-gown), and on her head was
a granny bonnet, making a green frame for
her bright face. This head garniture fairly
sprouted with black feathers. Oscar of the
Asches could not go far in dress owing to his
masculinity and the want of a stage, but he
caught the limelight with a good-bye speech.
The Mayoress saw to it that Everybody turned
up. All the stray titles our city possesses were
“among those present,” and the Elevated of the
land in general appeared on deck. The Asches
departed next day, and called back, “See you all
in two years.” Wonder if the boom will renew,
supposing we do see them again in two years.
The Glassy Harem, which is whirling through
quite a society season this year (the Glassy looms
and drops on alternate winters and so strikes an
average), is finding its sensation of the moment in
two matrimonial engagements that are apparently
cried off. There is much thrillsome speculation
as to which jilted who. Then there are a pair of
divorces in view. So we are being kept busy.
The Coopers, those stage-struck damsels who
used to drag society to the theatre to see them
lean up against a priina donna, are bursting into
the limelight on a new stage. The newest sensa-
tion is a manicure shop.
A delightful story is told of how a certain
highly-decorative she, who is the expensive friend
of a landowner and speculator, was introduced to
a distinguished official personage. The gaudy
lady, having been absent when the personage
took office, had never seen him before, and was
seemingly unacquainted with his photo; but a
lady friend of hers, who is also a shining light in
the world, had met the gentleman on a steamer
under circumstances equally respectable and de-
ceptive. The two stylish females took a run over
to Sydney together, and at the railway station
they chanced to meet the Personage, whom only
the one siren knew even by sight. To her the
gentleman paid his respects in his most affably
condescending manner, whereupon she introduced
her dashing pal, taking the precaution to change
the “Miss” into “Mrs.” But her dashing pal
didn’t take the Personage seriously. She thought
his title was only “spoof,” and in her own frank,
breezy way she said, “Rats! Tell me, sonny,
who are you, really?” The story ends here.
Anyway, the feelings of the Exalted One when
he was addressed as “sonny ” would be unfit for
publication.
Somebody suggests that the luckiest of all the
dames who live luxuriously in the separated state
is the one who caught her elderly hubby gallivant-
ing with her “double.” A handsome allowance
seems an unfair penalty to levy on a Lothario
whose indiscretion is almost a compliment. With
so many charmers to choose from, the poor man
picked one who was as like his wife as the pro-
verbial other pea in a pod. Such.duplication is
the sincerest form of flattery.
There looms divorce—not on the very highest
branch of the social tree, but still in the tree.
The two married young. They have ganged
their ain gait in a friendly enough way, tea-ing
and doing other social events with divers part-
ners. Now the lady makes her husband jealous
with a “Steddy”—a continuous escort, and a
bestower of furry coverings and other trifles. It
is understood to be the continuosity and the
obviosity and the furriness that the husband
objects to.
At a crowded theatre, the other night, one box
held a brilliant party, including a girl who cared
not to hide her light (nor her back) under a bushel
nor in the shade of the sheltering plush curtains.
She had gleaming hair and quite a quantity of
“ nood ” shoulder and back. She stood up every
now and then, and turned her dimpled back-
scheme to the house. She was, appa-
rently, looking for notice—and she got it.
The gods got on to the business and began
to applaud the back. The lady retired in con-
fusion to the dark depths of the box, and only
crept out when the lights went down. But the
gods were waiting. They howled and whistled
and shrieked, and a super on the stage wondered
if he would be offered “ lead ’’ after such an ova-
tion. The vision of loveliness again retreated,
covered with confusion. She sneaked back, half
an act later, disguised in the discreet envelopment
of a capacious cloak, and sat subdued, to watch
out the rest of the play.
The catch of the season is rumored with great
strength. Jessie, the unwedded last of the
Landale beauties who went wandering abroad
with papa, has, it seems, kept her heart for home.
Robert Murchison —the biggest gun of the ex-
clusive Melbourne Club set—is reported to be
the winner of the girl. Allowing weight for age
—the groom-elect is about 65 years young—it is
just about the best thing doing, according to
feminine notions of a match.
There has been grave discussion about a suc-
cessor to “ Money ” Bevan at the Coll ins-street
church while that comfortable gentleman is abroad
gathering foreign impressions. Great flutter and
disturbance arose among the gentle she-angels
when the Powers chose Leyton Richards, M. A.,
and announced him as a youngster of 32. Now
official notices of his approach and condition
include the bald statement that Mr. Richards is
married. He is a Glasgow man and studied at
the adjacent city of Auld Reekie ; so, possibly, his
wife is a Maggie, well primed with What Every
Woman Knows.
Hal Brasch, the champion boat-pusher in our
most reoent Henley meetings, is getting a minor
into training for the Diamond Sculls. The
youngster —a son—made his first earthly howl
about a week ago, and the new father struts
proudly.
Ex-Governor George Sydenham Clarke, who
left us in 1903, has collected another Mrs. Clarke.
The new annexe is a widow from India’s tiger-
haunted strand. Announcements invariably end
with a bald statement of the date of the last wife’s
decease at the very end of 1908. It is not too long
for regrets, to be sure ; but, then, Clarke is aged
62 and couldn’t be expected to wait any longer
ere picking up a pacemaker.
A giddy club man, much about town, has caused
comment by his sudden absence. “The boys”
see him no more nowadays. He is in strict
seclusion in his Far North sheep run, vegetating
in grass-widowerhood. He is a newly-made
grandfather. The force of such a reminder
of his age and responsibilities must have
been too great a shock to his jauntiness. For the
moment he decided to feel old and play the game.
Unto Josiah Thomas, P.M.G., was lately pre-
sented a Master Thomas. This makes the fourth
baby born to the Commonwealth Ministry since
April 13, the wives of Fisher, Tudor and Pearce
having also become mothers in recent days.
Pearce, by the way, has called his infant Lloyd
George, which is rough on the infant.
Did anyone notice that Mark Twain arrived
with Halley’s comet and the comet took him off
again ? He was born at comet time last trip, and
his erratic constellation made its first appearance
on the day of his death. Mark Twain always had
quaint conceits about comets. His “Captain
Stormfield’s Visit to Heaven ” brings in a jolly
old comet, and that book was a particular boon to
Clemens. He wrote it after the big clean-up of
his fortune. “ Stormfield ’’ brought good, money,
and Mark Twain built a comfortable home in the
country. He called the house “Stormfield.” It
reminds one of Barrie’s “ Noble Sims,” who
pasted on each piece of furniture the newspaper
article that paid for it.
I saw the ghost of Ernest Raleigh spooking
through town, t’other day, wandering drearily
without his froeky other half. They keep to
Sydney side nowadays, and nestle down in a
corner of Rose Bay. Mrs. Froeky spends less
time on elaborate decoration of her pretty self;
in fact, she is even known to spend a whole even-
ing and dinner time without getting into the
decollete of the dress that is full by name' and
skimpy by nature. How dinner food tastes when
one is not in dinner dress, it is hard to imagine.
When the beauteous two flatted sumptuously at
the Grand, practically every evening saw a new
picture in sartorial subtlety.
Walter Wimble Berry, the golden-haired dar-
ling of the tea-merchant Berry clan, has annexed
a widow in Canada, and the family is simmering
over quaint invitation cards and other parapher-
nalia. Mamma and Papa Berry are wandering
abroad, and were to reach Canada for the event.
The widow is a young thing to match the bride-
groom. Also, she is wealthy and beautiful and
altogether desirable. She was Mrs. Thompson
before she Berried herself.
The Carlyle Smythes are cheerful swallows.
The latest chirp conies from the snow peaks
around Innsbruck, where they made a summer
halt. That is to say, it was summer by the card ;
but the thermometer gets tired when it has
climbed to about 60° in the shade in that delect-
able region. The Smythes, when they wrote,
were just about leaving the frigidity for Munich,
where there is Wagner and lager beer.
The prophecy of charity shows was no false
stunt. They are hitting Melbourne like a rain of
brickbats. The Children’s Hospital gets up to
squeak. The surgical ward of that institution
has been condemned by the Board of Health and
the chief of the Fire Brigade. It could scarcely be
more thoroughly damned. There are about7oo poor
little sick babies being cared for annually, and
the work of getting a new ward should be helped.
An energetic crowd has grabbed the Princess’s
for two odd dates—August 13 and 15. The Mel-
bourne Amateur Operatic Co., with Captain
Maudesley and Molly Carew in the lead, will
play “La Fille de Madame Ang6t.” Mr. John
Wallace is producing the rather spicy French
opera, and Cyril Brinkley bangs the conductor’s
baton.
Marshall Hall, the lover of Art—he who wor-
ships only at the shrine of her pure beauty—
attracted a certain amount of attention lately.
The new Victorian Professional Orchestra, which
is a youngster of a few months old, but is growing
lustily, had a concert on Saturday afternoon.
The V. P. Orchestra is made up of Unionist men
only. The powers at the back of non-unionist
Marshall Hall sent circulars to all known music-
lovers inviting them to a free rehearsal of their
hundredth concert, in the Guild Hall, at two
o’clock on Saturday. The V.P.O. played in the
Town Hall at three o’clock. That was a below-the-
wind belt for the new orchestral youngster. There
has never been even a crack in the fence for a
listener at previous rehearsals, and no one ever
heard the word “ invitation ” whispered.
Freddie Beard took the spring-board at the
Saturday concert of the Victorian Professional
Orchestra. This is the second he has beaten into
blaring expression. Bertie Zelman has conducted
two, and Andrew MacCunn was to have polished
up the next, only an urgent comic opera company
dragged him away betimes. MacCunn gave up
the blessed holes of the golf-links to listen to the
Saturday harmony, and Tallis beamed placidly
from the back of the balcony. Comedian Wright
seemed to be soothed by the music—he has been
a devotee at any old musical matinee since his
advent here. Little Dr. Maloney and a fellow
politician tucked their hands in their pockets and
forgot orations for the hour, whilst the Marshall
Hall devotees stayed conspicuously away. The
house grows every time. There have been three
Thursday afternoon concerts. Now the orchestra
attacks Saturday and competes with football and
other out-door rages.
Slapoffski will come over from Sydney to con-
duct the second Saturday afternoon concert,
which is fixed for August 27. Slap, is billed as
“Herr.” Where does he get the Herr? He is
not German—his pedigree, in fact, is Russo-
Cockney ; but I suppose a ski on the end of one’s
name demands something more that plain Mr.
It seems a wild hanker after a title of some sort.
Beard gave the fanciful “Nut-cracker Suite,”
evolved from the brain of the sneezy Tschai-
kowsky. It is full of delirious dances. The
Arabian dance is a languorous thing of much
melody. For the “Sugar Plum Fairy” dance
J. C. Williamson lent his celesta—which he
lately bought from Allan’s—the only one of its
kind in Australia. Conductor MacCunn wove a
delicate accompaniment from it for Roberts’
“ Wayside Flower ” song in “ The Waltz Dream,”
and he kept the thing hidden and the audience
guessing. Harper Barker, who played the “Sugar
Plum ” air, had the machine in full view at the
concert, It is like a harpsichord or a very young
treble piano. The instrument was invented by
Tschaikowsky originally for this very theme, and
it is beginning to thrive in orchestras abroad.
An Australian has gone gathering glory in
South Africa. She is a baby violinist named
Katie Silva, her age is 11 years, and she got away
with the gold medal at the last Eisteddfod in
Johannesburg. She was a pupil of Foley West-
wood, one of the most juvenile of Melbourne
violin teachers, and Foley (who is almost blind)
wears his feather most modestly. The 11-year-
old managed to annex several engagements right
away on the strength of-her success.
Stage-manager Meyer, of the Asche company,
evidently thinks Australia a good wicket. He
proposes to return to Victoria, and is “going on
the land.” He has bought a place somewhere
about Yea. His wife, Dorothy Grimston, is
happy with her two youngsters, and the quartette
intends to live joyously ever after with the cow
and the corn for company.
Men are thinking that, before ladies really get
into Parliament, it would be well to hurry through
a Dress Limitation Bill. The first clause must
deal, of course, with big hats. That is an ever-
present shadow upon a man’s social and theatrical
life. Clause 11. will sternly regulate the width
of a woman’s skirt. Tight skirts may be funny
to the man in the street, but they are quite other-
wise to the tram man. A woman fell off aCollins-
street car t’other day because she absolutely could
not step. The male tram-travellers foresee the
time when they will have to bodily lift women on
and off, and there are times of the day when men
are scarce and women very plentiful. At these
hours a small and lonesome male might as well be
a coal-lumper. And if man once begins to lift
lovely, heavy woman off the car he may next be
expected to carry her to the pavement.
Voluminous skirts, according to fashion, mea-
sure l£yd. just at present, but fashion-plates get
themselves into a yard circumference just above
the ankles. Walk in a gunny bag, and one gets
the same graceful effect. One brilliant light of
Melbourne society hooks the base of her skirt to
the tops of her boots and shuffles to her motor.
Mamma’s maiden aunt says, “Oh, my dear! —
what if a bull should chase you ! ” No, there’d be
no chance of running, let alone shinning up a
tree. Women will yet evolutionise, into mono-
legs or mermaids. The strangest part of the
fashion is this: The skirt takes an infinitesimal
area of cloth, yet the tailor’s bill comes out just
the same as of old, only more so.
The gorgeous Springthorpe tomb at Kew is to
be surpassed, they say, by the statuary adorn-
ments of a wealthy widower’s family vault at
Brighton Cemetery. Side by side with the effigy
of his dead daughter will be placed a life-sized
figure of her mother, which should be more im-
pressive than the effect of the profusely gold-
lettered memorial at the other burying-ground.
It is whispered that the Richmond engine
carried the same number as the machine which
made the Sunshine holocaust. Possibly it was
the same deadly implement. Let us hope that
driver Milburn is not spooking. He left here on
the Waratah. It is not known for certain even
yet whether he is with the drowned or whether
he left the boat at Durban.
Don’t know why the city should let itself get
so far behind the times when the country booms
in its frivols. At a Melbourne dance a pro-
gramme is curiously limited—a set of lancers,
several waltzes and myriad two-steps make a menu
as meagre of variety as an Englishman’s dinner.
Listen to this card from a country dance.
There is a varsoviana. How could anyone
be happy without unravelling the mystery of that ?
Among the common or garden waltz, polka and
schottisehe, come spicy things like a march waltz,
a waltz cotillon, and something about an Irish
stew. Dance 22 is dubbed the “ Fitzroys.” This
precious programme reached me through the
hands of a lucky wandering man, but I’ll never be
content again until I have experienced the flavor
of a varsoviana ora Fitzroy. No wonder a self-
respecting country dance needs its M.C.—things
like that couldn’t go through without a stage
manager.
From “Rosna,” of Adelaide :
The Elder Hall wore a bright smile am) a flower in ifs
buttonhole on Monday of last week because the Oban-
eellor Ways were giving a party. The guest of honor
was the Rev. W. Temple, M.A., whose late father was
headmaster of Rugby, and afterwards Archbishop of
Canterbury. His Ex. and Lady Bosanquet went to the
party ; also, the nabobs of the University, the Parlia-
ment and its wife, the upper crust and its eldest daughter,
the landed gentry and its girl, and the clergy and a row
of nice white-clad girl-graduates. The Elder Hall blos-
somed as the rose ; and afternoon tea burst into bud at
the psychological -moment. And between times Dr.
Ennis played “See the Conquering Hero Comes,” or
some other Brahms rhapsody, on the organ.
The committee that has been out hunting S. Australian
facts to support the Dudley lady’s Bush Nursing scheme,
has made its report. This is to the effect that the flourish-
ing District Trained Nurses’Association—a feather which
we wave proudly in this State’s millinery—embraces all the
functions of the proposed scheme, and it would be un-
wise to bring in a new organisation to interfere or com-
pete with it.
Elizabeth Delprat, the tall and musical olive-branch of
the m m who is managing monarch of the Broken Hill
Proprietary mine, has put all the foreign fiddling lads
and lasses at the Conservatoire of Ghent into aback seat,
having been awarded first prize for violin-playing at the
Conservatoire’s final examination. She took her talents
to the old Flemish city some years ago, after receiving-
much local tuition, and for some time now has been
leader of the orchestra at the place to which the good
news was taken in the Browning piece we used to elocute
at school.
Hvmen was employed recently to knot Mr. Frank Giles
and Miss Ethelwyn Strawbridge, the handsome daughter
of the Surveyor-General. The new housekeepers will
dwell somewhere near the feet of the hills which we keep
to show visitors.
The Creswells, who belonged to South Aus. before we
handed over our navy to the Commonwealth, are
triumphing over the prowess of their son. The lad is a
cadet at the Naval College, Dartmouth (England), and he
has just captured the swimming challenge cup there.
The Melbourne express brought us a precious cargo on
Friday morn. It consisted of Calv 6. The joyous owner
of the Voice had not slept in the train, and therefore the
crowd which took its. nosegays to meet, her was only
allowed half a glimpse of her before she was whisked off
for a nap. In the afternoon, however, a refreshed and
fur-clad divinity held a kind of court, with her interpret-
ing companion to ward off the bad French of intruders
On the same night the Bach Society serenaded her and
presented her with a chiastolite pendant: chiastolite
being a “lucky” stone we dig up from our dark interior
—a'Stone that" wears on its face the sign of the cross in
white on a dark ground.
The A. W. Styleses, who are the head municipals ol
Thebarton, turned the local Town Hall into a flower-
garden for the ratepayers on Friday evening. Henry
Brose, Alexander Cooper, and some smaller exponents of
good noise, with a string band, made music for the tax-
payers. Then there was supper, and after that dancing.
Adelaide’s polar heyo, Douglas Mawson, has been lec-
turing us on Antarctic matters. He began it arrayed as
Solomon might have been if he had expected to meet an
iceberg ; but he gradually moulted most of the glory.
Amidst a deal of science, he dropped a hint of domestic
fconomy. With one lone gallon of kerosene he and Pro-
fessor David cooked three meals a day for three pole-
hunters for 17 davs. If rents are as cheap as fuel m
Shackleton-screet, Poleville, we are due to move there.
Our electric trams are beginning to get some deadly-
work in ; but their latest fatality was not their fault. A
lady of 57 was travelling on an open cross-seated car.
Suddenly she announced that she had lost her purse (as
a matter of fact she had left it at home on the table), and
she simply stepped off the fast-moving car and was picked
up dying. The Tramway Trust justly remarks that it
was not to blame.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.
From the Oldest Inhabitant :
The North Sydney Council has revived the old name of
Thrupp at Neutral Bay. Thrupp was a lieutenant in th e
Navy ; and while his ship was in Sydney in the ’twenties
he married a daughter of Captain John Piper, the naval
officer after whom Point Piper is named. A grant of
land was, of course, easily obtained ; something like 600
acres were passed over to Thrupp. This property
extended from Neutral Bay to Middle Harbor. Old
maps show “Thrupo’s grant.” The lieutenant lived in
a cottage which stood where the Hayes-street wharf now
is. Ages agone, Thrupp, for a consideration, transferred his
grant to the Coopers, who sublet it in large blocks to
many tenants on the usual 99 years’ lease. Before that
time, Thrilpps-road used to be in the directories ; but
“Thrupps” gave way to “ Kurrabba,” the native name
of the nose formerly known as Thrupp’s Point. Some
years ago a manufacturing magnate named Hayes lived
here—Paddy Hayes. He made bricks, and tried to make
glass, and a street running down from Eaymond-road to
Kurrabba-road was named after him. The council has
now wiped out Paddy and resurrected Thrupp. Which,
on the whole, is no improvement. For a man who tried
to establish a glass-making industry in the early days is
better worth remembering than a mere naval johnny
who married a girl, got a Crown grant thrown in, and
sold his little lot of land when the unearned increment
made it worth while.


The
Winters Tale ” of the Complexion.
A Hint or Two How to keep it Safe and Sound.
[HAVE before me as I write a tiny volume of Shakespeare’s “ Winter’s Tale
dedicated to Miss Ellen Terry. That play has marked important events in the
career of this wonderful woman. It was the first play in which she appeared
before the English public, and 50 years later it furnished Miss I erry with her last
Shakespearian part. . . .
In her Memoirs, which she has lately published, Miss Terry recites this incident
concerning Sarah Bernhardt “ Miss Sarah has been talking to Henry Irving, and
while so engaged she took some red stuff out of her bag and rubbed it on her lips.”
This story just serves as a handy peg on which to hang my talk on how one.
should take care of the lips during the winter.
Next to the nose, which is the first outpost of the face to weather
every vicissitude of the atmosphere, it is the lips that are called upon
to bear the brunt of the “ chiding of the winter’s wind.” The
membrane of the lips is the tenderest part of the face, and
the cracking or chapping caused by the cold or wind are not
only painful but a decided drawback to beauty. To
moisten them with the tongue, an unseemly habit, and
then expose them to the cold or wind only tends to
make matters worse. The great principle of a wise and
rational care of the lips in winter is to cover them with
a thin film of emollient substance, through which the
blast of cold winds cannot penetrate. Such a protective
preparation is found in the Valaze Lip Lustre. It not
only safeguards the delicate outer membrane of the lips
against cracks, chaps and sores, but is also the best
remedy when they have already appeared.
Another important matter to guard against during the
winter is the flushing of the nose and cheeks during the
cold weather. Fortunately sufferers from this unsightly
affliction can obtain relief and moderation of the flushing out-
breaks by the use of the Valaze Liquidirie and the Novena Red
Nose Ointment and Powder. The nose should be rubbed
thoroughly with a pledget of wool moistened with the Liquidine,
before going out. This treatment re-establishes perfect circulation in the organ,
and prevents the undesirable redness. It also acts as a means against the
effect of the cold. The Novena Red Nose Ointment and Powder are used at night
only. For this trouble there is a more thorough, deeper-going, exclusive and unfail-
ing treatment practised at the Valaze Institutes in Melbourne and Sydney.
Now, concerning the care of the face in general. It is not the easiest of tasks
to take care of the skin during the winter, unless it has been intelligently safeguarded
during the summer and autumn. But if you are ready and willing to pay the price
of vigilance for a good complexion, the first rule is to protect the skin from exposure
to the cold winds and frost. For this purpose a thin coating of Valaze Skin Food or

SUFFERERS FROM OVER-STOUTNESS.
How They May Cuke Themselves.
In the first place, it must not be lost sight of
that food is the very fuel of vitality. So many
people who are alarmed by the first symptoms of
an obese tendency are prone to sacrifice themselves
to a sparing and innutritious dietary, and to
exercise themselves out of all reason. There is no
cure for obesity in such physical abuses. The
human organism requires gentler treatment. We
are glad to give our stout readers the benefit of a
splendid recipe which they can make up them-
selves (or get the'r chemist to do so), and by means
of which they may cure themselves, without the
least physical discomfort, of obesity in any of its
stages. The prescription is One half-ounce of
Marmola, one ounce of fluid Extract of Glycyr-
rhiza 8.P., one ounce of pure Glycerine 8.P.,
with peppermint water to make six ounces in all.
Dose: Two teaspoonfuls after each meal. This
isimple and harmless home treatment entails
nothing restrictive—no fasting, po exhausting
exercises. Hence, while the superfluous fatty
matter is being rapidly removed the subject as
rapidly regains strength and stamina through
properly digested food in rational qualities, the
whole alimentary system being toned up and re-
fortified. It is a beautiful and most efficacious
method of self cure which no over-stout person
should neglect.*

This Man Suffered for Fourteen

Every year, for 14 years, as the cold weather of
June came on, Mr. W. Clarke, 214 Gouger Street,
Adelaide, S.A., was taken with crippling attacks
of sciatica, and had to be taken to the hospital,
where he would be an inmate for three or four
weeks. Not a single year in 14 was he without an
attack, but he passed the fifteenth without a
sign of Sciatica. He had found a cure. Acting
on the experience of others who had been cured
by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, he tried them and
obtained wonderful relief. The following are
Mr. Clarke’s own words :
“Some years back I got several severe wettings
when out.tishing, Subsequently I was subject to
severe sciatic pains, particularly in the back of
the hips. The pains were as if someone was
driving a knife into my hips. For 14 years every
June I have had to go to the hospital, with the
result that I had to stay there each time for three
or four weeks. I would leave the hospital very
little better than when I went in. About eight
months since I saw an advertisement in a paper
abuut cures effected by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills,
and having heard people talk about them also,
I determined to give them a trial, and I am glad
I did. My wife bought one box at Guenthers,
the chemist in Gouger Street, and I started taking
them. I found so much benefit from the first box
that I bought two more boxes, and have felt so
Well since that this is the first year I have not had
to go to the hospital for past 15 years. Any of
my neighbors can tell you what I suffered for
years, and of the wonderful improvement in my
health since I took Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and
now, although I am 59 years of age, I can do more
work than I could at aiiy time during my previous
15 or 20 years. I can sleep and eat and enjoy
life as well as any man of my age, and I attribute
it all to my use of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, my
only regret being I did not use them before.”
Remember, only the genuine Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills cure. Don’t be gulled into taking
something said to be just as good. Of all dealers
at 2s. 9d. per box, 6 boxes 15s. 3d., or from the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. of Australasia Ltd.,
Sydney.*

POLTICAL
POINTS
The words of “ Jean Sibi ” :
I see that Puller, M.H.R., proposes that a monument
“ with six faces representing the six Slates ” should be
erected on the Yassberra site. Certainly, but why all
this facial extravagance? Why not make the monument
simply two-faced by sticking up a statue of Mahon and
Hughes? The figure of either person in stone or brass
(brass preferred) would suit Fuller’s purpose and be a
striking memorial into the bargain. It would com-
memorate the man who had the face to believe in the
Referendum as a theory, and the face to hate it as a
practice on this particular cpiestion.
S. Australia was given a loolf-in at the
country around the Yassberra Federal capital
site the other day. Somebody wrote to the
Register asking what the country was like for
settlement. The Register sent the question on to
the N.S. W. Lands Department. And the N.S. \V.
Lands Department answered that the territory
itself wasn’t available, though the surrounding
country was. It pointed out how the land might
be taken up ; and then added : “ But the country
consists chiefly of steep granite ranges of inferior
grazing capacity.” It’s a cheerful prospect
altogether—dead rabbits inside the area and
steep granite ranges outside.
The whoop of an Australian in England
who wants his country to think a bit better of
itself :
You just need to take a trip to Europe from Australia
to realise how up-to-date in most things Australia really
is. At every Australian port—-Sydney, H barf,, Mel-
bourne, Port Adelaide and Fremantle—the big P. and O.
ship lay alongside a wharf ; but never again till we got
into Tilbury Docks were we able to land without a
tender. Colombo, Suez, Port Said, Naples, Marseilles,
Gibraltar and even Plymouth could not (at any rate they
did not) accommodate us.
Still, though it is something for the Australian to
feel good about, he might feel even better if, in-
stead of having such facilities for landing the im-
ported saucepan, his country had more facilities
for making the saucepan on the spot.
A paragraph in Melb. Age (26/7/TO) threw
what looks like a streak of light on the Rich-
mond wreck :
The delays in the introduction of the lock and block
signalling system on the Victorian railways become
more extraordinary and inexplicable when compared
with the advances made in Ne\^ South Wales. The Vic-
torian Department has fitted the Sykes apparatus to only
two suburban railway sections, although the system was
officially accepted six years ago. The New South Wales
Department has gone far ahead. It has had the lock and
block signalling for years, and now is installing a new
system known as the electro pneumatic.
Victoria, the land of big estates, and antiquated
railway apparatus, and ill-paid, overworked rail-
way servants, seems likely to get a mighty bad
name before somebody contrives to sit on the
head of this Richmond ghost and jam it into its
coffin. But while the western lines wander
through the unpeopled wildernesses of the big
estates, earning next to nothing, where is the
money for up-to-date-apparatus to come from ?
A recent cry by Abbott, of the Victorian
House of Ditched Landlords, was one of the most
interesting noises heard for an age. Standing up
in the place where the Landlords congregate, he
cried that the proposed Federal land-tax would
drag £404,000 out of the pockets of Victorian land-
owners. The intense silence that greeted this
statement was partly dispersed by an interjection
from Labor-member Evans. Said Evans quietly :
“ We could have had that money for the State ”
and in that sentence he reminded the Landlords
of their futile slaughter of Murray’s Land Tax
Bill.
More Socialism ! More ruin ! This time it
is the Christchurch (M.L.) Municipal Council
which proposes to do the mischief. The town’s
public clocks used to call one another a liar every
minute of the day, and to stop that sort of local
disgrace they were all connected electrically with
a small controlling clock in the town clerk’s office.
This implement sends out the electric current that
moves the hands of the public timepieces. In this
way time all over the city is kept uniform. The
council now announces that it is prepared to con-
nect the system with any private house or place
of business, and already many people are applying
for the service. All that is required is a wire
from the council office and a clock dial to show
the time. There are no works to get out of order,
no winding, no trouble, no “few minutes slow ”
and consequent missed trains —no anything except
perfect order and a small annual bill.
Colonel Onslow jettisoned most of his
allegiance to the Wade Government, last week.
This is how the S. M. Herald describes his
attitude toward the Saturday Half-Holiday Bill :
The most astonishing break-away of the night was
when Colonel Onslow rose. He smote the measure hip
and thigh, as being an additional restriction of the
liberty of the subject— i.e., the shopkeeper; but, recog-
nising “the absurdity of opposing it against the com-
bined forces of the Government and the Opposition,” he
went no further than making his protest.
Onslow has met with scurvy treatment at the
hands of Liberalism. The party preferred Jessep to
him at last State elections. And of late it is said to
have intrigued against him in his own electorate,
so that he lias been defeated for the local selection.
Can the infuriated warrior be blamed for refusing
any more to support the Socialistic proposals of
the Government which exists to smash Socialism.
Barrister J. £>. Fitzgerald, one of the
members of the first Parliamentary Labor party
in N.S.W. and now up against little Levy for
Darlinghurst, turns the tables this way
The Wade Government is constantly crying out about
“ the enemies of Sydney.” But who are the enemies of
Sydney? He could tell them. They were those who had
had the power to make Sydney greater than it was and
did not use it; those whose neglect of Sydney problems
had handicapped the city to-day in.its commerce ; who
had refused to give to Sydney proper wharfage accommo-
dation by resuming the Gas Company’s premises ; who
had neglected to give a proper suburban and city railway
service, and a proper connection between the north and
south shores by tunnel or bridge ; who had seen the
beauty spots filched from the citizens one by one;,who
had done nothing for housing, nothing for improvement
of our choked commercial avenues, nothing for better
approaches to our wharves, nothing for beautification.
These were the enemies of Sydney—those who had the
power and would not use it; those who were unable to
grasp a great question ; who were incapable of realising
the fact that Sydney had grown to the proportions of
one of the world’s great cities, with the immediate pro-
spect of an enormous increase of growth. Sydney had
outgrown the capacity to handle its great and growing
commerce, and the Government had pigeon-holed the
improvement scheme drafted by its own Royal Commis-
sion, and had pigeon-holed Greater Sydney. '
FAIR EXCHANGE.
The Government is now considering a scheme of
superannuation, the details of which will be made
public in the course of a few days, under which the rail-
way and tramway servants of the State will find relief
which will guarantee them in their declining years
against having to seek refuge in a poorhouse or asylum.
—Premier Wade at Ashfield ( N.S.W. ).
Holland at last gives up Freetrade as a
bad job. As Germany’s great river, the Rhine,
ambles into the sea through Dutch territory the
country has much transit trade, and that kept it
hanging to the tail of the old superstition longer
than might otherwise have been' the case. Its
defection will leave Turkey almost the only Free-
trade (or partially Freetrade) country on the con-
tinent of Europe, and the miserable wearer of the
fez can hardly help himself. In times past his
benighted sovereigns bought peace or purchased
assistance by commercial concessions, till finally
it was agreed that Turkey should never, except
by permission of the Powers, charge more than 8
percent, duty on anything. The figure was ulti-
mately raised to 11 per cent, in order that the
Unspeakable might have a chance to find money
with which to pay his foreign interest bill; but
Turkey is still a low-tariff country with a gun
pointed at it.

otherwise.

Bigjohn Forrest makes his complaint
anew in the House of Representatives
There is a lot of talk about borrowin’, a lot of talk
against the people who made the Great Country what it
is. I don’t like it. It is disparagin’; it is an insult to
MEN LIKE MYSELF !
The fact that Myself has ceased to attract any
notice worth mentioning is the constant sorrow
of the vain old Emperor’s life.
Japan, which had a fairly heavy tariff
aforetime, has lately increased it, and the tail of
the Monkey is greatly uplifted. The new move
is apparently directed with especial care against
Britain. On the things the Jap mostly buys from
Bull the duties have risen G 6 per cent, all round,
while on those which he mostly buys from Russia,
Germany, etc., the average increase is 50 per cent.
The duty on iron rails has been whooped up from
4s. 6d. to 275. 6d. per ton; on galvanised wire,
from Bs. lOd. to about 455., and many other taxes
.have whizzed skyward, almost out of sight. And
there are persistent rumors that when the Anglo-
Jap treaty runs out Japan will join the Triple
Alliance—Germany, Italy and Austria. Truly
there is little gratitude among the people of the
tree-tops,
In the House of Representatives last week
the Minister for Home Affairs promised to tackle
a well-known Tory League lady re the truth of
her allegation that “a man” had voted 15 times
for a Labor candidate at last Federal elections,
and that “aßiverina swagimin” had put in 27
votes for the same unholy cause. Considering
that a swagman who voted 27 times in thinly
peopled Rivenna would have to' run about 100
miles in a day the yarn looks improbable, but
a lady’s statement ought not to be inquired
into too closely. No right-thinking man,
much less a Minister for Home Affairs, should
bother a lady for proofs in support of her state-
ments. Her word is sufficient. The Bulletin,
in its mind’s eye, can clearly see that Riverina
voter, flying around to 27 scattered polling-places,
dropping an iniquitous Labor vote at every stage
of his mad proceedings.
Jusfc because the States won’t back up the
Commonwealth, and because the Commonwealth
hasn’t yet got the constitutional authority
to back itself up, Australia is consuming large
quantities of very cheap water with its
butter after paying for it as butter. In
Britain it isn’t legal to expose for:sale any butter
containing more than 16 per cent, of moisture;
and to prevent Australian butter being rejected
and given a bad name, the Commonwealth pro-
hibits the export of tat with more than that much
moisture in it. The result is that the butter
which is not passed because it couldn’t be put on
the English market is thrown on to the Australian
market; and the Commonwealth has not the
power to stop it and thus force the producers to
turn out a decent article. The States have the
power; but they are mostly too busy scratching
at the Commonwealth to use it.
During a recent scuffle between the Prime
Minister and a more or less holy deputation,
Parson Worrall complained that certain colored
missionaries from Fiji were put to inconvenience
under the laws for the restriction of alien immi-
grants, while Jack Johnson, the colored pugilist,
was “ received with open arms.” A hurried in-
vestigation of the records showed that Johnson
was required to find security that he would leave
within a certain time, while the colored mission-
aries were admitted on the mere assurance of a
religious society that they would depart in due
course. This is a postscript to the row :
Answering Mr. Bamford (Q.) in the House of Repre-
sentatives to-day, Mr. Batchelor said he had received
from Rev. Mr. Worrall a letter bearing upon his state-
ment as to theadmissioh of Jack Johnson to the Common-
wealth, in replv to a communication from the depart-
ment. THE LETTER CONTAINED NO APOLOGY, but
Mr. Batchelor took it to amount to a retraction of the
statement.
No, the letter contained no apology. After
accusing the Australian Government of favoring
the pugilist as against the missionary, and after
having it proved that the advantage was really
the other way about, a bookmaker or a hawker or
a scavenger might have expressed his sincere
regret. But the reverend gentleman offered no
apology.
The Victorian furniture trade isn’t a nice
thing to look at these days. The influx of the
Chow mystery continues, and no one seems to
know where he comes from. There is no question
about where he goes to, for he doesn’t go. In
1899 there were 488 Chinamen making cheap gitn-
crack furniture in Victoria. In 1900 the number
had danced up to 552 ; in 1901 to 574 ; in 1902 to
614 ; in 1905 to 708 ; and for the year 1908 it was
reported to be 734. A heavy excise duty on each
pint of furniture, with a rebate on the white-made
article, is looming in the near future.
BRITISH STEEL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
German makers have agreed to deliver steel plates to
Clyde shipbuilders at ss. fid. a ton under Scotch prices.—
Cable.
British hammers clanging, singing,
On the red-hot rivets ringing ;
In the Clyde-side dock yards bringing
Ships to berth upon the tide.
British built and British driven,
Through the dock yard gates they ride.
And the hammers tell a story
Of a nation’s pride and glory ;
Built up slowly from the keel—
“ British work and British workers 1 ”
Sing the hammers on the steel.
But her ancient glory’s dying ;
Empty forges are a-sighing,
For her dockyards all are buying
German steel from over-sea ;
German steel for British hammers,
Soon they’ll come from Germany.
And the hammers fall a-dirging
Of a nation that is verging—
On to madness truck and keel—
“ German steel and starving workers.”
Toll the hammers on the steel.
N.S.W.

There’s nianyacause.
Men are not often
troubled by head-
aches ; when they are
it is generally due to
biliousnesss or indi-
gestion. But women have head-
aches which seem peculiar to their
sex, frequent throbbing headaches.
Does it not seem that such head-
aches peculiar to women must be at
once related to womanly disease?
Women who suffer with diseases
peculiar to the sex do not realise
the drain of vital strength and nerve
force they undergo as a consequence
of disease. It is this that causes the
headaches of sick women.
In other cases these pains and dis-
agreeable feelings are generally
symptoms of some other complaint,
such as indigestion, biliousness and constipation,
all of which are caused by corrupt, noxious matter
clogging the circulations; hence a stream or rush
of blood to the head, and by the excitement a
great pressure on the brain. Giddiness, headache,
loss of memory, dimness of sight, and various
other complaints are the result. Thus it will be
seen that all the above painful and distressing
maladies owe their origin to the blood. Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills are acknowledged to be
the only medicine that will thoroughly purify the
blood. They enter the stomach and unite with
the blood, and follow the stream of life on its
journey through the system. They root out all
foulness and impurity, and drive out every
unhealthy obstruction. They should be taken
every night in sufficient quantities to operate
freely, by commencing with two pills on going to
bed, three the next night, four the next; if the
symptoms are not removed, commence again with
two pills and go up again as before. Continue in
like manner until the blood is thoroughly purified,
and all pain and distress is entirely removed.*
