SPORTING
NOTIONS
A N.S.W. Rugby League XIII. journeyed to
Bnsbane, and fell on the local Leaguers in great
Xhe local men were as willing as the invaders
and the result was a struggle which drove the crowd to
great excitement. Messenger opened the actual hostili-
ties by a place lock from near halfway; but the wind
blew the ball wide. Then New South came away with a
passing charge, which was carried over and gave
the Ma State first score. Messenger again failed at goal.
Queensland tried some passing charges in quick suc-
cession, and got across, but also failed to add the extras.
Having found that quick, short passing was a par-
able game, Queensland did it again, and once
more crossed. This time Brackenrigg booted the
ball over the bar, and Queensland led by eight to three,
then the struggle grew fast,and, in the rush, one Queens-
lander fell and was run over. Being carried off, his place
was taken by another fiend like unto himself, and the
pace increased, if anything, till Farnsworth, of N.S.W.,
bioke loose and swept down on a defenceless line with
only one man between him and a certain score. Broom-
ham was close behind and Farnsworth passed. Cann
kicked the goal and the scores were level. N.S.W. broke
through again and Mes-enger lifted the score to
lo in favor of the Ma State. The Cornstalk team
had got going, and went on at such a pace
that at half-time it led by 19 points to 11. The
second half the Walers found the sun in their faces, and
while they were cursing over this fact Queensland scored
a held goal, and followed up with a determined attack on
the line, which, after a great struggle, was stormed. So
the Banana score was lifted to 18, just one behind thatof
N.b.VV. The sun continued to worry the Walers, and so
did the Queenslanders, who scored another try but failed
to convert. This gave the northern team the lead bv 21 to
19. Messenger ran around addressing a few blistering
words to his men, who responded with a try and a goal, and
made the Ma State 24 to 21. Then the Wales team broke
loose, and Queensland, weakening, found itself thrown on
the defensive. Queensland was done and the triumphant
Walers went on scoring till at the whistle they had
made their record 40 to 21. The trouble with Queensland
w'as that, when hard pressed, the lack of experience at the
League game got the men down. There was no time to
think what to do, and ignorance prevented them doing
the right thing by intuition. When they have learned
some more they will be a formidable lot.
* * * *
In Sydney the Football League pursued the
even tenor of its , competitions. The surprise of
the day was the smashing of the formidable Glebe by
Annandale, the baby club, which ran out with 10 points
to Glebe’s seven. Western Suburbs got another hiding,
this time from Balmain, which notched 13 points
to 10. An awful walloping was that inflicted
upon North Sydney by the strong-legged men
from South Sydney. From the very start South
jumped incessantly on the struggling North, and,
when the whistle blew, it had jumped 53 points to
four. Most interest was centred on the struggle be-
tween Eastern Suburbs and Newtown. East having
many men away was reckoned certain for a bad defeat.
But these things are on the knees of the gods, and
though Newtown almost burst its boiler and its collar in'
its frantic struggles to break through, Eastern Suburbs
notched point for point, and the game ended in a draw
with four points all.
So Newtown is still ahead in the competition
with 15 points. South Sydney is second with 14. Bal-
main and Eastern Suburbs are third and fourth with 12
and 11 points each. A feature is that although New-
town is in front it has scored only 150 points to 51,
whereas South Sydney has mopped up 251 to 82.
Newtown is also leading in the Union competi-
tion with 16 points, and South Sydney is second in this
department likewise, but with only 12points ; moreover,
it shares the second line with University, Eastern Suburbs
and Glebe. Manly and Western Suburbs have eight
each. There are others, but mercy forbids that
their weakness should be exposed. On Saturday
South Sydney went under to University, the stud-
ents scoring 18 to South’s eight. Manly’s recent
triumphal progress was checked, Eastern Suburbs
downing the Village by nine points to three. Glebe
met St. George and knocked seven dragons out of it
winning by 23 points to nine. The horrible goal kicking
of St. George was a sight that harrowed up the soul, and
a failing that made victory an impossibility. St. George
team should either get married or-buy a couple of dogs
for each man. Western Suburbs nearly died of shock
when it discovered it had smashed up Balmain by 16 to
11. But the prize horror was the decline and'fall of
Sydney, when Newtown dartced on the prostrate city to'
the tune of 32 points' to-nil.
* * *
In the Sydney Soccer division things are grow-
ing exciting. For the Rawson Cup Glebe is leading,
with 14 competition points, followed by Rozelle witli 13
and Balmain with 11. For the Association Cup, Ultimo
is in the van with 17 points, while Western Suburbs,
Prometheus and St. George are behind in that order,
with 15, 14 and 12 points respectively. The coveted
Gardiner Cup is now in the semi-final stage, and the dis-
putants are Ultimo, Powerful, Rozelle and Helensburgh.
So things Soccer are humming.
* * * *
Although English Rugby may be decadent, the
real English game, which is Soccer, is verv virile. On
Saturday last, the English team that has been raiding
South Africa played its third test match at Capetown,
and wiped the floor with the hardy Afrikanders by six
goals to three. This was the third test of the tour,' and
England has come out on top every time. The first was
played at Durban, and the Rooineks won by three to nil.
The second was at Johannesburg, and the English scored
by six to two. The final match at Capetown completed
the tour.
* * * *
An army of “ Brokers” (from Broken Hill) in-
vaded Sydney last week, and played Australian foot-
ball at Erskineville Oval against a team which was
labelled “ New South Wales.” As a matter of fact, the
best players in the metropolis were in the stand, looking
on. Why this was the case only the selectors know, and
as the team they chose won, no remarkscan he passed on
their choosing. They were told to pick a team lodown
Broken Hill, and did it. Thinking the team was short, the
selectors asked sundry, players who had not been picked
to change their togs and stand by ready to join in the
fray ; and then informed them they were not required.
Under the watchful eye of Captain Mick Grave the home
team made a great fight and just managed to beat the
Brokers on the post and win bj’ three points. Grave’s
leadership was a humorous item ; the old man stood out-
side the pickets and howled his orders to the team
which obeyed implicitly. Cooper, the alleged captain,
was a mere lay figure.
* * * *
This English Northern Union team seems to
have got itself thoroughly disliked. It was referred to in
Sydney as the “worst tempered team” that ever left
England. In Maoriland things were strenuous from the
jump and the four matches played in the Hocking Isles
were all marred by rough play and a tendency to upper*
cut. According to the papers the Maorilanders did not
retaliate, or there would have been a free fight in every
match. From a toe-bull p .int of view the English were
very successful, scoring 187 points to 47—which is about
the worst downfall Maoriland has ever struck. Judging
by the attendances at two matches (17,000 and 10,000),
the League game has caughton in W rd’s Dominion. By
the time this is in print the Englishmen will lie back in
Sydney, unless the YVaratah act is repeated, having left
Auckland on the night of Monday last.
Al though the tour of N.S. W. this season by the
Maori Rugby (Union) team resulted in considerable
financial loss, the wealthy natives who footed the bill, as
distinct from those who booted the ball, reckon it was
worth the money for the sake of the educational advant-
ages to the young men of the tribes. Hori doesn’t mind
P'.ving for education, particularly if it can be found in
the vicinity of a racecourse, a billiard-table or a football
field ; and he is busy thinking out some more tours of
the kind. At present, proposals are being considered for
a visit to Britain in 1911.
In the Yiecorian Football Ass., Town and P’ran
are scrambling for fourth place, while’Scray nd Port
are lamenting the odd game they lost earlier in the
season when they didn’t think it counted ; consequently
only two games held any importance in the scheme of
things. Jf P’ran could defeat the North it might lie
back well pleased with itself. But it couldn’t. In fact,
it hadn’t Buckley’s. The undefeated, semi-League crowd
went ahead from the bounce and stayed there, with a
final tally of 52 points to P’ran's 29. The other episode of
moment was that between 'Town and Port, in which the
former took no chances, and landed a winner, with 64
points to 42. The other clubs spent the aftprnnon in
kicking the air bag about to li tle purpose. Offieial re-
cords : Essendon’s 106 to Brighton’s 38 ; Brunswick’s 118
to Northcote’s 43 ; and ’Scray’s 127 to Preston’s 20. This
last catastrophe so annoyed the Preston ’bailers that they
resorted to what Judkins would call “degrading
methods,” whereupon ’Scrap’s barraekers stepped in and
“ mixed it" willingly, being only induced to cease hos-
tilities by the application of police batons.
* * * fe
One of the inscrutable laws of Providence fre-
quently ordains, as the Rev. Mr. Mudkins would put it,
that the game that is expected to provide Melbourne
with an exhibition of strenuous and well-directed en-
deavor turns out instead to be a mere untidy spell of
bogging-in and flopping round. This was the disaster
that struck the 25,000 who squeezed into the East. Mel-
bourne ground on Saturday to view the collision • f South
and 'Varsity. Both teams are struggling to be in the
final four; and as the two eighteens consisted of large,
muscular men, the spectators promised themselves joy.
Bur there was no joy. After the first quarter Skinner’s
hirelings had it all their own way, and ran out. easy win-
ners by 67 points to 24. Across the Yarra Park Rich-
mond was doing a mighty but useless dash against Gee-
long. The former has no chances of semi-final honors, but
the latter has ; wherefore the know-alls opined the game
would be “crook.” The point is that Geelong’s victory
meant the probable “ outing ” of South, which Richmond
lovpth not. As usual, the know-alls were know-nothings.
Only Richmond’s disorderly kicking gave Geelong a
chance; hut it grabbed that chance with both hands,
and brought the score level—six goals six behinds to
Richmond’s five goals 12 behinds, which, being inter-
preted, is 42 points all. There was no earthly reason why
anyone should have spent the afternoon inspecting the
Carlton v. ’Roy unpleasantness, inasmuch as the posi-
tions of both are assured —one at the top, the other near
the bottom. Carlton, whose kicking was wretched, got
an undeserved win hy three points. On the Metropolitan
Ground ’Wood passed its opponents the time of day and
a handy walloping by 66 points to 48 ; while Essendon
deposited the Saints in their accustomed place, 77 points
to 38 being the official statement.
* * * *
The Maoriland Rugby team that went to the
United States recently had a curious reception. There
were 25 players, and they were accompanied by about the
same number of relatives and enthusiasts, their wives and
daughters. When they steamed in on the Mariposa the
haughty Yankee officials refused to allow them to land,
and talked of deportation till the British Consul took a
hand. Then the Yankees discovered that what they
thought was trachoma was merely the Maori eyelid. In
California, the home of western football, no team was
found that had the pluck to take on the Maoris, who
looked most formidable. So the men went on
tour, vainly seeking victims. To keep the pot
boiling they appeared in the music-halls; and
with their tattooed hakas, fantastic war
scenes and native singing became the rage. Then
on to New York, which was apparently taken by
storm. Whilst they were in New York the Gothamites
got together a terrible team that was reckoned good
enough to'eat anything on ea'rth. and invited the Maoris
to come out and be chawed up. The Maoris came, and
before their machine-like passing, fleetness of foot and
great weight the Yankees were crumpled up by 24 points
to three. America stood still. Till they got back to
California no Rugby club would risk such a dog-walloping
as the Maoris appeared able to inflict. There America’s last
hope played them before an enormous crowd. The Maoris
soon found they were playing the referee as well as ihe
team, and that he was equal to two teams in his
own person. Ends were changed without any score.
The Californian team, which was composed' mostly
of transplanted Britons, got its tail up, ini- j
proved immensely and scored a try by grace
of the referpe. The Maoris protested univail-
ingly. Another was obtained shortly afterwards,
and the Yanks began to show signs of insanity. ; The
Maoris declared hoth tries (which were unconverted) to
have been illegally given, and rather than submit the
Maori cap ain led his men off the field. Persuaded to
return, the Maoris regretted their weakness, as the fouls
and crooked refereeing continued to the end and they
were beat"n. The Maori regard for the Sabbath broke
the Yankees up. and their refusal to perform in the music
halls on Sundays caused much blasphemy. The jteam
leaves for home on August 7.
* * * * '
If the Australian Statistician would keep a
special and separate tally for a year of the accidents that
happen on the racecourses of the Commonwealth, the
record would throw an interesting light on the question
of dangerous and brutal sport. At Moorefield (Sydney),
on Saturday, there w'as a smash in which four horses fell.’
One had to be destroyed, and the four jockeys w-ere sent
to hospital. Although the injuries are said to be slight,
one of the boys has a damaged spine and all are suffering
from shock. The worst feature of the case is that the
smash is said to have resulted from the foul tactics of
someone unspecified. This sort of miscreant should he
rooted out with great thoroughness. His tvpe is too
numerous in racingoircles. Boxing, football and therest
of the sports are safe compared with racing. The onlv
enjoyments that appear to be near the same level are
burglary and cockfighting.
• * * *
During the 11 years between 1890 and 1910, no
less than £16,521,521 passed through the Maoriland tote,
which is equal to £1,501.956 per annum. What went
through the bookmakers’ bags is not checked, except by
the remorseful losers, and only the-Ring and its father,
whose name is never mentioned in polite society, have
any idea. All the man in the street knows is that it is an
enormous sum, This Writer is not in any way inclined to
be a Primitive Unitarian, but these figures grieve him.
To think of Maorilanders spending all that money and
never having a decent flutter for it is pathetic. Gambling
is a futile thing unless there is a chance of making a
competence at one stroke. Only a small soul is content
to gamble for 15. One can always gauge the character
of a man by his gambles. The humdrum individual,
intended to be a splitter of wood and a dragger of water
all his days, gambles at the most for half a week’s wages.
Men in whom reposes, deep down perhaps, the potential-
ities of greatness gamble for larger sums or bigger prizes.
In fact, the p issible really great man never gambles on a
racecourse; he can find greater excitement in other
flutters for prizes that may affect the whole of his future
career. If he should stoop to gamble on horses- which is,
after all, a tame business compared with founding a new
religion or building a new terrace of wild cats—then he
goes in heavily and plays for a fortune. If Maoriland
must spend £1.500,000 annually, why on e rth doesn’t it
get a d-cent gamble for its money in the shape of a huge
quarterly consultation with £400,000 to divide up
between the State (as promoter) and the winners. Four
hundred thous ndquid!!! That would mean at least
half a dozen solid fortunes going quarterly !

The Australian Hurdle Race, due at Caulfield
next Saturday, looks a pretty good thing for the Penance
horse, King's ltansom, who is asked to carry on!y9st. 131 b.
With the same weight up, King’s Ran»om won the Cup
Hurdl over three miles at Flemington last November,
and at last Grand National meeting he ran third to Obi
in the DomtaGalla Hurdle. Over the easier Caulfield
jumps, King’s Ransom gave one clumsy exhibition,
when, probably, out of sorts ; but there is no denying
that the horse can jump, and has been improving with
every gallop of late. Last week he won a jumpers’ flat
race at Sandown (Melb.) in good style, and, in the words
of Anderson’s melodrama, his show for next Saturday’s
long race is the chance of a lifetime.

The stake money in S. Ans. went mostly to
ocal owners during the- past vear. Victorian visitors
failed in their Cup-hunting in Adelaide. R. M. Hanker,
who buys high-priced youngsters all over Australia,
won £2560. Only two others, J. James and A. P. Bow-
man, went over uIOOO, but nearly 50 won £250 or more.
Lots of Adelaide doctors, lawyers, and such like own a
horse or two just for the love of the game, and the cer-
tainty of totalisator receipts enables the clubs to cut
entrance-fees low ; so a small stake occasionally pays
expenses. Howie, McCann and J. James were the most
successful tr dners ; Marshall Liddle and Campbell the
leading jockeys ; Pistol, To-tig and Malster the sires on
top of the list. The figures sweep in Broken Hill, where
the racing is done mostly by S. Aus. horses.
* * * *
Gordon Inglis : A. F. Wilding, the M.L.
crack, had by no means a smooth passage to the final of
the tennis championship at Wimbledon (England) on
June 30. He had to defeat in turn Roper Barrett, M. J.
G. Ritchie, J. C. Parke and Otto Froitzheiin. In his
match with Beals Wright he succeeded in wiping out the
Davis Cup singles of 1908, thus gaining the right to meet
A. W. Gore in the Challenge round. This was played
alternately in sunshine, gloom and rain—the English
climate had a day out. Gore made a great attempt
to retain his title, and, considering he is 17 years
older than Wilding and was winning matches before
the latter was born, the fight he put up ought to
stand to his credit when some of his victories are for-
gotten. Wilding’s game shows a material advance. His
service and forehand drive retain their old terrors, while
his backhand and overhead work have greatly improved.
Australasian players have done their share towards swell-
ing the Wimbledon banking account. Norman Brookes
drew large crowds in his triumphal 1907 meeting, and at
the last meetings of Wilding with Wright and Gore over
5000 people paid their money at the gates.

The Royal Sydney Yacht Squadron is not
going to be allowed to sleep on the Sa.vonara Cup. The
Victorian Royal Yacht Club asked and received an ex-
tension of time for a challenge for next snuner. In the
meantime it cabled Mr. Dixon, owner of the Sayonara,
asking him if he intended to carry out his threat to build
a 12-metre yacht to challenge for the Cup. At time of
writing no reply had been received from Mr. Dixon. The
Brighton Yacht Club, the register of which includes all
the crack boats on Port Phillip, has also asked the
R.Y.S. if a challenge will be accepted from it; though
what yacht it can put up with anv chance of success
against the slippery-heeled Culwu’lla 111. is hard to
think of.
* * * *
The irritating thing about aviation is the
sudden irruption of the unknown performer. Every
now and again it is announced that a man named Smith
or Brown has flown 400 miles over mountain, city and
plain, or done some other amazing feat. And then
Smith, or whatever'his name is, just drops out of view,
and the show aviator is worried and haunted by a feel-
ing that somewhere, lying low, is a man who is a past
master at the game. Some timeago a man whose name
was not even mentioned built an aproplane in his back-
yard, fitted it with an engine, hauled it out by hand and
started right across the United States, landed at his des-
tination, and vanished once more into private life. Now
it is cabled that two Lombards, no names mentioned,
have bi-planed to the summit of the Trois Seigneurs,
7750 ft. high, in the Alps. Whether they came back from
the topmost peak of the Three Bosses is not explained.
It is all vervirritating.
* * * *
Seems that Robl, the ex-pace follower who was
killed aviating a few weeks back, lost his life through
the insistence of the crowd which had paid for admission.
Bad weather set in and the flyers went on strike. But
the crowd growing nasty, Robl offered to make a flight.
He did so, and at 250 ft. altitude he was blown over. His
machine crashed to the ground among the spectators,
but unfortunately did not kill one or two of the worst
specimens. Robl was quite unrecognisable, and was, of
course, killed instantlv.
*'***■
Professional cyclist Fred Alpin is well-nigh
the speediest pedaller at present to be found in the ranks
of the N.S.W. League of Wheelmen. Previous to Satur-
day, the best effort over 10 miles- on the road stood at
24min., but Alpin put up new figures for the distance
by getting over the stretch in 23min. 27 1-5 sec. It was a
sterling effort, and likely to withstand many onslaughts.
* * * *
Sydney baseball premiership struggle is drawing-
near the end, after the liveliest season on record. The
result is a good thing for Burwood, which club, by
winning 12 out of 13 matches, accumulated 25 points.
North Sydney was next on 15 points, the other eight
clubs not being in the hunt. OnSaturday.gr de matches
will rest while the Melbourne club assaults Burwood at
Sydney Cricket Ground.

trol will, as a matter of principle, refuse the offer of the
Sydney Sun to indemnify it to the extent of £5OOO for the
African ciicket tour. In that case the Sun will commu-
nicate directly with the cautious Afrikanders and offer
them the guarantee, and then the Afrikanders will, of
course, pack < heir gripsacks and start for the land of the
Kangaroo right away.
* * * *
Overlander Francis Birtles, who left Sydney a
few months ago to traverse Australia on a bicycle, has
pedalled out of the hush at Turkey Creek, way up in the
northern section of Westrilia. A battle with blacks
varied the monotony of the exercise, and helped to ki-ep
him from emul iting Johnson, who sat on a crocodile in
the Northern Territory. Birtles is mounted on one of
Anthony Horderns’ Universal bicycles—the same machine
which carried him across Australia on a previous journey.
_ The Five Miles Cross Country Championship of
N.S. W. was quite an important function this year, inas-
much as it attracted more public attention than usual.
East Brisbane Harriers Club sent a team to Sydney for the
occasion, but some of the young' men were not gifted
with that reserve and self-control that makes for perfec-
tion in athletics. They impaired their wind by howling
like dingoes at night in the cold air on the balconies of
the hash-tower which had the misfortune to accommo-
date them. The other competitors w ere Botany, South
Sydney, East Sydney, Redfern and Newtown Harriers.
The course was chiefly round the Victoria Park race-
course, with a stretch across the horrible country that
surrounds that delightful oasis in the wilderness of Zet-
land. Ivan Coxon, of South Sydney, made the pace twice
round the course and led the field out of the gate
and away towards Rosebery Park. But he blew out at
the first sandhill, and Fitzsimmons, his club-mate, took
charge for a time. On the run back from Rosebery Park
Wevman, of Botany, led the way over the horrible, heart-
wrecking sandhills, and looked a certain winner right on
to the racecourse. It was when rounding the bend into
the straight that Temple Wood, of East Sydney club,
came with a mighty rush and, passing Wevman, got
home 15yds. to the good. But that represented merely
the individual placing. When the points were dealt out
it was found that Botany Harriers had won with a margin
of seven points from South Sydney, which was followed
by East Sydney, with 49 more points debited against it.
The others trooped in in the following order: East
Sydney, East Brisbane (the howlers on the balcony), Red-
fern and Newtown.
* ■* *
The billiard match between old man John
Roberts and young Fred Lindrum (18,000 up, with Lin-
drum receiving 3000 start), at Billiards Ltd., Sydney, was
a mighty struggle which lasted a whole fortnight. In
the early part of the match Roberts held the advantage ;
hut youth asserted itself, and half-way through Lindrum
led by 1802 points—3o2 points ahead of the sessional
average. The battle during last week was brilliant, and
though, at times, Roberts spurted (particularly in one
break of 500 in 35min.), Lindrum’s consistency stood to
him, and he ran to the 18,000 mark when Roberts was
anchored at 15,693—a clear advantage of 2307 points.
Roberts only gained 693 points in the long tussle, and,
after Lindrum’s showing in the matches at Melbourne
and Sydney, the old man should climb down off his tall
steed and give the Australian champion a level event.
The highest breaks in the match were 500, 414, and 49
other efforts over 100, by Roberts; while Lindrum’s best
were 391 (twice) and 45 other scores over the century.
* * * *
The advent of John Roberts is bringing a boom
in billiards. In the coming week, the veteran will
attempt to concede Fred Weiss 2000 in 12,000 at Sydney ;
after which matches with Lindrum, Weiss, and possibly
Englishman A. E. Williams at Melbourne, will be pro-
ceeded with. Lindrum and Weiss will also battle for the
Australasian championship; and when these matches
have blown themselves out, all four shining lights are
billed to rush off to England in search of gold and glory.
“Snell ” : I feel there’s an enormous scope for
disagreement with “ 0.K.” in his lengthy animadversion
on the pug industry (B. 21/7/’I0). He scorns pugilism,
and rejoices, as a white man, that stoushing has finally
been branded a black man’s game, because fist fighting is
a miserable human imitation of a business in which the
brutes excel. In support of this view he instances the
fact that, as a puncher, man is a wretched failure when
compared with the lion or the tiger. Does “0.K.” make
nothing of the casual circumstance that Beaurepaire is a
despicable amphibian compared with a seal, and would be
donkey-licked by a barracouta in a 100 yd. sprint?
Furthermore, Postle can’t run for nuts as com-
pared with an antelope, and George Blake’s five-
mile record has been beaten by 9000 ostriches and
motor-cars. Because of this deplorable human incom-
petence must swimming as a sport be abandoned to cod,
pedestrianism be given over to springbok, and high-
jumping be forsaken as a pathetic compliment to the
superiority of the ’roo? The low types “ 0.K.” finds at a
fight are those he may discover at a race meeting or a
football match. They are not ring products to any
extent. If one sport is more responsible than another
fo'- their degradation, it is horse racing—a business that
deliberately cultivates physical deterioration in the
humans who take active part in it. Johnson’s triumph
at Reno is a slap for the white, since it represents the
superiority of the coon in a sphere in which hundreds of
thousands of fine young palefaces are striving to excel.
If it is nothing for the white to be inferior in courage
and strength and coolness and skill, the negro’s success
is of no consequence ; but meanwhile “ 0.K.” does not
alter the case by calling those virtues hard names.
* * * *
Stevenson would probably have got the decision
in his wrestling bout with Shima at Broken Hill had he
not lost his temper. He had the first and fourth falls to
his credit and was going strong, while the Jap was about
worn out through securing the second and fifth. In the
sixth, the deciding bout, Shima’s head bashed Stevenson
heavily, and the white man appealed for a foul. It was
disallowed, whereupon the professor is reported to have
seized the Jap by the paw and banged him heartily, and
the referee gave the Jap the verdict on a foul.
“ The Passing of the Third Floor Back ” is said to have
been translated into Russian for a St. Petersburg
manager. The supernaturalism of the Stranger will pre-
sumably be emphasised by some extra limelight effects
in Russia, unless the censor prohibits the play as a
socialistic sermon in disguise. It raises a question as to
what would happen if the Stranger came and lodged with
the Czar.

Warrnambool (Vic.) is taking its coat off
and preparing to bog into the business of re-
discovering an alleged Spanish galleon that is
vaguely reputed to lie somewhere six miles NW.
of the village. The wrecked vessel was first seen
by Port Fairy sealers in ’36, and in 46 Captain
Mason, of Belfast, saw “the hull of a vessel high
and dry in the hummocks, far above the reach of
the tide. It appeared to have been of about 100
tons burden, and must, from its appearance and
position, have been there very many years. The
timber composing it was either mahogany
or cedar.” The local aborigines, when ques-
tioned, averred it “was always there.” It was
last seen in 1880, but nobody appears to have ever
taken exact bearings, and since then shifting
sands have covered up the derelict, and now no-
body knows just where to look for it. Nobody
knows, either, whether it will be worth tuppence if
it is found. But the “ Wake up, Warrnambool,”
cry has got hold of the inhabitants, and they are
going to try to dig up the remains—before going
to sleep again.
Contrary to what might be expected of it,
South Aus. isn’t by any means such a Bible-buyer
as some of the other States. Of course, the
British and Foreign Bible Society doesn’t do all
the trade in the good book, but it does a good
deal of it. And according to agent Kennedy, now
in Melbourne endeavoring to raiste £lO,OOO for
a Bible House—whatever sort of an emporium
that may be—the Ma State last year took 21,600
Bibles; Victoria, 16,400; S. Aus., 1600; Tas-
mania, 1000; and Queensland, 12,000. Appa-
rently Bananaland is preparing to turn over a
new leaf, and will presently quit borrowing, throw
out Phidston, and tread the narrow track in real
earnest. But the S. Aus. figures seem discrepant
whichever way you look at them. Possibly the
citizens hand the volume down from father to
son, and, being already well supplied, need buy
few more; or possibly they know the Scriptures
by heart, and. being thrifty,,save the money to
put on the tote ; or, again, they may be wearing a
false reputation for godliness, and don’t really
indulge in .the Word at all.

VIEW OF CAIRNS,
MR. DONALD McDONALD (the well-known writer)
describes the trip as follows: —
«t- **OR the greater part of the journey on the Queensland coast the steamer runs
r“* between the Great Barrier Reef and the mainland, making it one of the smoothest
of the sea trips of Australasia. The route is dotted with picturesque islands,
covered with foliage strange to southern eyes, and the boat is ever rounding headlands,
steaming into fine bays and estuaries. The famous Whitsunday Passage, between Mackay
and Bowen, is extremely picturesque.
“ In a short railway ride of 20 miles from Cairns one may travel through some of the
finest scenery in Australasia, culminating in the magnificent Barron Falls—the king of
cataracts. The trains run most of the way along the Barron Gorge, in scenery wild and
majestic, and the trip is one which no tourist going northwards should miss. I have ridden
on the box seat of a coach through the famous Bullerand Otira Gorges of New Zealand, and
looked practically down into eternity, with only a solid foot of roadway intervening; but it
is not more thrilling than this railway run through the Barron Gorge, where there is always
a comforting feeling that your lease of life is not terminable by a false step on the part of
one of the leading horses. The trains stops at the Falls, of which there is a superb view.
There is no necessity to rhapsodise—go and see it.”
CAIRNS
is picturesquely situated, has a healthy climate, and the temperature in
winter seldom falls below 50°. First-class hotel accommodation at Cairns
and Kuranda (Barron Falls) can be obtained at a reasonable tariff, and
the fishing at Cairns is excellent. A trip to the Barron Falls and gorgeous
scenery about that part of North Queensland should not be missed.

“Camera” : How futile are the disparagements of pic-
ture shows which professing “sufferers” put into smart
paragraph form! The filmophobiacs are guilty of the
wildest tarradiddles. They gravely assert that the picture
business is “ dead ” in places where it is most alive and
booming, or they flippantly suggest that its regular
patrons will soon be surfeited. The filmophobiacs, seem-
ingly, are unemployed mummers and their admiring
sympathisers, pub-keepers and their faithful apostles,
and people whose notions of popular taste in entertain-
ment are formed at Riekards’s. Speaking as one who has
known the picture business from its feeble flicker infancy
to its present lusty youth, and who merely looks in occa-
sionally to see how it is getting on, I would say that its
popularity is assured. The “comic” pictures are less
puerile and less frequent than of old, the dramas
are stronger in design, the educational subjects
more interesting, the spectacular-fact pieces better
arranged, and the all round production of the photo-
graphs is wonderfully improved. Then there is the
orchestral accompaniment, which, with some pictures,
is half the effect. The King’s funeral passing silently
across the screen whilst a well-knit orchestra plays
selections from the classic dead marches, is more im-
pressive than the original show could have been.
Pictures are the weekly entertainment of more people
than go regularly to the theatres, whilst their occasional
visitors grow' more numerous every month. Statistics
show it, and the pictures themselves explain the fact.
Why, the cinematograph is but just beginning to realise
its possibilities, and the prosperous managements are
only young students of their business.
Two true stories of small Paris theatres. One of them
was doing poor business with a rotten show but a charm-
ing leading lady. So the management advertised:
“ Pretty and well-known actress wishes to meet gentle-

“Arcadian” Jockey.

to the above.
Letters only.”
Every inquirer—
and there were
swarms of them
—r ecei v e d a
photo, and an
appointment to
meet (at the
theatre), and
business boomed.
The other dodge
was more subtle.
A small house
was rehearsing
diligently for a
much-advertised
play, “Banco.”
The first night
was postponed,
and special at-
tention was given
to “Act 2;

The police began

was most im-

have ever been written.
“Lud”: The first electric light seen in London was
when Manager Hollingshed flashed a searcher from the
Gaiety Theatre roof, in 1869. There was a public outcry
that it was a disgraceful thim;, scaring horses, etc. In
1878 he used electric light for his theatre front. About
that time Professor Erasmus Wilson said the first and last
of the electric light were seen at the Paris Exhibition.
It will be remembered how this light was scoffed at in
meetings of the Sydney and Melbourne gas companies.
“Wang”: I enthusiastically back up “Bloggs’s” re-
marks concerning the “ standing room only ” notice out-
side theatres ; but I wish to add that, when a manager is
hauled before the beak for overcrowding his show, he
should simultaneously be arraigned for fraud. lam not
so fortunate as “ Bloggs ” in the matter of a seat ; mine
is in the upper circle, and I pay an extra bob for the early
door. When you are on this lurk you get inside about
7.30 p.m., and secure a comfortable seat until a creature
with hands the size of frying-pans comes along,
flops them on your shoulders and says, “Move up,
please.” It is no use protesting. Frying-pan raises
a row, and the major-domo or chief steward or
somebody comes along and tells you to get a
move on streetwards if you don’t like it. No
mention is made of the two bob being returned.
Choking with the desire to murder the frying-pan man
and assassinate the janitor, you make room, and, just
about then, the late doors, at a bob a time, surge in. Half
a dozen more frying-pan men shove, push, propel and
grip your neck, and bang you against your neighbor, and
stand on vour girl’s knee until the curtain rises,
when your final objection is cut off by a thousand or so
voices hissing, “Shut up!" By this time your clothes are
dirty, your ear bulged, your temper torn in 10 places,
and not all the platitudes of the hero or the outraged
palpitations of the heroihe’s 14 x 7 bosom can take the
kink out of your neck.
“E.”: The advertisement curtain at Melbourne Royal
is but a mild outrage compared with the booklet or
encyclopaedia issued as a playbill. The programme, ex-
clusive of its wrapper, is a 16-page publication, wherein
the information that people look'for is scattered ip frag-
ments. A portion of the first page is devoted to a state-
ment of the show ; then, after an interval of six irrelevant
pages, comes a bit of the cast of characters. This is
continued, with particulars re scenery, etc., on six sub-
sequent pages—a small allotment on each page—and all
the rest of the space is given to trade advts., relieved by
occasional portraits and streaks of theatrical matter. As
a commercial speculation the programme is doubtless
satisfactory to the persons who buy space in it, but as a
book of reference for the theatreaudienceitis as trouble-
some as a railway time-table. Programmes of old existed
to tell you about the show. Now they exist to urge you
to drink somebody’s corset or wear somebody’s tight
whiskj\
The twice-nightly theatre is spreading widely in
England, particularly in crowded centres. It fetches in
the many who can get out at seven, after tea, and who
want to be home again by about nine ; also, the many
more who are in shops that close at eight, and can do
with an evening’s fun that runs from nine to 11. The
performers are probably not paid much more than for a
single show, and admission prices can of course be cut
low. The programme is Variety mostly—but not always.
Plays as solid as “ Caste ” are now being sprinted through
twice in the four hours.
“ The Undersigned ”: Attended Melbourne Royal the
other night to see “The Merry Wives of Windsor.”
Chance led me to a seat behind a couple who seemed to
make hard work of it following the play. During an act
or so they spoke scornfully of the piece and the actors,
the lady audibly declaring that it was all tomfoolery and
the gentleman scoffing at large. In the middle of the
second act the lady suggested leaving, but the male said
he would chance it a little longer just to see the waltz.
His neighbor, scentingan opportunity to shunt the pair,
politely intimated that the only dancing in the play was
at the end, and that in any case there was no waltz.
“ What,” said the male nuisance, “don’t Asche and Miss
Clayton dance the Merry Widow waltz in this?” He
was assured that that was in another play altogether.
That decided them, and in a hurried whisper they agreed
to ro across to Rickards’s, where they could get some-
thing sensible for theit; money. As the lady threw on
her Chantecler headgear and jabbed in a hatpin, she
was understood to mutter, through the two others held
in her mouth, her complete dissatisfaction at the de-
liberate dishonesty of some theatrical people in attaching
misleading names to their plays for the purpose of
snaring the coin of folk who were out in search of in-
tellectual food.

ONE HUNDRED AND TWENTY-FOURTH HALF-YEARLY REPORT of
The Commercial Banking Company of Sydney
LIMITED.
Presented to the Shareholders at an Ordinary General Meeting held at the
Head Banking House, George Street, Sydney, on Friday, 22nd July, I’JIO.
THE Directors submit to the Shareholders a Balance-Sheet showing the Liabilities and Assets of
the Bank on the 30th June, 1910, and present the following Report:—
The balance at the credit of Profit and Loss Account, after deducting rebate on current bills and
accrued interest on fixed deposits, reducing premises account, paying note-tax, and providing for
land and income taxes and bad and doubtful debts, as well as granting to the Officers a special lionus,
amounts to £149,610 14s. 4d., out of which the Directors recommend the following appropriations:—
To Payment of a Dividend for the half-year at the rate of 10 per
cent, per annum ... ... £75,000 0 0

~ Balance being undivided Profit ... 34,610 14 4

This meeting is called upon to elect a Director, consequent on the retirement, in terms of the
Articles of Association, of the Honorable Henry Moses, M.L.C., who is eligible for re-election, and
has given tfye requisite notice that he is a candidate for the office. It also rests with the meeting to
appoint Auditors for the ensuing year.
Duriner the half-year Branches have been established at Ariah Park, Burren Junction, Campsie,
Culcairn. Ganmain and Grenfell, in this State ; and at Atherton, in the State of Queensland.
The Dividend, free of income tax, is now payable.

We hereby certify that we have examined the bills, compared the balances, and counted the coin
in the Head Ciffice of The Commercial Banking Company of Sydney, Limited, and have compared
the returns of the Branches, and have found the same as specified in the foregoing Balance-Sheet.
W. L. DOCKER, {.
E. S. CAPE, »
Auditohs.
The following resolutions were curried unanimously :
“That the Report and Statements of Account be received and adopted, and ordered to be circu-
lated among the Proprietors.”
“That the Honorable Henry Moses, M.L.C., be re-elected a Director of the Bank.”
“That E. S. Cape, Esq., and F. W. Hixson, Esq., be appointed Auditors for the ensuing year.”
“ That the thanks of the Shareholders be presented to the Directors, General Manager, and
Officers of the Bank for their very satisfactory management of the affairs of the institution.”
T. A. DIBBS, General Manager.
In moving the adoption of the Report and Statements of Account, the Chairman, Mr. George J.
Cohen, said :
I am pleased to say that the Directors are able to-day to present to you a very strong and satis-
factory Balance-Sheet for the half-year just ended, and one that shows a marked expansion in the
Bank’s business. /
In comparing it with that of the corresponding period of last year it will be seen that the total
figures have increased over two and one-half millions in the twelve months. Looking at the debit side
you will notice the Reserve Fund has been increased by £BO,OOO ; in the same period Notes in Circu-
lation and Bills in Circulation, items which fluctuate with the requirements of the public, have
together increased £107,000, the main increase being a trifle under £2,350,000 in Deposits and other
Liabilities. *
Turning to the other side you will find our liquid assets have been increased by £2.338.000, to a
total of £10,870,000, equfil, I might add, to about fifty-five per cent, of our total liabilities to the
jjuuny.
This total is made up of increases in our Gold and Bullion in round figures of £1,660,000 ; money
at short call, £194,000 ; additional investments in Government,.Securities, £379,000 ; Bills Receivable
in London, etc., and Notes and Bills of other Banks. £169,00(1
Bills Discounted and all Debts due to the Bank show an increase of £185,000; and Bank
Premises Account, £16,000.
The Board has granted a special bonus to the staff. The Bank can well afford it, and the Officers
certainly deserve it. , .
Finding the business of our Exchange Branch, in Pitt Street, has outgrown our premises there,
we have purchased the building opposite, known as “ Ramsay’s Chambers.” Plans of the Banking
Chamber are being prepared to suit the full requirements of our Customers, and it is proposed to let
out in offices the upper floors. As soon as the alterations are made to the new purchase the old
building will be sold.
You will note that our Reserve Funds now total £1,414,000. Having in view the large increase
in our deposits, as shown in the figures now placed before you, it is the policy of the Board to
augment the Reserve Fund from time to time.
You are all aware it is ip contemplation bv the Federal Government to issue Notes to replace
those now in circulation by the Banks. This we consider a retrograde movement, for it is against
the evidence of financial experts in all countries.
So far as this Bank is concerned we shall not suffer in profit,, j&s we shall be saved the cost of the
Notes, and the 2 per cent.'Note tax we now pay for the privilege of dtk circulation, say about £13,1 0J
per annum. Of course, more gold will now be paid over the counter.
I now beg to move that the Report and Balance-Sheet be adopted.

Manly, Sydney’s marine suburb, although a
charming place with a sleepy and a dolee far viiente
atmosphere, has always labored under the serious disad-
vantage of having few hostelries where one can com
bine the comforts of civilisation with the ozone of the
rest resort. Ordinary hotels and hn*h-towers have failed
to fill the bill, and the consequence is that thousands of
people who would love to go to Manly have stayed
away. This trouble is drawing to a close. The Manly
Hotel Co., Limited, steps into the breach. It has pur-
chased the old Clarendon Hotel, and its 173ft.0f frontage
to the Esplanade, and on the site will erect a splendid
modern hotel, modelled on the best European and Ameri-
can models. There will be accommodation for all pur-
poses, from the palatial suite of rooms, with ex-
clusive attendance, down to the apartment of the
ordinary casual guest who takes a nice bal-
cony room for his wife and children, and has his
meals at the table d'hote. A splendid eafd, for the
benefit of day visitors will he a special feature of the
establishment. The co„ is being floated by Frank J.
McDonald, Solicitor, of Royal Chambers, Sydney, and ail
the shares hare been, underwritten. Subscription list
will be opened for one week only from August 15_*
AT POVERTY POINT.
" Vlo.": Matheson Lang makes the religious play more
aoooptable than any previous actor. He is cousin of
the Archbishop of York and nephew of Dr. Marshall
Lang, who preached in Melbourne. So theology runs in
the blood. The new piece is not a daub, like “ The Sign
of the Cross” and “ Barabbas.” I fancy “A Message
from Mars” suggested it, but it is better than that. The
suooess of that old mystery play, “Everyman,” set
authors thinking. Julius Knight was never better than
in this, as seen at Melb. Town Hall. He has a suitable
oharnel-ly tone in the voice. When he sunk into the
coffin he was all there. He would read Dante’s Inferno
well, where Dante kicks the skull of the poor chap under
the ice, or where Ugolino bites the back of his enemy’s
head through all eternity. Here are some religious
plays: Rostand’s “La Samaritaine,” rapturously wel-
comed in the little Renaissance Theatre, Paris, on a Good
Friday night, with Bernhardt as the Woman of Samaria,
and a most reverent and beautiful actor as Jesus. James
O'Neill, at San Francisco, acted the Saviour in a play
which included the crucifixion. It was announced else-
where, but prohibited. Then Massenet’s opera of
“Herodiade,” introducing John the Baptist, is familiar
in Paris. There are also Strauss’s “ Salome,” and Oscar
Wilde’s ditto. Saint-Saens’s opera of “Samson and
Delilah” is only just allowed in England. Alfieri’s
*• Saul" is a grand tragedy. Two operas on the Queen of
Sheba are by Gounod and Goldmark.
Amy Castles to an Argus interviewer : “ Isn’t it lovely
to be back in my own Melbourne again after so
many weeks in Maoriland, where the people are hospit-
able, but the weather isn’t. lam a lover of sunshine and
Australia and all things bright! I longed to be home
again.” Now, it was very sweet of Amy to glorify her
own Melbourne weather by comparing it with Maoriland
ditto, whilst taking care to praise the M.L. people; but
she can’t persuade, say, the Federal legislators, who are
an all-round body of Australians, that Melbourne it a
little bit merry and bright during the winter months.
For some days before Amy’s arrival the sky was grey and
the rain pretty persistent. Two years ago there were
six successive weeks in Melbourne when the sun remained
Invisible. It is, in fact, the last big city of Australia that
a lover of sunshine would select for winter quarters, and
the present opera season there is in danger of being
somewhat d d by the weather.
“ Mahlstick ”: The Argus republishes a selection from
Tom Carrington’s caricatures of public men, given in the
Weekly Times in the last generation. There should be a
good number in the National Gallery; but there isn’t.
There is only one lady, a fat-and-forty frosty prima
donna, Mrs. Palmieri, imported by Lyster. Her husband
brought out a miraculous fire escape, which long encum-
bered the Town Hall basement. Only a few of the por-
traits are bad. Coppin is the one atrocity. Harwood is
all right. He is there in his long beard—one of the very
few English or Austral Thespians who have liked a beard.
Ho would grow it on any pret- nee. After a trip to Eng-
land he made a speech at the Theatre Royal, in his long
face-trimmings, between the pieces. Then he appeared in
a few minutes clean shaved, as Cassandra, in “ The Siege of
Troy.” The French leading man often wears his own
beard ; and a man of the Matheson Lang cut might
enchant Australian ladies if he did the same.
Actors are apt to grow somewhat reckless in farewell
speeches. When the Melbourne gaud mayoress gave an
afternoon-tea party in honor of the Asches the day before
their departure Oscar said: “ For myself I would ask
nothing better than to remain in the Commonwealth;
but my wife, who is English-born, has longings for her
own people.” He forgot that on the previous Friday
night he had said to the audience at the Royal: “Mv
wife has not sufficient nerve to thank you for herself. As
a matter of fact, she does not want to go away at all.
But Desdemona must follow Othello "to the wars,
Petruchio must bear away his Katharine.” Of course,
Osoar, being ambitious and full of go, must get back to
the wars, and naturally his wife is thinking of her own
people. But why this muddle ?
“0.K.”: Can see no real family likeness between
“ The Passing of the Third Floor Back ” and “ A
Message from Mars,” or Dickens’s “ Christmas Carol.” In
the older stories the supernatural visitors appeared only
in a dream, and in each case there was only one person
to be converted. Horace in the comedy and Scrooge in
the “ Qarol ” were respectively an everyday, selfish, lazy
young capitalist and a miserly misanthropical old cur-
mudgeqn. They were changed into practical, warm-
hearted Christians by imaginary visitors from another
world—ghosts in Scrooge’s case—who showed them
dream-pictures, and reproved them for their selfishness.
The Third Floor Back lodger is introduced’as an actual
faotrwhp transforms 11 wide-awake contemptible citizens
into sweet-dispositioned creatures by simply looking - 'at
them and giving them “ taffy.” By spiritual influence he
does what the woman in Barrie’s comedy of “Little
Mary ” does to the people whom she cures of dyspepsia.
As the result of changing their dinner-hour and reducing
their consumption of food they all become merry and
bright and considerate for one another. The doctrine of
“ Little Mary ” is rational and wholesome, whilst that of
“ The Third Floor Back ” is irrational and soulsome, but
between the methods of their plots there is a humorous
resemblance.
“JeanSibi” does a “ show ” notice :-A somewhat
humorous entertainment was the Salv army s Daj
Salvation” at Melbourne Exhibition. from the start
Imbecility held the platform. Speaker after speaker,
full up to the brim with gaudy prayers and claptrap
hymns, raged, waved arms and defied all the forces o
logic and reason. They told fatuous stones of strange
conversions. They plundered their minds for yarns or
penitent sinners. They filled in the cracks of their dis-
course with rhodoniontade. Commissioner Hay was pro-
bably the most tempestuous of the lor. He was all frotn
and fire. He stamped and shouted. He wore the glare
of the bull in the Bovril advt. He fought for Belief with
fury, and outed Unbelief with frenzy and a violently-
throwii brick. Down below and surrounding him
was a vast mosaic of faces. These people re-
sponded to his slightest movement. When he upper-
cut Satan with a preposterous statement, they howled in
rapture and shrieked “ Alleyloover.” When he slapped
Lucifer with a piece of pious derision, they rocked in
unison and shrieked in concert. Whatever he did galva-
nised them into hysterical ecstasy and filled their mouths
with extravagant noise. And yet this man. who so
moved the crowd, is one of the crudest Salvationists in
the business. Possessing a fine sense of the theatrical,
he is, nevertheless, the epitome of the Cheap Tract and
the Intelligence That Won’t Grow Up.
“Surcingle” : Re Nat Gould’s play. “The Chance of a
Life-Time,” why the Ascot Gold Cup? Isn’t there a
Melbourne Cup? “No,” answers somebody; “hut
Blackwood used to add on a little 100-guinea cup to the
V.R.C. event.” Stealing that, of course, wouldn’t make
a drama. The thief must go for the entire stakes. Dar-
rell produced an Australian turf drama, “The Double
Event,” at least as good as Gould’s. But probably the
best horse drama of all is Boucioault’s “ Flying Scud.” It
ran 400 nights at the London Holborn, where they got
an equally long run out of its imitation, Merritt’s “ New
Babylon,” with Clarence Holt as Flotsam, the ’Tec, or
Deteekative, in stage Hebraic. Bland Holt was the
Australian Flotsam.
“ Early Door ” : The plaint of The Bulletin’s critic in
his “Sundry Shows” notice of “ The Brass Bottle ” (B
28/7/10), re‘the inordinate length of the first perform-
ance (three hours and 10 minutes), moves me to
rise and give a cheery
husk—l mean a
husky cheer. Why
should wearied
people,most of whom
have afterwards to
journey to distant
suburbs, be kept lin-
gering on in city
theatres till 11 p.m.
and after? There
seems to be a hard
and fast convention
that the play-builder
must hang on to
the coat-tails and
opera cloaks of his
customers somehow
until midnight is
well in sight, even
if he has to run in an
extra act to do it;
also, the theatre
people seem to have
the idea that the
patrons of the drama
will consider them-
selves defrauded un-
less they are kept in
tbeir seats till they
are half asleep, and
all but the last few
trams, trains and
boats have scooted
for their shelter-
sheds and wharves.
In my opinion, the
average amusement- .
hunter is not such a
whale for entertain-
ment as all that. In
fact, I think that a
theatre which ac-
quired a reputation
for closing up
promptly at 30.30
p.m. would become
a favorite house with
all classes of theatre-
goers. There are few
more pathetic sights
in this hurried world
than that of a hard-
working band of
actors endeavoring
to hold the attention of several hundred yawning
people at the end of a long evening. It is all so un-
necessary. Time was when the shortest song con-
sisted of 13 verses, with a chorus to be repeated
after every verse. That sort of song is now defunct;
people haven’t got time to listen to it, and demand
short, artistic numbers that don’t tax their powers of
endurance. Why can’t the comedy, the musical play and
the tragedy be pruned down likewise?
The Sunday-evening societies in London that go in for
encouraging the serious and unpopular drama have a bad
name for hitting on plays that lack popularity chiefly
through being frankly improper—and not funny. These
societies are independent of the Censor, and a “ Sunday
night play ” has come to mean an immoral play. The latest
production of the Incorporated Stage Society, a German
play, “ Champions of Morality,” dealt with the raiding of
a disorderly house under pressure from a local Vigilance
Society. The proprietress shows without difficulty that
the leading Purity men are down in her ledger as among
her best customers. (Sensation.) Then the local prince-
ling is found to beincluded in the haul which the police
have made. So the matter is hushed up. Finally, the pro-
prietress claims £lOOO or so for “ damage to remunerative
practice,” and the Purity men are bluffed into paying. It
might make a roaring Parisian farce, but it seems poor
stuff for a Superior society to specialise in.
“Silas”: To my idea, the brightest bit of work in
“ The Passing of the Third Floor Back ” is Torn Cannam’s
convincing ex-booky, the man who was compelled to
give up rooking the public when his voice broke. The
thing is cleverly done. The figure moves. And when
the Stranger gets at Joey, enlarging on his merits, and
weaning:him from infamy, the crippled hooky’s conver-
sion is a touching spectacle. In the silence that fell
upon a large audience on opening night I distinctly
heard the drip of falling tears. A hurried investigation
resulted in the discovery that six of Melbourne’s most
notorious layers of the odds were weeping bitterly in
the most expensive section of the house.
“Maitai”: When Hugh Ward put up his “Girl from
Rector's” pictures on the Wellington (M.L.) hoardings,
some parsons mustered and passed a resolution condemn-
ing the bright little comedy. The pilots alleged it to
be brimful of suggestive features, and deputationised the
Minister of Justice, demanding that it be put down. It
wasn’t; but an extra man had to be put on at the book-
ing office. Hugh Ward, doing the Quay this afternoon,
is reported to have raised his hat to no less than three
parsons.
“Edyson”: “Harrison O.” (B. 21,'7/’XO) hurls the
ancient cat of derision into the hair of those “ pedantic
and wildly impracticable” persons who prefer the plays
of Shaw, Barker and Galsworthy to the dramatic rot that
is the nightly fare of the frequent theatre-goer. Harri-
son drags in the “business aspect,” to the confusion of
the issue, through five-sixths of his feverish par., and then
abandons it with the assertion that even the average
“ literary person ” would rather sit through a perform-
ance of “ The Second Mrs. Tanqueray” than one of the
Shawian “ episodes ”or “ debates.” Does Harrison know
what he is talking about? How many “Second Mrs.
Tanquerays ” are we being refreshed with in Australia?
I have a fancy there is something radically wrong
with the literary side of the person who is not
satisfied with the dramatic element of “Arms and
the Man,” “Mrs. Warren’s Confession,” “Strife,” or
even “Candida,” and who prefers the furniture-smashing
Activity of the paying farce to the delicious humor and
sane movement of “You Never Can Tell,” or “The
Importance of Being Ernest,” or those delightful
Shawian trifles, “The Man of Destiny”~and “How He
Lied to His Wife.” Harrison may prefer the physical
activity of the managerial drama to the play of human
forces in which the soul is more in evidence than the
shinbone; but why should he howl? He and thousands
like him are getting what they want.
“ S.S.”: The Silver Ivingget-up is the nearest approach
human imagination, as now employed in and about the
drama, is cap ible of as a sartorial expression of profound
reverence. Jerome adopts it for his Stranger in “The
Third Floor Back.” And yet the flowing black cloak has
been the distinctive garb of the “ rooned gyurl” in melo-
d rani a for ages. Why the large black butterfly tie, so
long the property of the comic musician or the pallid
artist? And why the wig that looks like two-thirds of a
merino fleece ? In what sense are these things adjuncts
of other-worldliness? Apparently, Jerome never dreamed
of dressing this supernatural visitant as a bishop. In
point of fact, it is only necessary to put the Unknown
into gaiters, a black pinny and a shovel hat to reduce
“ The Third Floor Back ” to broad farce.
“Lud”: Hermann Vezin passes, aged 82. The
last of the veterans in Beerbohm Tree’s “School for
Scandal.” An unlucky man—a little too good for a mere
CRIMINAL CARELESSNESS.
BROWN (to Jones , straight from the roof): “ Devil
take yeh, yeh might have hurt a man!”
supporter, not quite good enough for a star. He married
Mrs. Charles Young, who had divorced her first husband.
The Youngs, with Mrs. Y.’s father and mother, the
Thomsons, were in the company which Coppin brought
from Launceston to Melbourne in 1845. Mr. and Mrs.
Vezin did not seem to agree, either. She committed
suicide by throwing herself from a high window, because
of grief for the loss of her daughter. Young died in
Sydney. Vezin was short, and not favored by Nature.
He was what is called an actor’s actor, like Wybert
Reeve, admired more by the profession than the
public. In early life he did extremely well, and
his ambition seemed to he justified." His most
successful performance was in “The Man of Airlie,” uni-
versally criticised as remarkable, yet never drawing and
continually followed with ill-luck. Vezin could not
acquire prestige. Hollingshead tried to bring him for-
ward in a very strong cast of the comedy of “John
Bull,” at the Gaiety, including also Phelps and Mathews.
However, they were regarded as a stale trio. Toole and
L. Brough were in the same cast. Vezin was the original
Dr. Primrose, with Ellen Terry, in “Olivia”; but Irving
later eclipsed him in the part. Irving would always
send for Vezin in an emergency, to replace him ; but this
might be because he did not think Vezin dangerous.
Thus he played Richard 111., Macbeth and other good
parts. Alwaj's praised—and that’s all.
“JeanSibi”: Anderson’s equine drama has some few
spots which tend to make the punter feel scornful.
There is no clerk of the course for the Gold Cup race, and
only the riders of the first and second horses weigh in
after the event. Moreover, some fastidious folk allege
that the leading jockey of Ned Duggan’s stable shouldn’t
spend his leisure in cleaning harness. They say that this
combats the tradition that no principal jock ever soils
his hands with such base employment. They also say
that it would bean advantage if Anderson supplied the
Gold Cup nags with number-d saddle-cloths. Personally
I think that this suggestion is a little too much. Ander-
son has adequately provided for the backs of his horses
with cloths like towels or serviettes. These, I imagine,
satisfy all artistic demands. As a matter of fact, the
race arrangements in “The Chance of a Lifetime” are
somewhat less open to criticism than they were in many
a Blandolt production, although they are infinitely less
funny than the circumstances, under which Smith Sim-
plicitas rides to victory.in “ The Arcadians ” ,

“Mell.: Remarkable how the Melbourne Royal has
got back its old prestige. It was toppled from the
leadership in 1855 bv Coppin’s Olympic. Coppin and
Brooke took the Royal in 1856, when it started on that
premiership which it maintained until 1862. Then the
genius of Jefferson gave the wooden Princess’s the lead
for some months. Coppin’s Haymarket, opened in 1862
could never shake the Royal, which came to the fore
again with Barry Sullivan in 1863. Thenceforward, until
the opening of the new Princess’s in 1885, it was the
leading house. Williamson, Garner and Musgrove leased
the old Princess’s, but mostly kept it shut till it was re-
built. The Opera House opened in 1872, and the Bijou,
opened in 1875, neverdeposed the Royal. Thedominating
theatrical firm made the Royal plav second fiddle to the
new Princess’s. It sunk until lifted by Bland Holt, He
was that sort of tenant who makes a place so good that
he is outbid. I understand that £lOO a week was the
Meynell and Gunn offer, at which Bland flung up the
sponge.
There has been much time, energy and good coin
of the realm wasted lately by the Rich Landlords
of Victoria. In fact, the action of the Landlords
has largely resembled that of the burglar who
lost his boots, jammed his finger, and chanced six
months’ gaol to secure a bag which was found to
contain nothing but a handkerchief and a tram
ticket. The Rich Landlords’ exertions have been
directed to retaining control of the State Upper
House. A little while ago Wrixon’s retirement
left a vacancy; and Wrixon was hardly out of
sight before Squatter Austin tore through the
constituency like a whirlwind. He has been
elected unopposed, and is now recovering his
breath. But every time Squatter Austin’s class
scores a victory of this sort it brings its own de-
struction nearer. The people can turn the flank
of all the Upper Houses in the- Commonwealth
by simply voting, at a referendum, that the power
now held by the State Governments and Parlia-
ments, and the assets and liabilities of the States,
shall be wholly transferred to the Australian Par-
liament, which shall then proceed to cut Aus-
tralia up into a reasonable number—2o or 30
perhaps—of small States, each with a handy
Constitution and a Legislature of one House
elected by adult suffrage. It is quite simple.
The Labor majority in Parliament can doit, aided
by the Labor majority in the country which put
that Labor majority into Parliament.. No one
need ask the assent of the State Assembly or
Upper House or Governor, any more than he
need ask the assent of the committee of the
Poultry Show. And every time the Legislative
Councils of Victoria or Tasmania, or any other
similar body, throws out a democratic measure, it
is bringing its downfall nearer. These bodies are
wholly at the mercy of the people, and they insist
in an insane sort of way on making the people
remember the fact.
+ :
The proposal in the new Federal Arbitration Bill
to give the President of the Court power to sum-
mon to a conference the parties to an industrial
dispute, made the N.S.W. Premier a very angry
State-Fright indeed. Said Wade :
The essence of the new departure was apparently a
conference—in itself an excellent idea ; but it. would be
intolerable to the public, whether employers or em-
ployees, to be hustled about the continent attending
conferences under Federal control when the matter
could be dealt with locally.
Well, last time_a big N.S.W. dispute was-dealt
with locally, Wade refused to force the stub-
born mine-owners to hold a conference with the
willing employees. Also, Wade was the man who
rushed special legislation through Parliament, by
the application of the gag, to rattle the keys of
the gaol in the ears of the men who were seeking
to bring about the “ excellent idea ” of a confer-
ence. It is largely, if not entirely, due to the
one-sided energy of Wade that the general public
reckons the time has passed for allowing great
industrial disputes to be dealt with locally—at all
events, while politicians of the Wade and Wood
persuasion are the local dealers.
A Christchurch (Maoriland) lirm is ap-
pealing to the Supreme Court against the de-
cision of a magistrate—just a plain magistrate—
who fined.it £30,000. At least he ordered it to
pay £lOO fine and £30,000, being threefold the
amount of certain allegedly dodged taxes. Ap-
parently a magistrate is a man of large discourse
in M.L. On this side he mostly tells the Tanked
One that it will be ss.
Alongside Parson Worrall, whom Prime
Minister Fisher pinned down the other day to the
sharp end of a gross inaccuracy, The Bulletin
proposes to nail Parson Spurr, of Melbourne.
Recently this careless preacher, while pouring
forth eloquence on the subject of the family life,
observed incidentally that “nearly 4000 infants
aged under one year perished in Melbourne last
year.” The audience seemed to receive this
piece of intelligence as proof of the iniquity
of living in Melbourne, though the most
casual recourse to official figures would prove the
wild inaccuracy of the pammical announcement.
According to the last “Victorian Year Book”
(page 302) only 4731 infants under one year
perished in Melbourne during the five gears 1904 8-
And, as a matter of fact, only 1129 one-year-olds
passed out in Melbourne during 1909 —not 4000.
Are parsons ever accurate?
THE ARCADIANS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Arcady is ecstasy,
The skies burn blue above ;
In every tree is minstrelsy,
In every heart is love.
“O Friend,” she said, “ let us away
To Arcady the blest ” ;
And arm-in-arm, at break o’ day,
They journeyed on their quest.
\
They went by hill, they went by vale,
The foreland ways they dared ;
And he grew tired and she grew pale,
Yet on and on they fared.
They met a sailor by the sea—
An old man grey and grim—
And stayed his steps, and wistfully
A question put to him.
“ Now I have felt the burning breath
Of red-lit lands afar,
And I have seen the grin of Death
Beneath the Northern Star.
“Yea, I have wandered far from home—
So far—ye may not guess,
And watched the grey waves wash with foam
The shores of Heathenesse.
“ And I have seen the isles that bask
All golden in the sea ;
But never saw I what ye ask—
This land of Arcady.”
The forest round them seemed to swoon,
No little leaf did stir,
And in a glade, made gold with noon,
They met a forester.
“ I know no Arcady,” he said,
“ But forest-war I know—
The wolf with dripping jaws of red,
The flying fawn and doe.
“ The hawk hangs hungry in the blue,
The leopard haunts the tree ;
And ye may tread the forest through,
Nor find your Arcady."
They journeyed on till, wan and weak,
They met a pilgrim grey.
He said, “ This Arcady ye seek
Ye left at break o’ day 1 ”

Consult any competent judge of billiard tables, and he’ll
promptly tell you that there are no tables equal to “ Alcock’s.”
Many of the leading billiard matches have been played on
these tables—the recent match between George Gray and Fred
Lindrum, jun., when Gray scored 836, 831 coming off the red,
thus establishing two world’s records, and for which Gray
received a gold medal, is an example. This score is another
proof of the marvellous accuracy and perfect construction of
our billiard tables.

FASHION AND FAME
OLD habits are often better than new ones, but not always.
For instance, the old-fashioned method of cleaning the
teeth and mouth with tooth-powders and tooth-pastes is a
great mistake, presuming the object in view is to possess sound
teeth, which is of course the real aim of all treatment of the teeth.
All who wish to keep their teeth sound and whole should clearly
understand that it is necessary they should accustom themselves to
cleansing the mouth and teeth with an antiseptic liquid!
Brushing the teeth with tooth-powders and tooth-pastes can
never safeguard them from tooth-destroying microbes, for the
simple reason that the parts which are most liable to attack, the
backs of the molars and the fissures and interstices of the teeth—
the very parts where the microbes live and thrive—are not purified,
because they cannot be reached by such things as powders and
pastes. Consequently the mischief being once sent up strengthens
its hold without interruption. But a liquid dentifrice can penetrate
everywhere, and when it is antiseptic it sweeps away every trace
of microbes. Odol is such a dentifrice, and has such a practical,
antiseptic effect that it arrests all bacterial inroads and fermenta-
tion processes.
The complete disinfection of the mouth and teeth resulting from
the 1 use of Odol is due to a remarkable property which this and no
othjer preparation possesses, which being absorbed by the surface of
the. mucous membrane, and forcing itself into the hollows of the
teeth, deposits a store of antiseptic matter whose purifying
L ' ..
action continues for some
with Odol, healthy teeth
hours. By a regular cleansing
are kept absolutely intact, while
teeth already
impaired are
preserved from
further de-
cay. For this
reason Odol
must certainly
be acknow-
ledged to be
the best of all
known prepara-
tions for cleans-
ing the mouth
and teeth.
The taste of
Odol is most
delicious and
refreshing.
Odol is sup-
plied to the
public in two
distinct flavors
“ Sweet
Rose” and
“ Standard
Flavor.” The
former is de-
lightfully mild, and in special favor with ladies, while, generally,
“ Standard Flavor” is preferred on account of its niore expressed
taste and refreshing and invigorating effect.
It should be borne in mind by purchasers that the full-
size flask is more advantageous to them than the haif-size
flask, as it will last TWICE as long.

IN
NANANA LAND
A Mr. Tinckvell, of Brooloo, near Gympie,
is alleged to have killed a quarter of an acre of
that imported curse, water-hyacinth, which was
growing in a creek on his farm by spraying it
with cattle-dip mixture. Five gallons, double
the strength of that used in the dip, was sufficient
for the murderous deed. The water in the creek
was not poisoned, and stock which drank it were
not affected in any way.
Queensland is setting a good example to
the other States in regard to the imported “ex-
pert,” and has apparently come to the decision
that the locally-grown article is just as good as, if
not better than, the article grown, trained or pro-
duced in the Cold Country, United States or
Canada. When dairy-expert Thomson unhar-
nessed from his job, the vaqancy was filled by a
local cow authority. Dodd, an imported chief
“ vet,” has been succeeded by C. <T. Pound,
Government Bacteriologist. A. H. Benson, the
boss overseer of the fruit fly and other pests,
having taken up a similar position in Tasmania,
has been succeeded by Ross, manager of the State
farms at Hermitage and Westbrook. His former
job of farm-manager has been taken over by
James Mitchell, late manager of Brisbane Accli-
matisation Gardens. Altogether, Bananaland
has filled quite a number of responsible positions
without yelling for help once.
Queensland’s sheep have got very close up
now to where the tally stood in 1892 —the last of
the big boom years—and, all going well, they
ought to reach the old record before 1910 is done
with. The 1892 tally was 21,708,310; but from
that point there was an avalanche of perishing
jumbucks till bedrock was reached at 7,213,985 in
1902. The number at the end of last year was
19,593,791. And if next year it reaches the sum-
mit and then starts to swoop down again, ex-
N.S.W. Astronomer Russell will probably
shoulder up his 6ft. of earth to remark that the
experience merely supports his ridiculed 19-year
cycle theory. But it will be more to the
purpose if somebody gets up and points out that
Queensland’s sheep-ownership still isn’t distri-
buted to any extent worth mentioning, since 245
squatters own 67 per cent, of the total, and GB4
holdings (many of them in the same hands)
include no less than 90 per cent, of the lot.
Anderson Dawson, who died the other
day, was one of the many unsuccessful Kimberley
(W.A.) miners. Then he settled in Charters
Towers, made things hum during the ’9l strike,
and was one of the 15 who climbed into Parlia-
ment on the back of that upheaval in 1893, having
for his mate as'game a fighter as himself, little
Johnny Dunsford. When Tom Glassey got politi-
cally unsound, “ Andy ” led the party, and formed
the first Labor Ministry in Australia. It only
held its grip for a week, because Boyd Dunlop
Morehead and his crowd, who promised to sup-
port it, turned turtle. But Morehead, being
mortgaged up to the eyes to the Q. N. Bank,
could hardly help himself. Andy passed his
mantle to Billy Browne, when he went into the
Senate with Higgs and Stewart. Of the 15 Labor
members who arrived in 1893, Dawson, Dunsford,
Browne, Lovejoy and King are dead ; Fisher is
Prime Minister ; Turley is President of the Senate,
and McDonald Speaker of the Reps. Daniels and
Hoolan dropped out. Cross, Kerr and Jackson
meandered after the fleshpots. Mat Reid retired
after a monotonous round of defeats, and only
Herbert Hardacre remains where the electors of
Leichhardt set him 17 years ago.
C. F. Pemberton, who succeeds R. T.
Darker on the railways as locomotive engineer,
got his early railway experience in India, where
lie was born. When only 21 years old he-was in
charge of the construction works on the railway-
then being built from Rawal Pindi to
Peshawar in the jaws of the Khyber Pass.
Four years later he came to Australia and
joined the Victorian Railway Department.
In another seven years—in 1891 —he was ap-
pointed foreman of the Queensland railway
workshops at Ipswich. Since then he has been
district locomotive superintendent at Rockhamp-
ton and Townsville. Last April he was appointed
locomotive superintendent, in succession to
Darker, who was promoted to Henry Horniblow’s
vacant shoes as locomotive engineer. Now he
again succeeds Darker, who has given up the
struggle with old age. As for Henry Horni blow,
he is dead and in Heaven.

MRS. BLITHER" So Mrs. McSlummicky called you a
gossip! Well , well! Did you give her the lie? ”
MES. BLATHER: “I did; but it took me all the morning to convince her.”
The Thursday Island pearler is finding out
what sort of a gentleman the Jap is when he
thinks he has you at his mercy. There are not
raa ny new colored divers arriving nowadays, for
, . , . .. J
tbe Australian policy isn t as elastic as it used to
he; so the old Jap hands begin to smile blandly
and put the screw on. The business has already
got to the point at which the Jap diver (practically
divers nre J.p, lessee tl ,e whole rern-oor
f roin the alleged pearler, and in return merely
undertakes to sell him the shell at £BO or £B5 per
ton. When this scheme started the pearler was
supposed to get half the pearl; but he has been
