in which case, of course, the pearler provides the
boats and the very perishable gear, and gets abso-
lutely nothing in return. The Aru Island
pearlers have got so tired of Itchi Nippon that
wherever possible Malays are being substituted.
And So
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I CLOTHED you in the fairest fancies
Of a poet’s necromancies ;
Not contented when I found you
Just to put my arms around you.
I built for you a temple holy,
And its incense, drifting slowly,
Bathed you in a mystic splendor—
You, so human and so tender !
Then some word you’d lightly spoken,
And my dreams lay crushed and broken.
It seemed as though there ne’er could be
A balm to heal my misery.
You were stripped of all my fancies,
All my mystic necromancies.
You were then just as I’d found you,
And—l put my arms around you.
QUEENSLAND.
GARRY.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane. Jury 30, 1910.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Invitations are already beginning to pour out
for the carnival week. Among them are the
cards for a Government House reception on Fri-
day (12th). It is located at the Exhibition Hall,
and is to be about the briefest thing of its kind on
record—9.3o to 11p.m.; scarcely worth donning
war-paint for.
Speaker J. T. Bell, though at latest slightly
better, is still very seriously ill; and a host of
friends are sympathising sincerely with Mrs. «T. T.
in her long-continued anxiety.
The balls have started rolling, and for the next
month or so they may be expected to roll two
deep. The tram boys had their annual hop last
week, and the arrangements were so good that
they might well be followed by those who direct
things from the seats of the mighty. The rail-
way men came next with a square yard of invita-
tion ticket, followed by a palm-decked room full
of signal lights, with lovely roses from engineer
Pagan’s garden. Finally, Mrs. South-Brisbane
Mayoress Savage engineered a successful whirl in
the interests of the Brisbane General Hospital,
whereat the decorations were almost as interest-
ing as the get-up of the only male in fancy-dress.
Bob Philp, who turned the handle that opened
the Beaudesert show last week, uttered a remark
that has made people sit up and take notice, as
well as refreshment. Hesaid that he hoped the pre-
sent Government would be allowed to live out its
allotted span, and not be brought to an untimely
end. Now, just lately, nothing has been heard of
any murderous designs upon the Phidston regime,
but this utterance suggests that a canker is gnaw-
ing' at what Percy, in “The Dollar Princess,” calls
the “ victuals.” The said Beaudesert show was a
record for many things—for the flowers thf.t came
from Mrs. W. Collins’s garden, and from those
of the Strettons and Hinchcliffes; for the
l>ad service at t! e luncheon, and for the tinned-
dog vegetables with which the visitors were re-
galed what time the show benches were crammed
with lovely fresh market-garden eatables. And
in the matter of young population, it would be
hard to find a better showing than Beaudesert’s
within the Commonwealth.
To be a successful medical practitioner in the
wilds of this State a man must be a versatile
genius. I know one such in a country town. Keisa
top-notcher at the sawbones business; shoots with
a directness that generally lands him a handsome
winner at the targets, rides a motor cycle at a
pace that defies every known traffic regulation,
scrapes a pretty fiddle in the local orchestra, and
does a little in the champion tennis courts. One
time, during an annual outbreak of frivolity, he
was billed for a place in the overture and in a
subsequent quartette. The orchestra had just
disposed of the overture when an urgent call came
for Sawbones, a man having torn himself to pieces
about five miles out of town. It looked tottery
for the quartette; but the doctor mounted his
motor, dashed off into the night, fixed his shat-
tered patient, and racing back, twiddled the first-
violin part in the quartette as if nothing had hap-
pened to disturb his artistic thoughts.
A downpour of pre-wedding teas has com-
menced. After one or two small flutters, Mamma
Lightollar set the cup a-steaming at the Caffi
Eschenhagen on Friday afternoon with about 150
guests, in honor of the coming Palmer alliance.
The pretty little bride-elect, in a copper-tinted
chiffon cloth and a greeny brown hat, smiled her
happiness on all and sundry.
Many friends are mourning the death of Mrs.
George Warry, whose wedding last year—she
used to be Miss Lizzie Heal —was one of the social
events of the season. The Warrys only returned
from a European honeymoon late last summer.
Theirs was a brief happiness for which they had
waited about ten years, and it ended with almost
tragic suddenness.
A visiting doctor—he hails from Victoria—
calls attention to the incompleteness of a univer-
sity without a medical school. As the Kidston
idea has it at present, the Queensland Uni. will
partake, as far as the medical student is con-
cerned, of the nature of an empty shell. With a
general hospital of 250 beds handy, this seems
like a waste of good opportunity.
The coming show is trying to make and break
another record ; and as for the country cousin’s
difficulty of finding a place whereon to lay his
head, it looks like being a very easy first. There
will be some new points of interest, among them
a court devoted to the Solomon Islands, where
the rubber is going to come from, and where it
used to be quite easy to be eaten to death. The
crowd is always a show to itself ; and then your
own Excellency and his determined helpmate will
be with us once more, and the welcome will be
something to remember, for Queensland loves the
Pale Governor and his practical spouse.
Packed houses nightly honor J.C.W.’s “Dollar”
bill. In spite of cool press notices in the South,
Grace Edmond is toasted as just It here. She
is smiling her way into big popularity, in which
the whole co. is sharing. Florence Young and
Roberts will transplant “The Orchid” to His
Majesty’s for show week, with “The Waltz
Dream ” to follow.
The South Brisbane Council has at last decided
to have a picture show of its own, and the col-
lection of the late R. J. Randall will soon deco-
rate the walls of the “South’s” Technical Hall.
Father George Randall makes the gift, which is
one of real value. The Council’s determination
to house the collection constitutes something of a
record in a way of an Australian city honoring a
local prophet.
The Brisbane city fathers are on fire ; and the
city’s continued prosperity is responsible for the
incendiarism. Provided the ratepayers approve,
they are going to ask for a loan of £lOO,OOO to buy
a decent ferry service and make sundry wharf
and other improvements. Mayor Hetherington,
who will probably get his name upon the long-
contested foundation-stone of the new town hall,
is at any rate bent on really doing something—
even if somebody else has to pay for it.
They still take their hats off to the Chinaman
up north. The very smartest wedding a northern
coastal town has had for ages happened the other
day, when a little Ah Kitten was married to a
young Ah Cat. There isn’t anything wrong in
that, of course; it is far better than a piebald
wedding, anyhow. What made a correspondent
feel so uncomfortable about it was that the
mayor and mayoress, and the police magistrate,
and all local society turned up and beamed at the
ceremony, and made fulsome speeches at the
subsequent breakfast, which, according to the
papers, was “a very sumptuous affair.”
The local Auto. Club had a very jolly run on
Saturday afternoon to Sandgate. Thirty cars
carried out about 84 folk. The dandy of the meet
was a huge machine owned by Mr. and Mrs.
McCarthy, of Melbourne, who are on a tour that
won’t end till almost Yarra bank's Cup time,
according to present arrangements. Dr. and Mrs.
Leigh, also of Melbourne, were of the party.
Yours affectionately, Mario.

“My aunt advised me to try Cuticura

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC) CONTRIBUTORS.
C.R.E. : We only pay for fresh-laid jokes. The first
one you send knows its way to the basket so well that
the others naturally followed it E.S. : It used to be a
better joke than that when it was new C.T.C. : Very
inferior thing and apropos of nothing that is visible to
the naked eye J.L.: The train that was quite safe
because the Commissioners were aboard lacks perspective
and nearly everything else that is desirable in a train
M.N.W. : They are a lamentable collection. Something
more might be said, but the dictionary has given out.
E.N. : Every writer and every artist has rushed us with
ideas about the Richmond accident. It is too much
altogether... Dill T. : Why should we reproduce a small
blotted monstrosity labelled “ The Kid” in the act of
grossly insulting a large stupid outrage labelled “ The
Loafer”?
“ 0.K.” : Following up my assertion that
Victoria, which was quite recently opened-up by
“new' chums” has developed a petty spirit of
antagonism to them, I quote from a last-week’s
debate in the Vic. Assembly :
Mr. McLellan said that lie was against spending the
money of the people to bring out immigrants, who would
enter into competition with Australian workers.
According, then, to this parochial-minded spokes-
man for a Vic. constituency, no immigrant who
comes to Australia to work for his living and
make a home can be an Australian worker. He
can do Australian work, but he can’t be an
“Australian worker.” Heavens and Earth!
What better recommendation can a man have as
a worker than that he came here to work ? And
what better recommendation can he have as an
Australian than that he voluntarily became an
Australian? Many men are Australians because
they can’t help it. They just happened to be
born here.

Two honorable samples of the J.P. class
,‘recently figured in Melbourne courts. Sample I.
‘was fined for gelling butter that had heightened
its complexion with. coal-tar. Sample 11. was
said to own an extempore wife, and was ordered
to maintain the legal one. These gentlemen are
important citizens, and, as J.’sP., are held to
,be above reproach. Which clearly proves that
Reproach is low when these J’s.P. can be above it.
“The Heathen” heaves the Brick of
Scorn.
On a recent wet Sunday I read with interest a magazine
article on revivals and revivalists, wherein the Saviour
was robbed of his “ u ” and spelt “Savior” to save some
of America’s time. There was a good deal in it about
Billy Sunday, the ex-baseball player who is now the lead-
ing revivalist of the U.S., mainly because he preaches
with baseball actions. One of "the illustrations showed
him performing before an immense crowd. His atti-
tude is that of one who has received a knock-
out blow, or has been jerked backward off his feet
by the grapnel of a drifting balloon catching him
under the chin. Of M. B. Williams, a famous traveller
in hysteria, who trained Mr. Sunday, another revivalist
says : “ He conducted revivals in 134 cities and towns in
lowa, and, so far as I know, there has never been a re-
vival in one of them since. Ministers call it ‘the burnt
district.’ He was so sensational that anything after him
was tame and powerless. He did everything that way.
Big chorus, big crowd, his attacks on sin so fierce, his
slang, so picturesque, in nearly every place a personal
fight with fists or horse-whips, and even threatened
gun-play—you can’t follow that with any ordinary
method.” These laudatory remarks, of course,
were made before the recent throat-cutting of
a Yankee weverend by a holy assassin whose pulpit
he had been occupying. That record will probably stand
until some saint scalps another with a sharpened collec-
tion plate and grills him on his own altar. The freak
revivalist has not yet struck Australia; but there are
shoals of him—and her—doing well in America. Two
twin brothers, who shave, dress and talk alike, draw well
because they keep the congregation guessing which is
which. A female horror’s prize stunt is shaking down
her hair during her platform frenzies and putting it up
again in full view of all. A cowboy Bible-puncher relies
on his unshorn hair and exaggerated pants. Another
bustles on to the platform with his drummer’s sample-
case as his trade-mark. The prize freak, though, is the
“Child Evangelist”—a noxious cub of 14, who has been
taught a certain series of declamations which he yelps
and shrieks at the lamentable grown-ups that go to his
meetings. The .whelp’s father “manages” him in the
theatrical sense. Apparently, the Chapman-Alexander
troupe of holy showmen, recently in these parts, are old-
fashioned and conservative in their methods when com-
pared to some of the leaping hysteria-vendors of religious
vaudeville now showing in the U.S.
' ‘Surcingle ” digs up an old story :
Under the title of “The Great Turf Fraud,’’ Dick
Donovan makes a'book of the greatest English horsey
sensation, the case of Benson, Kurr, Bale and Murray.
The most startling feature of the case was in their having
bribed three leading Scotland Yard- detectives—Meikle-
jolin, Druscovich and Palmer. Benson was the chief
rascal. They had printed in Edinburgh a newspaper
called Sport, which began with -No. 1713. -Benson, an
expert journalist, wrote the stuff. The sole purpose was
to exalt the virtues of himself, under the name of Mont-
gomery. This Montgomery had invented an almost
infallible system of turf betting, for which he was boy-
cotted by the .bookmakers. Five hundred copies of Sport
were printed. These were sent round to rich people on
the Continent of Europe, with French translations of the
portions about Montgomery as “ a foe to the harpies of ’
the .turf, who prey upon a deluded public.” Victims
were found.to invest large sums through Montgomery,
and the rogues thus netted about £14,000, including
£lO,OOO from a Madame de Goncourt. They would have
had her for £30,000 more but for her having to realise
securities in order to raise the wind. On this point
she consulted her lawyer, who sniffed the swindle
at once. The lady promptly started for London.
Her first business was to see the “Lor Maire.” The
mayor communicated with the detective office. There-
upon Benson and co. received the wink, and boited.
Druscovich had principal charge of the job of hunting them
down. The complicity of the detectives was ultimately
proved bv clumsily-disguised telegrams which they sent to
the offenders. The latter had changed their French notes
for Bank of England ones, but the numbers of these were
known. Yet it was contrived to swap the notes for
Scotch ones of £lOO each. However, after some of the
Scotch notes had been negotiated, the numbers
of them also were published. Consequently, there
was a drying up of resources. The slipperiness of
the offenders suggested to Abrahams, the prosecuting
solicitor, that there was knavery in the detective
office.; Superintendent Williamson became suspicious,
on this hint, and kept on the qui vive. Light
broke through with the. capture of Murray. Being
hard up, he had tried to cash one of the Scotch £lOO
notes. Then Kurr was arrested. Then Benson and
Bale, who were found at Rotterdam. Benson got 15
years, Kurr and Bale 10 years, and Murray 18 months.
The next swoop was on the unsuspecting detectives—
Meiklejohn, Druscovich and Palmer, all arrested while
quietly at work in the office. With them was arraigned
Froggatt, Murray’s solicitor, the charge being aiding a
conspiracy. Benson and Kurr, haled from prison, gave
evidence against them. They were sentenced to two
years apiece, i
“Home Signal”: There is no need to
mention on which State’s railway system the
following incident took place. Just as a suburban
train was about to start, a determined looking
littlp old woman in black scurried from the book-
ing offibe and scrambled nimbly into the last com-
partment of the last carriage—a second-class
smoker. After giving the five men travellers it
contained permission to continue smoking, she
settled back and gazed tranquilly outof the window
whilst three stations came and went. Then the
collision-caravan ran into a station where a big
goods engine was juggling ponderously with
a string of trucks. The shunting had reached
the stage at which the goods engine required
the down line, on which the passenger train
stood, to run past its string and hook on to
the other end. In order to be ready as soon as
the passenger train moved out, the goods driver
brought his locomotive slowly up to within a few
yards of the guard’s van. As it approached, the
juggernaut gave a preliminary hiss and then
burst into a ferocious roar. On the instant, the
door of the last compartment of the stationary
train flung open, and a small black figure bounded
on to the platform and charged like a distracted
hen’ at the steam-enveloped giant. The stout,
red-faced driver saw her dashing towards him and
flinched in his cab; but she didn’t strike'him.
Bushing at the front of the juggernaut, she
clouted it three times on its immense iron chest
with her umbrella as though she were
beating off an intruding cow. Then, running
jdong to the cab, she shook a thin, black-
gloved fist in the driver’s face and shrieked :
“ Pon’t you dare to run your filthy machine over
jpp, you clumsy fellow ! I don’t travel on your
sjjly railways often; but when Ido I take good
pjire that no fool of a man makes mincemeat of
me. Go along with you. you great booby!”
Without giving the wretched man a chance to
reply, the alert old lady scuttled back to her car-
riage, leaving the stout driver redder in the face
and wilder than he had ever been since entering
! the service of Mr. Fate. It is a humiliating thing
for a driver of many years’ service to have his
engine publicly thrashed by an old lady ; but Mr.
Fate’s railways deserve all the humiliation that
can be heaped on them. If old ladies would take
to beating railway engines with their umbrellas
as a regular thing, it might be the salvation of
Mr. Fate.

In a recent; court tussle on the North
Coast (N.S.W.) the two litigants were a Hindu
and a Syrian. The latter opened the Bible and
kissed it fervently at a page describing the fall
of Syria. And Syria fell sure enough. Which re-
calls that when Josiah Thomas, P.M.G., opened the
Good Book to take the usual oath of Ministerial
office, the yarn is that the passage which met his
eye read, “Josiah... reigned thirty and one
years.” The yarn is probably a lie; but it is a
good lie, anyhow.

The British Law Society has solemnly
passed a vote of censure on Lloyd George (himself
a' lawyer) for having stated that his learned
brethren are consistent opponents of all legal re-
forms which would tend to lower their fees. The
pretentious hypocrisy of wig and gown deceives
even the wearers, seemingly. They actually
believe themselves devoted to the interests of the
public. '• •

22 years but sir I feel so ashamed that I must dis continu
the same since that horid pictre in your last paper of

A Jingle of Hurry.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HEAVENS ! how we have to hurry through
this noisy little life ;
Going hard from nine till six to keep a
kiddie and a wife ;
Catching trains and missing ferries—hanging on
to cabs and trams ;
Dodging motor-cars and lorries with indignant,
hurried “ Damns.”
Buying, selling, cheating, scheming, adding
figures page by page,
Using ev’rv blessed minute until finished by Old
Age;
Seconds, minutes, months and decades pass us
swiftly by, alack !
Pass and fly like laden robbers and we cannot call
them back !
Bending o’er our work we promise, in a faint,
half-hearted way,
“We will snatch a sunny fortnight from the
scramble some fine day ! ”
But we never do break wholly from the grip of
the Machine;
Death alone comes in to free us from the merciless
routine.
Hurry, hurry from the cradle to the deep and
narrow grave;
Learning lessons, manners, dancing—how to work
and how to save ;
Etiquette and regulations—ev’ry sort of formulas,
From the flush of dawn to midnight, help to spend
Youth’s summer day ;
Then the world-cares, fierce, insistent, come in
force to take their toll
(Paid in leisure, peace and sunlight) of the life-
time of a soul;
Days are shrunken, minutes scarcer, there is less
of love and song
When the black time-tables beckon to the young,
reluctant throng ;
From the college to the city—from the counter to
the chair,
Moving ever swiftly onward each to : his allotted
share;
Each to do his work or shirk it—each to his suc-
cess or fall,
Ever do the young men answer to the mighty
System’s call.
Hurry, Brothers! Hurry! Hurry! There is
little time to spare ;
Telephones are ringing wildly—telegrams lie
ev’rywhere ;
If you will insist on wedding, hurry up and tie
the knot;
Marriage is the Happy Ending, but it complicates
Life’s Plot;
If you ever think of dying, try to let the office
know
When the funeral is starting, and no doubt some-
one will go;
One of us will follow, Brother, to the crowded
burial ground,
And wait till the undertaker spreads the wreaths
upon the mound ;
Then providing, Silent Brother, that it is not
after five,
He will once more seek the office, thanking God
he is alive;
Then we’ll gather up your papers and square up
the draughty room
Where you toiled till you departed for the chill
and idle tomb.
Hurry! Hurry / Life is fleeting, and the jobs
keep piling up !
Breakfast! You’ve no time to eat it! Standing,
drain the steaming cup !
Hear the roar of the electrics ! Hear the whistles
and the bells !
Bidding you to Hurry ! Hurry ! What a tale
that racket tells !
Vain is all your foolish sighing for the coaching
days of yore ;
Modern Life flies smoking onward with a rattle
and a roar;
Hear the clatter of the signal and the clashing of
the gate !
Through the hills the mournful wailing of a train
some seconds late
Echoes, warning us to hurry ! ’Tis the mandate of
the Age,
And he cannot disregard it who would earn his
daily wage !
Take the challenge up or leave it—Progress sets
the killing pace ;
Life has quickened from a canter to a smoking,
steaming race.
N.S.W.
HORATIO BREATHLESS.
The Triumph of the Dead.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BEATRICE WARD, an art student of
some years, suffering from misery and a
woeful lack of room, determined to have
a studio.
After a week’s hunting, she found a suitable
room—large, airy and well lit, with windows
facing north and south.
It was not luxurious, but she had plenty of
space. She had made a specialty of heads, and
three parts of her accumulated art treasures con-
sisted of studies of the northern extremity of
mankind. These she arranged right round her
studio above all the other work, and in the winter
nights she would sit there with the fire burning,
its ruddy light playing over the heads, making
them live.
She gave up the boarding-house altogether,
took a tiny bedroom adjoining the studio, and
made her home there. In the day-time
other artists drifted in and out. Some of them
were Bohemians —sorts of parodies on humanity
into which God had breathed the breath of life
and never endowed with principles—and they
brought strange atmospheres with them. At
night, when the big front door downstairs was
closed, Beatrice would lock her door, light her
fire, and sing softly while she worked on black
and white studies.
■* * * *
The Business Man was portly, middle-aged and
comfortable. He had not the slightest interest in
either literature or art, except as aids to the
enjoyment of life. A picture was a useful thing
to relieve the monotony of a wall, but between
the making of pictures and boots there was no
difference, and, anyhow, he always preferred
photographs to pictures —they were more real and
took much less time in the making. A book was a
useful thing to have when one had time to kill and
all the available newspapers had been devoured.
But as for being the expression of the soul
of the maker, well —rats ! There were two books
that summed up the wisdom and pathos of
humanity—the works of Burns and Shalcspeare.
All the rest were vanity and repetition.
He used to stroll in in his quiet way, and bring
with him bags of choice fruit. He hated senti-
ment ; solid comfort was his hobby.
And Beatrice would smile while she munched
the fruit, and lead him to talk of the events of the
work-a-day world —topics on which he was deeply
interesting, for he was a man of the world, philo-
sophical and human.
One afternoon he found her with her hat and
gloves on, ready to go out. His face fell, but
brightened up as she said, gleefully :
“Oh, I’m so glad you’ve come. There’s a re-
ception on at Negri Sindhia’s studio, to see his new
picture. Negri, you must know, is a Eurasian
artist, who just paints for the love of it—he has
private means which make it unnecessary for him
to live by his art. I want you to take me
down there. I’m just dying to see his picture.”
He took the longest way round. It pleased him
to be seen on the block with the beautiful girl
in her dainty costume of book muslin.
It was four o’clock when they arrived at Negri’s
studio, and that polished Asiatic received them
with extravagant expressions of delight. The
rooms—there were three of them—were full of a
well-dressed, brainy crowd of young people of
both sexes, who had exhausted the pictures,
cleaned up the refreshments, and split up into
groups, smoking.
Some one dragged the Business Man off to
introduce him to a Burns crank, and Beatrice
was whisked off in another direction to be intro-
duced to the Pagan, who was just beginning to
make himself something of a name as a writer.
* * * *
The next afternoon found the Pagan at Bea-
trice’s studio. He looked round the long array of
heads, examined them critically, and remarked
reflectively :
“ You’ll be a great portrait-painter some day if
you persevere.”
“ I want to add yours to my procession. Will
you let me?”
“ With pleasure. I say, you seem to have the
gift of preserving the likeness and yet idealising
the person.”
She laughed :
“It is intentional. I found I could retain the
perfect likeness and make the face that of an
angel or demon, just as I chose ; so I have ideal-
ised all those I hang on my wall.”
“I see. You chose your company, even in
pictures.”
“ Yes. Environment, according to Drummond,
is everything.”
“ When shall I sit?” inquired the Pagan.
“Now, if you will. Sit here and talk to me
some more of the surf and the black shingle
beaches. I want you to be quite natural, and
then I will paint you'as you really are.”
He sat for her every day for a week, and as she
painted him she studied him while he reeled off
tale upon tale and reminiscences by the volume.
The man was a born storyteller, and the girl,
analysinghim, discovered that hehad noidea where
fact ended and fiction began. He was a creator
and not a record. His riotous imagination swept
him away, and he suffered from a species of
mental intoxication that made it impossible for
him to give a plain unvarnished account of any-
thing. He would embellish unconsciously, and
brighten the facts with gems of fiction which
sparkled in the memory.
She laid down her brush to listen to his story.
He leaned towards her with set brows and half-
closed eyes, the words coming in a tense whisper.
His haunting blue eyes became more eerie.
Beatrice watched him, hearing no word of his
tale but noting every line of the expressive face.
Then grasping her brushes, she painted feverishly
for half a minute.
The Pagan’s voice died away —the girl sprang
up.
“It’s my masterpiece!” she cried, clapping
her hands.
In a moment he was at her side. The face
seemed to start from the canvas, and he almost
bent his head to hear the words it seemed trying
to utter.
“ Surely that’s not me, Beatrice!” he said in
an awed whisper.
“It is—as you looked when you leaned forward
and whispered.”
He turned to her in admiration.
“Beatrice, let us join forces. Paint pictures
like that and I’ll weave romances around them,
and together we’ll bewilder the world.”
“ If you will always be my model !” she half
whispered, dipping her face into the Chinese
water lilies that trembled over the rim of a majo-
lica vase.
There was silence for half a moment, silence in
which the waving of the jonquil-like Chinese lilies
was distinctly audible, and then Beatrice felt his
arm steal round her shoulders.
“ Alwav and alway, darling—right to the end,
and in the dim beyond if memory and choice sur-
vive.”
She pushed his face away.
“Look at all those people watching you ! ” she
laughed, pointing to the painted heads and faces
of black and white.
They spent the rest of that afternoon up the
river, and then they floated homeward, hand in
hand, through the sunset fire. Dinner at the
Trocadero, and then through the glittering streets
of an enchanted city to hear the Italians in “II
Trovatore.”
The golden summer days sped away in a
species of intoxication. All their friends gave
them up—or said they did ; for they had only
time for one another. And how they worked !
Two novels and a multitude of short stories and
sketches and a whole gallery of studies for the
illustrations. Then there were “jVlodern Fairy
Tales” by the Pagan, illustrated by Beatrice
Ward—dainty tales founded on the story of their
own loves.
But there was a dark shadow in the picture, a
minor note in the gushing music of their happi-
ness. The Pagan’s sight was failing. He con-
sulted oculist after oculist, and just as he stood
on the threshold of fame and in the gates of
success he knew the end had come. Total blind-
ness was within measurable distance.
Day by day he fought the failing sight, hiding
the worst from the girl whose heart he knew it
would break; and week by week his visual power
melted way. At last he could not distinguish
even Beatrice across the street, and his fingers
trembled as they hovered over the typewriter
keys.
All in a week the last glimmer of light died
out. No more splendid sunrises, no more skies
of maize green and flaming stars of evening, no
(Continued on next page.)

more sunset fire seen over the long reaches of the
rarramatta River. Only the music of her voice,
and the honey of her kisses broke the awful, soul-
crusbmg monotony of the everlasting darkness.
Amidst the horror of his blindness stalked the
ghost of the financial problem, with which he
knew Beatrice was struggling. He had embossed
letters placed on the keys of his typewriter, and,
even in the desolation of his darkness, strove to
write tales and impressions of one suddenly
darkeiied for ever. But it was impossible. The
world had gone. It was only a conglomeration of
sounds wild music and haunting refrains. In
the corrosive darkness only the music of her voice
and her kisses remained real.
Taste, touch and sound were all that were left
to him, and the most important of these was the
sense of hearing. Then the insidious, mysterious
affection that had destroyed his sight attacked
his aural nerves. Fear fell upon him. As long
as ho could speak and converse intelligently he felt
he was still a source of some comfort to the girl
he loved. &
I hen sound began to fade, till an ocean of
silence overwhelmed his world, and before him
lay the abomination of black, empty silence.
In his extremity he remembered Negri Sindhia
in response to a letter Negri called to take him to
the Hardens. Was it premonition, or what ? But
Beatrice could hardly bring herself to let them go
without her. He was not gone an hour when she
threw on her hat and jacket and went to the Gar-
dens in search of them.
The loveliness of Sydney’s Gardens grated on
her that afternoon. Suddenly Negri appeared,
staggering like a drunken man, and gripped her
by the arm.
“ A little faintness—they have taken him to
the hospital. He will soon be better ” and he
reeled and fell into a seat.
lell me the worst —what has happened, what
have you done ? ”
Negri shuddered, rocked backwards and for-
wards, and then exclaimed :
“The eyes that longed for light and the ears
that yearned for hearing—in Heaven, Elysium
iNirvana, they shall be satisfied.”
That night it was the Business Man who walked
quietly, without knocking, into Beatrice Ward’s
studio, and found her sitting in a rattan chair, her
hat and gloves on as she had returned from’ the
Gardens and that other place, looking at the
I agan’s picture on the easel before her.
She looked at him like one in a dream. He
drew her arm through his and led her downstairs,
handed her into a cab and drove her away to his
sister’s care.
“ Try and make her cry, Sis; a real, good howl
is the only thing that will save her.”
“He might have lived on for her sake. Men
are horribly selfish,” said his sister two hours
later.
“Bah! You don’t understand. He was real
grit—never thought his sort could have played
the game so well. His insurance policy would
have run out in another fortnight. Now she’ll
get what little he had coming to him.”
“ You are a fool, my brother ! ” said his sister
and turned away to do a “ howl ” herself.
The Business Man went out to think it over
* * * *
The Business Man was patient. He waited
two years, and, after much deliberation, decided
that it was a reasonable period, especially in view
of the fact that the Pagan’s portrait had been
removed from the easel and placed over the fire-
place of her studio.
“ I don’t expect you to love me very wildly
Beatrice. But I’m very fond of you, my dear
girl, and you’re not strong enough to go alone
1 his sort of thing is not good enough ; what you
want now is solid comfort. You can set aside all
scruples. I’ll be proud to have just whatever
love, or respect, or friendship you have for me—
anything, so long as you’ll give me the right to
look after you, and give you the solid comfort you
require. There’s nothing intense or ideal about
me, my girl; just solid comfort. I’ll be as good a
husband to you as I can, and I generally succeed
in most things I try.”
“I’ve been expecting this,” said Beatrice-
‘ b,lt ever y time I have tried to face the subject
my heart has failed, and I have put it off.”
“ I know, I know, my girl— very natural, verv
natural. I don t want to bustle you in any way
my dear. Take a week to consider it. Put senti-
ment out of the matter. You are lonelv, and
this life is not suited to you.”
He stopped and looked up at the Masterpiece.
The blue eyes of the Pagan looked down on him
in that wild intensity, trying to speak. He
rubbed his eyes, and, striding across the room let
the spring-blind up with a bang.
By .Tove, my dear, that’s a wonderful paint-
ing of yours! It seems to be trying to speak, and
yet, there s a dash of the mocker in it. However
to come back to what I was saying, this life is not
suited for you. You’re here alone with these
pictures, and your imagination feeds on ’em till
you’re just a bundle of nerves. My sister is going
up to Leura for a fortnight. Suppose you go up
with her. Shell be delighted with your company,
and then when you come back you can let me
have your answer. Now is that a bargain?”
She agreed—and looked, almost fearfully, at the
Masterpiece to see if it made any sign. But the
light was bad, and the Pagan was only a blur of
paint and brush marks.
The fortnight at Leura did her so much good
that her companion stayed another week, and when
Beatrice returned to town she was a different
woman. She saw the wisdom of accepting the
Business Man. She realised that she was not
strong enough to go alone, and, under the cir-
cumstances, was most fortunate to have such a
suitor. She did not return to her studio home
but stayed with her friend, and when the Busi-
ness Man mentioned the subject she accepted
him.
* * * ,
The Business Man had been obliged to go out
of town, and Beatrice sat in the boudoir, which
was her sanctum sanctorum , with her eyes fixed
on those of the Pagan.
The asbestos fire gleamed tranquilly. Silence
brooded over the place, and Beatrice sat back in
the chair gazing at the portrait of her lost genius.
The red shades on the lights brought back the old
atmosphere, and she felt happier than ever before
during her married life. Down in the drawing-
room someone began to play selections from “II
Trovatore ”on the pianola. She looked round on
her procession of heads, and they seemed to smile
at her. The “Tumult of the Heart” throbbed
out, and there rose before her a vision of that first
day on the Parramatta River—the blaze of sunset
fire, the sky of tender maize green with the great
queen jewel of evening floating in its liquid
depths, and then, afterwards, the Grand Opera.
“ Beatrice! ”
She awoke with a start, for there was reproach
in the voice. Someone had thrown an eider-down
quilt around her and turned out the lights, and
but for the glow of the fire the room had been in
darkness. «Its tranquil refulgence was reflected
by the full-length mirror right on to the face of
the portrait. She felt that the picture had
spoken she was sure she had heard her name
pronounced.
She gazed on the eyes of her lost lover. The
room seemed haunted, the face of the portrait
more terribly in earnest. Every moment she ex-
P e cted to hear the words burst out:
“Why have you been untrue?”
But what need for words? Her own conscience
shrieked the words louder than any human voice
could.
She burst into tears : -
I was so lonely, dear—so lonely. I did not
know what I was doing,” she wailed up to the
P' ctll f e - *' But lam more lonely with him. I
shrink from his touch, and his voice grates so, on
me that I could cry out. But what can I do?
.®, 18 kindness itself. The gentle consideration
with which he treats me is beyond description,
it i could only come to you. I loathe to live with
him, but have no excuse to leave him. He has
been so kind. Can’t you speak to me for once,
dear, and tell me what to do ? ”
She sobbed awhile, with her head in her hands,
6O , su ddenlv arose, lit the gas, and went to
the desk in which she kept his typewriter. It was
since she had touched it, and she had
difficulty in finding the letters. She felt her
fingers guided as by another hand, and slowly,
with spasmodic efforts, wrote :
My dear Robert,—
1 1 an i n u ot ""grateful. I know that I owe you a life-
ong- debt of gratitude for your tender consideration,
your unceasing kindness, and your care and sympathy in
e terrible hour of my loss. But I cannot live with
jou. xt is quite impossible. It is degradation— not
on your part, but mine. lam that dead man’s wife, and
from the very grave he holds me to him. lam a haunted
woman. His presence fills my room. His picture is
replete with life, and every day it accuses me of my infi-
delity. To-night I heard his voice—only a dream, of
course ; but to me it is real. I am going back to
my studio to earn my own living, to be alone
with my picture and the haunting presence of my
dead genius; and you must not try to take me from it.
~u "i" 0 have been so good will not make me unhappier
than I am by trying to baulk me in this. I cannot go
back on this resolve. You are so good that in yotir dear,
practical wav you will immediately say, “ But what about
money t His works are selling well now, and my
royalty will be quite sufficient to maintain me when I
cannot work. Good-bye, dear friend ; forgive me if I
hurt you a little.—Yours gratefully, Beatrice.
What are you going to do about it ? ” asked
Ins sister, when the Business Man showed her the
letter.
Well, it s a case that neither Shakspeare or
Burns provided for, and I don’t rightly know
what to do. She’s a good woman, and it would
have been all right if I had got her before that
mad writer. It might have been all right if she
had never painted that picture of him—it gave
me the creeps, and I always expected to hear the
damn thing speak. That’s what’s kept his memory
green. It was all so tragic, and the poor girl
wasn t strong enough to stand it. I want you to
go down and see her. Be very gentle with her and
tell her I will agree to all she proposes on one
condition, and that is, that she draws on me—as
a loan only if she likes, interest 4 per cent.—when-
ever she is short of money. That’s all.”
And the Business Mari went out puffins furi-
ously at an unlit cigar.
UNA KIDGELL.
The Horror of the Harbor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I DOZED on the Eleven boat—
The common Mosman ferry ;
I say I dozed—but kindly note
That I was weary, very.
The deck-hand wore an evil scowl,
And oily tresses parted ;
He waited by to loose the howl
With which the boat is started..
A new mouth-organ in his hand
Did glitter like a pistol;
He looked the Hispaniola brand
Of pirate out of Bristol.
And then I must have closed my eyes
And started wildly dreaming ;
The night seemed full of angry cries
And horrid, helpless screaming.
A brutal pirate hurried past
A long, limp lady hauling,
Whilst other captives stood aghast
And shuddered at his bawling.
Blindfolded by the rail I lay
That savage bully hating ;
I heard him howling of some “ Bay ”
Whilst my dread sentence waiting.
Then suddenly I heard a crash,
A horrid yell of “ Hurry ! ”
I saw a light—or cutlass—flash,
And heard a dreadful scurry.
Like frightened sheep along a race,
Towards the plank they started.
Where he stood by with leering face.
And oily tresses parted.
Out from the vessel’s side they shrank
At his relentless bidding,
Along that frail and trembling plank—
Some stumbling and some skidding.
I was the last to drown—or land,
He dragged the plank from under,
And words I could not understand
Roared in a voice of thunder.
The long, limp lady he unwound
(“That there stern line” he called her);
The tireless screw in foam went round
As fast inboard he hauled her.
Upon the wharf with sleep half blind
I tried to solve the riddle ;
Thin music giggled down the wind
Like some old pirate’s fiddle.
At last the sound I recognised—
It came from his mouth-organ ;
I said, “ I would not be surprised
To hear he sailed with Morgan ! ”
AVhen next you venture on the seas
Watch for one darkly leering;
His name is Harry Opftherepleask,”'
His game is buccaneering.
I’m now too old to go a trip,
But from my cliffside arbor
I hear him roaring on his ship—
The Horror of the Harbor.
N.S.W.
